Second Argument for Void: Motion of Bodies
Second argument for void from motion of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second kalam argument for the possibility of void, which follows from the impossibility of motion without void.

Core Idea
Motion of bodies proves void must exist.

This lesson examines the second argument for the existence of void, which is based on the observable phenomenon of motion. The argument systematically considers what must happen when a body moves into a space occupied by another body, ruling out each alternative until void is necessary. Although the text cuts off, the logical structure is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow of the reasoning. Note the branching structure.
The passage is incomplete; it ends mid-sentence.
2
Trace the logical options
Identify each possibility that the argument considers for the occupying body when a body moves into its place. List them: cease to exist, persist then stay, persist then move, stay and condense.
Each option is shown to be false, except the conclusion that void exists.
3
Examine why condensation is rejected
Understand the author's objection to condensation: it implies the doctrine of hyle (matter that accepts different sizes), which the author considers false.
This is a key point often misunderstood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لو لم يمكن الخلاء، بل لو لم يوجد لامتنع حركة الجسم من مكان إلى مكان لأنه إذا انتقل إلى مكان الجسم، فالجسم الشاغل لذلك المكان، إما أن ينعدم ويحدث جسم آخر يشغل المكان المنتقل عنه، وهذا باطل باعترافكم بل بشهادة العقل في كثير من المواضع، كحركة عصامير الدولاب كل إلى حيز آخر وإما أن لا ينعدم وحينئذ: فإما أن يستقر في مكانه، أو ينتقل عنه، وإن استقر فإما أن يبقى على مقداره فيلزم تداخل البعدين مادتين، واجتماع الجسمين في حيز واحد وهذا باطل اتفاقاً وضرورة. وإما أن لا يبقى بل يتكاثف أي يصغر مقداره بحيث يحصل للجسم المتحرك حيز يسعه وذلك إما أن يكون الجسم ذا مادة تقبل المقادير المتفاوتة في الصغر والكبر، وذلك قول بالهيولي، وسنقيم الدلالة على بطلانها، أو لكونه
The second [argument]: if the void were not possible, rather if it did not exist, then the motion of a body from place to place would be impossible, because when it moves to the place of a body, then the body occupying that place either ceases to exist and another body comes into existence occupying the place from which it moved, and this is false by your own admission, rather by the testimony of the intellect in many cases, such as the motion of the pegs of a wheel each to another space; or it does not cease to exist, and then: either it settles in its place, or moves away from it. If it settles, then either it remains at its size, necessitating the interpenetration of two dimensions as matter, and the conjunction of two bodies in one space, and this is false by agreement and necessity. Or it does not remain but rather condenses, i.e., its size becomes smaller such that a space accommodating the moving body is obtained, and that either requires that the body possess matter that accepts differing sizes in smallness and largeness, which is the doctrine of hyle (hayula), and we will establish proof of its falsehood, or that it [is...]
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the possibility of void. The argument claims that if void were impossible, then motion of a body from one place to another would be impossible. The reasoning is that when a body moves to a place occupied by another body, the occupying body must either cease to exist and create a new body in the vacated place (which the author says the opponents admit is false in many cases like wheel pegs), or it does not cease to exist. If it does not cease, it either stays in its place (then we would have two bodies in one space, which is absurd), or it moves away. If it stays and condenses (becomes smaller) to make room, that would require that the body is composed of matter that can accept different sizes (the doctrine of hyle), which the author says is false and will be proven so. The argument is cut off at the mention of 'or that it...' The implication is that since these options are all false, void must exist. However, the author does not complete the response here; he presumably will refute the argument later.

Key Terms
حركة
تداخل البعدين
تكاثف
الهيولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that condensation of the occupying body is a valid way to avoid void, as it makes room without requiring empty space.
Correction: The author rejects condensation because it implies the doctrine of hyle – that bodies are composed of matter that can accept different sizes. This doctrine is considered false in kalam, so condensation is not a viable solution.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what is one of the consequences if a moving body's place is occupied by another body that does not vacate and does not condense?
A) The moving body stops.
B) Two bodies interpenetrate (تداخل البعدين).
C) The first body breaks into pieces.
D) The void is created.

Key Takeaway
The second argument for void uses the observed fact of motion to force the conclusion that void must exist, by ruling out all alternatives such as interpenetration or condensation (which implies hyle).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you move from one room to another, do you ever stop to think that your very movement testifies to the existence of something unseen—the void? How does this awareness deepen your consciousness of the signs of Allah in the most ordinary acts?
?
The author rejects the doctrine of hyle because it leads to absurdities. Are there ideas or beliefs you hold that seem plausible but, when followed to their logical conclusions, lead to contradictions? Will you have the courage to reexamine them today?
?
The argument forces you to accept void because the alternative is to deny motion, which is absurd. In your faith, are there truths you must accept because rejecting them leads to greater absurdities? What unseen realities of the unseen world are you called to affirm right now?

Refutation of Elevation Implying Vacuum
The author refutes the claim that elevation necessarily implies vacuum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that elevation necessarily implies a vacuum is refuted by distinguishing between instantaneous and temporal interpretations of motion.

Core Idea
Elevation is a motion, and motion requires time, allowing air to fill gaps.

In this passage, the author engages with an opponent who argues that elevation (irtifāʿ) automatically creates a vacuum (khalāʼ). By carefully analyzing what 'instantaneous' might mean, the author shows that either elevation is impossible in an indivisible instant, or if interpreted as simultaneous movement of parts, it still occurs over time and air can flow in, negating the need for a vacuum. This lesson will walk through the logical steps of this refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: the author grants a premise and then examines two possibilities.
2
Identify the two interpretations of 'instantaneous'
The passage presents two possible meanings of 'instantaneous' (دفعة). Find each one and understand how the author refutes each.
The first interpretation is about an indivisible moment; the second is about simultaneous movement of parts.
3
Understand the logical entailment
See how the author concludes that the opponent must either deny the implication itself or deny the possibility of the implication. Then note the additional argument about non-contact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جاز أن يستلزم محالاً ، ولو سلم إمكان الارتفاع في الجملة . فإن أريد بكونه دفعة كونه في آن لا ينقسم أصلاً ، فلا نسلم إمكانه. كيف والارتفاع حركة تقتضي زماناً، وإن أريد كون حركة الأجزاء معاً، لئلا يلزم التفكك، فلا نسلم استلزامه للخلاء، فإنه حركة لها زمان، يجوز أن يمر الهواء من الأطراف إلى الوسط، في ذلك الزمان، ففي الجملة الخصم بين منع اللزوم، ومنع إمكان اللزوم ، ولا يتم المطلوب إلا بثبوتها .

نعم : لو جعل اللزوم هو اللاوصول أعني لا مماسة السطحين الحاصلة عند الارتفاع ، الزاماً لمن يقول بكون اللاوصول آنياً يتعين منع إمكان الملزوم.
It is possible that it [the implication] implies an absurdity. Even if we grant the possibility of elevation in general, if by 'instantaneous' is meant that it occurs in an indivisible moment, we do not grant its possibility. How [can it be so] when elevation is a movement that requires time? And if by 'instantaneous' is meant the movement of parts together, lest disintegration follow, we do not grant that it necessitates vacuum, because it is a movement with time, during which air may pass from the edges to the middle in that time. So in summary, the opponent is between denying the implication and denying the possibility of the implication. The desired conclusion is not established except by proving them [the premises]. Yes: if the implication is taken as non-contact, i.e., the lack of contact between the surfaces that occurs upon elevation, as a necessary consequence for the one who claims that non-contact is instantaneous, then it is determined to deny the possibility of the implied thing.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer within a larger argument about whether a body's elevation (irtifāʿ) necessarily implies a vacuum (khalāʼ). The author begins by stating that even if one grants the possibility of elevation in general, there are two interpretations: first, if instantaneous means occurring in an indivisible moment, we deny its possibility because elevation is a motion (ḥaraka) and motion necessarily requires time (zamān). Second, if instantaneous means the simultaneous movement of all parts to avoid disintegration (tafakkuk), we deny that this necessitates a vacuum because it is a movement over time during which air can flow into the gap. Thus the opponent must either deny the logical implication (luzūm) itself or deny the possibility of the implication. The argument concludes that the opponent's claim is not established unless these points are proven. The author then adds a note: if the implication is defined as 'non-contact' (lā wuṣūl), i.e., the lack of surface contact that results from elevation, this can be used to compel someone who asserts that non-contact is instantaneous, by denying the possibility of the implied event (the elevation itself). The passage shows careful handling of temporal and spatial conditions in classical kalām physics.

Key Terms
ارتفاع
خلاء
حركة
زمان
دفعة
لاوصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Elevation can be instantaneous, creating a vacuum instantaneously.
Correction: All motion requires time; elevation is a motion, so it takes time, and during that time air can flow in, preventing a vacuum.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main reason for denying that elevation implies vacuum?
A) Elevation is impossible altogether.
B) Elevation is a motion over time, so air can fill the gap.
C) Elevation occurs instantaneously, so vacuum is unavoidable.
D) The opponent's definition of elevation is incoherent.

Key Takeaway
Elevation, as a motion, requires time, and this temporality allows air to fill any gap, undermining the claim that it necessitates a vacuum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems to have an inevitable negative outcome, do you examine whether that outcome is truly necessary or if there is an alternative you haven't considered? This passage shows that even a seemingly necessary implication can be denied. In what area of your life are you resigning to a negative outcome because you believe it is unavoidable? What step can you take today to explore an alternative?
?
The author carefully distinguishes two meanings of 'instantaneous' to expose a flaw in the opponent's argument. When you hear someone's statement, do you often assume one meaning without considering other possible interpretations? Think of a recent misunderstanding you had—could it have been avoided by clarifying the meaning of a key term? How will you change your listening habits starting today?
?
The argument here depends on the nature of time and motion. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes expect instant change in your heart, forgetting that growth requires time and patience? What 'gap' in your faith are you impatient about, and how can you allow Allah to fill it gradually through consistent effort and trust?

The Impossibility of Interpenetration
Author rejects possibility of body cessation and shows interpenetration absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first part of the kalam argument that if vacuum does not exist, then motion would require two bodies to occupy the same space, which is absurd.

Core Idea
Two bodies cannot share one space without vacuum.

This passage begins a rigorous proof by the author. He assumes that vacuum is impossible and then shows that if a body moves to another's place, the occupying body must either cease to exist or persist. If it persists and stays in its place without changing size, the moving body would have to share the same space—an impossibility. We will examine this first branch and the example of wheel pegs.

Guided Path
1
Read the assumption and first alternative.
Read the first few lines in Arabic and English. Note the assumption: vacuum does not exist. Then the first possibility: the occupying body ceases to exist.
The author rejects this with the example of wheel pegs.
2
Understand the example of the wheel pegs.
Consider the motion of pegs on a rotating wheel. Each peg moves to the place of another. It is not reasonable that each peg ceases to exist and is replaced. This shows that the first alternative is false.
3
Examine the second alternative: the body persists and stays in place.
If the occupying body stays in its place and remains the same size, then the moving body would need to occupy the same space. This leads to interpenetration of two bodies, which is absurd.
The author states this is false by consensus and necessity.
4
Recognize the importance of this branch.
This branch already shows that without vacuum, motion leads to an impossible consequence. The argument continues to other branches in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو لم يمكن الخلاء، بل لو لم يوجد لامتنع حركة الجسم من مكان إلى مكان لأنه إذا انتقل إلى مكان الجسم ، فالجسم الشاغل لذلك المكان، إما أن ينعدم ويحدث جسم آخر يشغل المكان المنتقل عنه، وهذا باطل باعترافكم بل بشهادة العقل في كثير من المواضع، كحركة عصامير الدولاب كل إلى حيز آخر، وإما أن لا ينعدم، وحينئذ: فإما أن يستقر في مكانه، أو ينتقل عنه، وإن استقر فإما أن يبقى على مقداره فيلزم تداخل البعدين مادتين، واجتماع الجسمين في حيز واحد وهذا باطل اتفاقاً وضرورة. وإما أن لا يبقى بل يتكاثف أي يصغر مقداره بحيث يحصل للجسم المتحرك حيز يسعه وذلك إما أن يكون الجسم ذا مادة تقبل المقادير المتفاوتة في الصغر والكبر، وذلك قول بالهيولي، وسنقيم الدلالة على بطلانها، أو لكونه مملوءا بجسم ذي أجزاء بينهما فرج خلاء قد تقاربت تلك الأجزاء بحيث حصل خلاء، يسع الجسم المتحرك، فيلزم الخلف، لتحقق الخلاء على تقدير عدمه. هذا إن استقر الجسم الشاغل للمكان الثاني في مكانه، وإن انتقل عنه فإما إلى المكان الأول فيلزم الدور لتوقف انتقال كل إلى مكان الآخر، على انتقال الآخر عن مكانه لامتناع الاجتماع، وتوقف انتقاله عنه على انتقال الأول إليه، لئلا يلزم خلوه، وإما إلى مكان آخر فيلزم تصادم الأجسام بأسرها، وتعاقب الحركات لا إلى نهاية، ويؤول إلى الدور ضرورة تناهي الأجسام، وبعض هذه الترديدات تجري في المكان الذي ينتقل عنه الجسم، فإنه إما أن يبقى خالياً، أو يصيـر آخر إليه، أو يتخلخل ما حوله من الأجسام بطريق ثبوت الهيولي أو فُرج الخلاء، فتعين أن يكون المكان الذي ينتقل إليه الجسم، إما محضاً وإما مملوءاً بجسم فيه فرج خلاء يقل وتتقارب الأجزاء فيحصل للجسم المنتقل إليه مكان وتكون حركة السمكة في البحر من هذا القبيل .
Second: If vacuum cannot be, rather if it did not exist, then the motion of a body from one place to another would be impossible. Because when it moves to the place of another body, the body occupying that place either ceases to exist and another body comes to occupy the vacated place—this is false by your own admission, indeed by the testimony of reason in many cases, such as the motion of the pegs of a wheel (ʿaṣāmīr al-dawlāb) each to another location—or it does not cease to exist. Then either it settles in its place or moves away. If it settles, then either it remains at its size, leading to interpenetration (tadākhul) of two dimensions (buʿdayn) as two materials, and the combination of two bodies in one space—this is false by consensus and necessity. Or it does not remain but becomes denser (yatakāthaf), i.e., its size decreases such that there is space for the moving body. This densification is either because the body has a matter that accepts different sizes—this is the doctrine of hyle (hayūlā), and we will demonstrate its falsity—or because it is filled with a body having parts between which there are gaps (furuj) of vacuum; these parts come close together so that a vacuum results sufficient for the moving body, leading to a contradiction (khalaf), because vacuum would be realized on the assumption of its non-existence. This is if the body occupying the second place settles in its place. If it moves away, then either to the first place, leading to circularity (dawr), because the movement of each to the other's place depends on the other moving from its place (due to the impossibility of co-location), and its moving from its place depends on the first moving to it (lest the place become empty); or it moves to another place, leading to collision of all bodies, and sequence of motions to infinity, leading to circularity necessarily due to the finitude of bodies. Some of these branchings apply also to the place from which the body moves: it either remains empty, or another body comes to it, or the surrounding bodies become rarefied (takhalkhul) by way of hyle or gaps of vacuum. Thus it follows that the place to which the body moves must either be pure (khalāʼ) or filled with a body having gaps of vacuum that become smaller and parts come together so that space is made for the moving body. The movement of a fish in the sea is of this kind.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalīl) by reduction to absurdity that motion requires the existence of vacuum. The author, al-Taftazānī, presents a detailed case analysis. He begins by assuming that vacuum is absolutely impossible (lā yumkin al-khalāʼ). Then he shows that if a body moves from one place to another, the body that originally occupied the destination must either cease to exist (which is rejected as contrary to reason, e.g., pegs of a wheel) or persist. If it persists, it either stays in place or moves. If it stays, it either remains the same size (leading to interpenetration of two bodies in one space, which is absurd) or becomes denser. If it becomes denser, this is either by accepting a smaller size (hyle, which will be refuted later) or by having internal gaps of vacuum that close up—which would imply the existence of vacuum (contradiction). If the occupying body moves, it either goes back to the original place (leading to circular dependence) or to another place (leading to infinite regress and eventual circularity). The same reasoning applies to the vacated place. The conclusion is that the destination must either be pure vacuum or a body with vacuum gaps. This shows that vacuum must exist. The example of a fish moving through water illustrates the concept of gaps of vacuum (furuj al-khalāʼ) allowing motion. The argument is exhaustive and uses the principle of excluded middle.

Key Terms
خلاء
حركة
تداخل البعدين
عصامير الدولاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body can simply cease to exist when another moves into its place, allowing motion without vacuum.
Correction: The author shows this is false, as in the case of wheel pegs: each peg moves but does not vanish.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the occupying body stays in its place and does not change size?
A) The moving body cannot move.
B) Two bodies would occupy the same space.
C) The moving body would be created from nothing.
D) A vacuum would appear.

Key Takeaway
The first part of the proof shows that if a body remains in its place without changing size, motion would require two bodies in one space, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author uses physical impossibility to argue against vacuum. In your own life, have you ever held onto a belief that, if consistently applied, would lead to an absurd consequence? What would it cost you to abandon that belief and accept the truth?
?
If it is impossible for two bodies to occupy the same space, then no two contradictory truths can coexist in your heart about Allah. Which truth about Allah are you currently trying to 'squeeze' into a heart already filled with doubt or negligence? What is one step you can take today to remove that contradiction?
?
The proof forces you to choose: either vacuum is possible or motion is impossible. But you know motion is real. Does your intellect submit to the logical conclusion that vacuum must exist, even if your senses do not perceive it? Where else in your faith are you tempted to deny what reason demands because it feels uncomfortable?

The Necessity of Vacuum for Motion
Author completes the proof by examining densification and movement away, concluding vacuum exists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the remaining branches of the kalam proof that motion requires vacuum, including densification and movement away, leading to the conclusion that vacuum must exist.

Core Idea
All alternatives to vacuum lead to contradictions; thus vacuum is necessary.

Having ruled out interpenetration, the author now explores the other possibilities: the occupying body becomes smaller (densification) or moves away. Each path leads to a contradiction unless vacuum is assumed. This lesson completes the proof.

Guided Path
1
Review the densification alternative.
Read the passage about the occupying body becoming denser. Understand the two explanations: hyle or vacuum gaps.
The author will later refute hyle, so the only remaining explanation is vacuum gaps.
2
Analyze the contradiction in the densification via vacuum gaps.
If the body becomes denser by closing internal vacuum gaps, then vacuum must exist to allow that contraction. But we assumed vacuum impossible, leading to a contradiction.
3
Consider the case where the occupying body moves away.
If the occupying body moves to another place, it leads to circularity or infinite regress, which are impossible.
The author also applies the same reasoning to the vacated place.
4
Reach the conclusion.
The only viable option is that the destination is either pure vacuum or a body with vacuum gaps. This proves vacuum must exist.
The fish in the sea example illustrates this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو لم يمكن الخلاء، بل لو لم يوجد لامتنع حركة الجسم من مكان إلى مكان لأنه إذا انتقل إلى مكان الجسم ، فالجسم الشاغل لذلك المكان، إما أن ينعدم ويحدث جسم آخر يشغل المكان المنتقل عنه، وهذا باطل باعترافكم بل بشهادة العقل في كثير من المواضع، كحركة عصامير الدولاب كل إلى حيز آخر، وإما أن لا ينعدم، وحينئذ: فإما أن يستقر في مكانه، أو ينتقل عنه، وإن استقر فإما أن يبقى على مقداره فيلزم تداخل البعدين مادتين، واجتماع الجسمين في حيز واحد وهذا باطل اتفاقاً وضرورة. وإما أن لا يبقى بل يتكاثف أي يصغر مقداره بحيث يحصل للجسم المتحرك حيز يسعه وذلك إما أن يكون الجسم ذا مادة تقبل المقادير المتفاوتة في الصغر والكبر، وذلك قول بالهيولي، وسنقيم الدلالة على بطلانها، أو لكونه مملوءا بجسم ذي أجزاء بينهما فرج خلاء قد تقاربت تلك الأجزاء بحيث حصل خلاء، يسع الجسم المتحرك، فيلزم الخلف، لتحقق الخلاء على تقدير عدمه. هذا إن استقر الجسم الشاغل للمكان الثاني في مكانه، وإن انتقل عنه فإما إلى المكان الأول فيلزم الدور لتوقف انتقال كل إلى مكان الآخر، على انتقال الآخر عن مكانه لامتناع الاجتماع، وتوقف انتقاله عنه على انتقال الأول إليه، لئلا يلزم خلوه، وإما إلى مكان آخر فيلزم تصادم الأجسام بأسرها، وتعاقب الحركات لا إلى نهاية، ويؤول إلى الدور ضرورة تناهي الأجسام، وبعض هذه الترديدات تجري في المكان الذي ينتقل عنه الجسم، فإنه إما أن يبقى خالياً، أو يصيـر آخر إليه، أو يتخلخل ما حوله من الأجسام بطريق ثبوت الهيولي أو فُرج الخلاء، فتعين أن يكون المكان الذي ينتقل إليه الجسم، إما محضاً وإما مملوءاً بجسم فيه فرج خلاء يقل وتتقارب الأجزاء فيحصل للجسم المنتقل إليه مكان وتكون حركة السمكة في البحر من هذا القبيل .
Second: If vacuum cannot be, rather if it did not exist, then the motion of a body from one place to another would be impossible. Because when it moves to the place of another body, the body occupying that place either ceases to exist and another body comes to occupy the vacated place—this is false by your own admission, indeed by the testimony of reason in many cases, such as the motion of the pegs of a wheel (ʿaṣāmīr al-dawlāb) each to another location—or it does not cease to exist. Then either it settles in its place or moves away. If it settles, then either it remains at its size, leading to interpenetration (tadākhul) of two dimensions (buʿdayn) as two materials, and the combination of two bodies in one space—this is false by consensus and necessity. Or it does not remain but becomes denser (yatakāthaf), i.e., its size decreases such that there is space for the moving body. This densification is either because the body has a matter that accepts different sizes—this is the doctrine of hyle (hayūlā), and we will demonstrate its falsity—or because it is filled with a body having parts between which there are gaps (furuj) of vacuum; these parts come close together so that a vacuum results sufficient for the moving body, leading to a contradiction (khalaf), because vacuum would be realized on the assumption of its non-existence. This is if the body occupying the second place settles in its place. If it moves away, then either to the first place, leading to circularity (dawr), because the movement of each to the other's place depends on the other moving from its place (due to the impossibility of co-location), and its moving from its place depends on the first moving to it (lest the place become empty); or it moves to another place, leading to collision of all bodies, and sequence of motions to infinity, leading to circularity necessarily due to the finitude of bodies. Some of these branchings apply also to the place from which the body moves: it either remains empty, or another body comes to it, or the surrounding bodies become rarefied (takhalkhul) by way of hyle or gaps of vacuum. Thus it follows that the place to which the body moves must either be pure (khalāʼ) or filled with a body having gaps of vacuum that become smaller and parts come together so that space is made for the moving body. The movement of a fish in the sea is of this kind.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalīl) by reduction to absurdity that motion requires the existence of vacuum. The author, al-Taftazānī, presents a detailed case analysis. He begins by assuming that vacuum is absolutely impossible (lā yumkin al-khalāʼ). Then he shows that if a body moves from one place to another, the body that originally occupied the destination must either cease to exist (which is rejected as contrary to reason, e.g., pegs of a wheel) or persist. If it persists, it either stays in place or moves. If it stays, it either remains the same size (leading to interpenetration of two bodies in one space, which is absurd) or becomes denser. If it becomes denser, this is either by accepting a smaller size (hyle, which will be refuted later) or by having internal gaps of vacuum that close up—which would imply the existence of vacuum (contradiction). If the occupying body moves, it either goes back to the original place (leading to circular dependence) or to another place (leading to infinite regress and eventual circularity). The same reasoning applies to the vacated place. The conclusion is that the destination must either be pure vacuum or a body with vacuum gaps. This shows that vacuum must exist. The example of a fish moving through water illustrates the concept of gaps of vacuum (furuj al-khalāʼ) allowing motion. The argument is exhaustive and uses the principle of excluded middle.

Key Terms
خلاء
حركة
هيولي
دور
تصادم الأجسام
فرج خلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body can simply become denser without requiring any vacuum, perhaps by compression of internal parts without gaps.
Correction: The author shows that if there are no vacuum gaps, compression would require a different explanation (hyle), which is false. Thus, only vacuum gaps explain densification, but that implies vacuum exists.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of the occupying body moving to another place (not the original)?
A) Circularity.
B) Infinite regress of motions.
C) Immediate creation of vacuum.
D) The body cannot move.

Key Takeaway
The complete argument shows that without vacuum, motion leads to contradictions; therefore vacuum must exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every time you move a limb, the existence of vacuum makes that motion possible—something you never see. How does this truth about the physical world deepen your conviction in the unseen realities of faith, such as angels and the afterlife? Do you live with the same certainty in the unseen as you have in the existence of vacuum?
?
The author's proof shows that without vacuum, motion would be impossible, leading to contradictions. In your spiritual life, are there beliefs or actions that seem contradictory? Are you willing to accept the necessary conclusions of Islamic teachings, even when they challenge your comfort or convenience?
?
If the denial of vacuum leads to absurdity, then consider: what absurdities might arise if you deny the necessity of turning to Allah in every action? Can you point to a specific area of your life where you are 'denying vacuum'—trying to move without relying on His sustaining power?

Objection and Answer on Hyle and Simultaneous Motion
Objection and answer on the refutation of hyle and the possibility of simultaneous motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the proof against the charge that it relies on an incomplete refutation of hyle, and how he allows for the possibility of simultaneous mutual dependence in motion.

Core Idea
Mutual dependence does not imply impossibility if it is simultaneous.

This passage presents an objection to the earlier proof that motion requires vacuum. The objector argues that the proof is flawed because the refutation of hyle is incomplete. The author responds by clarifying the logical structure of the argument and then addresses a deeper objection about the possibility of two bodies moving into each other's places simultaneously. The key concept introduced is that of simultaneous mutual dependence (maʿiyya), exemplified by the pegs of a wheel.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation to get the overall flow of objection and answer.
Notice the structure: two objections and two responses.
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection claims the proof is incomplete because the refutation of hyle is not yet established. The second objection argues that mutual dependence in motion (like two bodies moving to each other's places) is impossible.
3
Understand the author's two responses
For the first objection, the author says the proof does not require the hyle refutation to be complete; it only shows that the alternative leads to hyle, which is problematic. For the second objection, he introduces the concept of simultaneity (maʿiyya) instead of precedence, using the analogy of wheel pegs to show that simultaneous mutual dependence is possible.
The wheel peg example illustrates how motion can be coordinated without vacuum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا ترد نقضا على ما ذكرنا من الدليل وأجيب بأن دليل إبطال الهيولي لا يتم لما سيأتي ، بل غاية الأمر القدح في مقدمات إثباتها، وهو لا يفيد في معرض الاستدلال، ولو سلم فإن أريد بتوقف انتقال كل من الجسمين إلى مكان الآخر على انتقال الآخر إلى مكانه امتناع كل منهما بدون الآخر كما في المتضايفين، فلا نسلم استحالته، لجواز أن لا يكون بصفة التقدم، بل المعية كما في عصامير الدولاب فإن انتقال كل منها إلى حيز السابق يتوقف على انتقال اللاحق إلى حيز لئلا يلزم الخلاء بل التفلك يتوقف على انتقاله إلى حيز وانتقال اللاحق إلى حيز السابق
So it is not an objection (naqḍ) to the proof we mentioned, and it is answered that the proof for refuting hyle (hayūlā) is not complete for reasons to come; rather at most it is a criticism of the premises of its affirmation, which is not useful in the context of inference. Even if this is granted, if by 'dependence of the movement of each of the two bodies to the other's place on the other moving to its place' is meant that each is impossible without the other, like correlatives (mutadāyifayn), we do not grant its impossibility, because it is permissible that it is not characterized by precedence but rather simultaneity (al-maʿiyya), as in the pegs of a wheel (ʿaṣāmīr al-dawlāb). For the movement of each to the space of the preceding one depends on the movement of the following one to its space to avoid vacuum, but rather the revolution (al-taflak?) depends on its movement to a space and the movement of the following one to the space of the preceding one.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is an objection (iʿtirāḍ) and its answer (jawāb). The objector argues that the earlier proof that motion requires vacuum is flawed because the argument against hyle (hayūlā) is not yet established. The author responds that the refutation of hyle is not necessary for the proof; even if the hyle argument is incomplete, the proof still stands because it only points out that the alternative leads to hyle, which is itself problematic. Then the objector raises a second point: in the case where two bodies move to each other's places (as in the example of the wheel pegs), the movement of each depends on the other moving first, which seems impossible. The author answers that this dependence is not one of precedence but of simultaneity (maʿiyya), like correlatives (mutadāyifayn) which are simultaneous by definition. The example of the pegs of a wheel illustrates that each peg moves to the space of the preceding one simultaneously in a coordinated manner, such that no vacuum occurs. Thus the apparent circular dependency is actually a simultaneous mutual dependency, which is logically permissible. The final part seems to explain that the revolution (taflak?) depends on this simultaneous motion.

Key Terms
نقض
هيولي
متضايفان
معية
عصامير الدولاب
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mutual dependence in motion always implies impossibility because it leads to circularity.
Correction: The author shows that if the dependence is simultaneous (like correlatives), it is logically permissible. The wheel peg analogy demonstrates how simultaneous mutual motion can occur without contradiction.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that the movement of two bodies to each other's places is impossible due to mutual dependence?
A) He denies that such movement occurs.
B) He argues that such dependence is possible if it is simultaneous.
C) He claims that the hyle argument resolves the issue.
D) He says the objector has misunderstood the proof.

Key Takeaway
The author shows that simultaneous mutual dependence is logically permissible, using the example of correlatives and wheel pegs to refute the objection that such motion is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Have you ever rejected a truth because you assumed it involved an impossible circular dependency, only to later realize that the relationship was simultaneous rather than sequential? The wheel pegs example shows that mutual dependence can be harmonious when it is simultaneous. What one step can you take today to foster that simultaneity?

The passage distinguishes between sequential dependence and simultaneous dependence. The Third Proof for Vacuum and Its Refutation
Third proof for vacuum and its refutation based on infinite surfaces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for the existence of vacuum and how it is refuted by denying the entailment and clarifying the definition of vacuum.

Core Idea
Vacuum is defined as a body not touching another; the proof's infinite chain is blocked by a final surface.

This lesson presents the third proof for vacuum, which argues that without vacuum surfaces would touch infinitely. The refutation denies this entailment, stating that bodies end at a surface beyond which there is nothing, and that pure non-existence is not a void.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the argument. Then read the translation.
Notice how the proof is presented as a conditional statement.
2
Identify the proof and its refutation
Identify the proof's premise (no vacuum → infinite contact) and the refutation's denial of entailment.
The refutation also redefines what is meant by vacuum.
3
Reflect on the key terms
Consider the terms 'surface', 'contact', 'finitude of bodies', and 'pure non-existence' as used in the argument.
These terms are central to understanding the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه لو لم يوجد الخلاء لكان كل سطح ملاقياً لسطح آخر لا إلى نهاية، لأن معنى تحقق الخلاء، كون الجسم بحيث لا يماسه جسم آخر، واللازم باطل. لما سيجيء من تناهي الأجسام، وأجيب بمنع اللزوم، بل تنتهي الأجسام إلى سطح، لا يكون فوقه شيء، والعدم الصرف ليس فراغاً يمكن أن يشغله شاغل على ما هو المراد بالخلاء المتنازع فيه.
Third: If vacuum did not exist, then every surface would be in contact with another surface ad infinitum, because the meaning of the existence of vacuum is that a body is such that no other body touches it, and the consequent is false—due to what will come regarding the finitude of bodies. And it was answered by denying the entailment; rather bodies end at a surface above which there is nothing, and pure non-existence is not a void that can be occupied by an occupier, according to what is meant by the disputed vacuum.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the existence of vacuum and its refutation. The proof argues that if there were no vacuum (i.e., if every place were filled with body), then every surface would necessarily be in contact with another surface, leading to an infinite chain—which is impossible given the finitude of bodies. The refutation denies the entailment: bodies terminate at a surface beyond which there is nothing, and that nothingness is not a void capable of being filled, which is what is meant by vacuum. Thus the argument fails.

Key Terms
خلاء
سطح
ملاقاة
تناهي الأجسام
عدم صرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vacuum is simply empty space that could be filled.
Correction: In this argument, vacuum is defined as a body not touching another body; pure non-existence is not considered a void.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the third proof, what do bodies ultimately end at?
A) Another body
B) A surface with nothing above it
C) An infinite chain
D) Pure vacuum

Key Takeaway
The third proof for vacuum is refuted by denying the necessary link between absence of vacuum and infinite surface contact, as bodies terminate at a final surface and pure non-existence is not a void.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This passage distinguishes between vacuum (a body not touching another) and pure non-existence. Are there areas in your heart where you fear 'nothingness'—like loneliness or purposelessness—and try to fill them with distractions? How can trusting in Allah as the only true reality shift your perspective on those fears?

The Fourth Proof for Vacuum and Its Refutation
Lists four observational arguments for vacuum and refutes them with alternative explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four observational arguments used to claim vacuum exists and how classical theologians refuted each with alternative physical explanations.

Core Idea
Observations that seem to prove vacuum can be explained by rarefaction and condensation of air.

This passage presents four everyday observations that proponents of vacuum use as evidence. Yet, the author demonstrates that each can be explained without assuming a vacuum, appealing to principles like rarefaction, condensation, and the porosity of materials. Let's examine the arguments and their refutations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the vacuum controversy
Recognize that the debate is about whether empty space (vacuum) can exist. The proof claims to show it does.
This lesson examines the fourth proof.
2
Read the four proofs
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify the four observational arguments: sucked flask, pressed wineskin, inflated sealed wineskin, and wine in jar.
Each proof is introduced with 'منها'.
3
Analyze the refutation for each
The refutation uses rarefaction, condensation, air penetration, and evaporation to explain each observation without vacuum.
The refutation is general, not point-by-point, but covers all examples.
4
Recognize the logical pattern
The refutation shows that what appears to be vacuum can be explained by alternative physical processes that are plausible within natural philosophy.
This safeguards the principle that vacuum is impossible.
5
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this approach avoids assuming an extraordinary cause (vacuum) when ordinary explanations suffice.
This reflects a methodological commitment in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: إنا نشاهد أموراً تدل على تحقق الخلاء قطعاً. منها: أن القارورة إذا مصت جداً، بحيث خرج ما فيها من الهواء، ثم كبت على الماء تصاعد إليها الماء، ولو لم تصر خالية، بل كان فيها ملء لما دخلها الماء كما قبل المص، ومنها أن الزق إذا ألصق أحد جانبيه عن الآخر بالآخر بحيث لا يبقى بينهما هواء، وسد رأسه وجميع مسامه بالقار، بحيث لا يدخل الهواء من خارج، فإذا رفعنا أحد جانبيه عن الآخر حصل فيه الخلاء، ومنها أن الزق إذا بولغ في تمديده وتسديد مسامه، ثم نفخ فيه بقدر الإمكان فإذا غرز فيه مسلة بل مسلات فإنها تدخله بسهولة، فلو لم يكن فيه خلاء لما دخلت لامتناع التداخل، ومنها أن ملء الدن من الشراب إذا جعل في زق، ثم جعلا في ذلك الدن فإنه يسعهما، ولو لم يكن في الشراب فرج خلاء بقدر الزق لما أمكن ذلك. وأجيب: بأن شيئاً مما ذكر لا يستلزم الخلاء، لجواز أن يتخلخل قليل هواء يبقى في القارورة، ثم يعود إلى مقداره الطبيعي عند ترك المص فيتصاعد الماء ضرورة امتناع الخلاء، وكذا يجوز أن يبقى بين جانبي الزق قليل هواء فيتخلخل عند الارتفاع، وأن ينفذ الهواء في المسام وإن بولغ في تسديدها، وكذا الزق المفتوح تدخله المسلة، لتكاثف ما فيه من الهواء، أو لخروج بعضه من المسام، وأما شراب الدن فلجواز أن يتكاثف، أو يتبخر، ويتخلخل منه بالأعصار شيء يسير على مقدار الزق.
Fourth: We observe things that definitively indicate the existence of vacuum. Among them: that when a flask is sucked vigorously such that the air inside comes out, then it is inverted over water, water rises into it; if it were not empty but full, water would not enter it as before sucking. And among them: that when a wineskin has one side pressed against the other so that no air remains between them, and its mouth and all pores are sealed with pitch so that no air enters from outside, then when we lift one side from the other, vacuum occurs in it. And among them: that when a wineskin is stretched maximally and its pores sealed, then inflated as much as possible, then a needle or needles are thrust into it, they enter easily; if there were no vacuum, they would not enter due to the impossibility of interpenetration. And among them: that when the full measure of a jar of wine is placed in a wineskin, then both are placed in that jar, they both fit; if there were no gap of vacuum in the wine equal to the wineskin, that would not be possible. And it was answered: that nothing mentioned necessitates vacuum, because it is possible that a small amount of air remaining in the flask becomes rarefied, then returns to its natural volume when sucking stops, so water rises due to the impossibility of vacuum; similarly, it is possible that a little air remains between the sides of the wineskin and becomes rarefied upon lifting; and that air penetrates the pores even if sealing is intensified; and similarly, the needle enters the open wineskin due to condensation of the air inside or egress of some through pores; and as for the jar's wine, it is possible that it condenses or evaporates, and a small amount is rarefied from it by squeezing equal to the wineskin.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for vacuum, consisting of four empirical observations, and then refutes them. The proof offers examples: (1) water rising in a sucked flask, (2) separation of pressed-together wineskin sides creating empty space, (3) a needle easily penetrating an inflated sealed wineskin, and (4) a wine-filled wineskin fitting inside the same jar as the wine originally occupied. The refutation explains each case without recourse to vacuum: in the flask, residual air rarefies and then contracts; in the wineskin, air remains between sides and rarefies; needles enter because air condenses or escapes through pores; and the wine may condense or evaporate to make room. Thus, none of the observations prove vacuum.

Key Terms
خلاء
قارورة
زق
مسلة
تخلخل
تكاثف
تداخل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These everyday examples definitively prove the existence of vacuum.
Correction: The refutation shows that alternative physical processes (rarefaction, condensation, air leakage, evaporation) can explain each observation without assuming vacuum.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation explain water rising into a sucked flask?
A) Vacuum sucks the water in
B) Residual air rarefies and then contracts upon release of suction
C) The flask is completely empty
D) Air pressure pushes water in

Key Takeaway
Everyday observations that appear to prove vacuum can be explained through rarefaction, condensation, and air penetration, preserving the impossibility of absolute vacuum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a phenomenon that seems to confirm a belief you hold, do you pause to consider other possible explanations—as the scholars did here—or do you quickly accept what is apparent? How can you train yourself to actively seek alternative interpretations, especially in matters of faith?
?
The refutation relied on unseen processes like rarefaction and condensation. In your life, when you face trials that seem to contradict Allah's wisdom, do you trust that there are hidden explanations beyond your immediate perception? What step can you take this week to strengthen that trust?
?
This lesson demonstrates how empirical observations can be interpreted in multiple ways. Reflect on a time when you judged someone's actions based solely on what you saw. How can you apply the principle of seeking alternative explanations to avoid hasty judgments and nurture humility in your relationships?

First impossibility argument against vacuum with objections and partial response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first impossibility argument against vacuum, the objections raised, and the beginning of the author's response.

Core Idea
Time and resistance are proportional; vacuum would make them equal, which is absurd.

This passage presents the first of several impossibility arguments against the existence of vacuum. The argument hinges on the relationship between motion time and the density of the medium. The author then considers an objection that motion inherently requires time, and a further objection about the nature of motion, before offering a partial response.

First: The equality of existence of resistance and its non-existence; if we suppose a body moving a parasang in vacuum in one hour, and another like it moving a parasang in plenum in two hours, and another like it moving a parasang in a plenum whose density is half the density of the first, then it would also be one hour—necessarily because time differs according to difference of resistance. Commentary
This passage presents the first impossibility argument against vacuum from the proof of impossibility. The argument claims that if vacuum existed, then the time for motion through a given distance would be the same whether resistance is absent (vacuum) or present (plenum), which contradicts observed variation of time with resistance. Answering the Vacuum Objection: Phenomena Explained Without Vacuum
The author answers an objection that certain phenomena imply vacuum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that common phenomena like water rising in a flask do not necessitate the existence of vacuum.

Core Idea
Phenomena that seem to imply vacuum are actually explained by air expansion, condensation, and pores.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that certain observable phenomena—such as water rising when sucking on a flask—prove the existence of vacuum. He systematically shows that each example can be explained without invoking vacuum, using concepts like air expansion (takhallukh), condensation (takathuf), and penetration through pores. This lesson walks through his arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the use of words like yatakhalakhalu (expands) and yatakāthafu (condenses).
2
Identify each phenomenon and its explanation
The passage presents several examples. List each one and note the alternative explanation given by the author. Check the guided breakdown for a structured list.
3
Understand the unifying principle
Reflect on why all these examples are cited together. The author shows that the impossibility of vacuum (imtināʿ al-khalāʾ) is not contradicted; rather, physical processes like expansion and condensation account for observations.
This reinforces the broader theological/physical principle that vacuum does not occur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب : بأن شيئاً مما ذكر لا يستلزم الخلاء ، لجواز أن يتخلخل قليل هواء يبقى في القارورة، ثم يعود إلى مقداره الطبيعي عند ترك المص فيتصاعد الماء ضرورة امتناع الخلاء وكذا يجوز أن يبقى بين جانبي الزق قليل هواء فيتخلخل عند الارتفاع، وأن ينفذ الهواء في المسام وإن بولغ في تسديدها، وكذا الزق المفتوح تدخله المسلة، لتكاثف ما فيه من الهواء، أو لخروج بعضه من المسام، وأما شراب الدن فلجواز أن يتكاثف، أو يتبخر، ويتخلخل منه بالأعصار شيء يسير على مقدار الزق.
And it was answered: That nothing of what was mentioned necessitates vacuum, because it is possible that a little air remains in the flask and expands, then returns to its natural amount when sucking is stopped, so water rises by necessity of the impossibility of vacuum. Likewise, it is possible that a little air remains between the sides of the waterskin and expands when rising, and that air penetrates through pores even if they are tightly sealed. Likewise, the opened waterskin allows the awl to enter due to condensation of the air inside or due to some air exiting through the pores. As for the beverage of the vessel, it is possible that it condenses or evaporates, and a small amount expands from it according to the amount of the waterskin.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection. The objector claimed that certain observed phenomena imply the existence of vacuum. The author argues that none of these phenomena necessitate vacuum. He gives several examples: when sucking water from a flask, a little air remains and expands, then when sucking stops, the air returns to its natural volume, causing water to rise because vacuum is impossible. Similarly, when a waterskin is squeezed, a little air remains between its sides and expands when pressure is released. Also, air can penetrate through pores even if the skin is well sealed. When a waterskin is opened, the awl enters easily either because the air inside condenses or because some air escapes through pores. For the beverage in a vessel, it is possible that it condenses, or evaporates, and a small amount expands from it according to the capacity of the waterskin. In each case, the behavior can be explained by the expansion, condensation, or movement of air without requiring a vacuum. Thus, the objection fails.

First Impossibility of Vacuum: Equality of Resisting and Non-Resisting Mediums
The author presents the first impossibility that would follow if vacuum existed: equality of resistance and non-resistance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first of the three impossibilities that would follow from the existence of vacuum, and how the author argues that motion in vacuum would imply equality of time with and without resistance.

Core Idea
If vacuum existed, motion in it would take the same time as motion through a resisting medium—an absurd contradiction.

Sanusi begins his proof against the possibility of vacuum by showing that its existence leads to a contradiction regarding motion. He constructs a thought experiment comparing motion in vacuum, in a dense medium, and in a medium of half density. The conclusion that times would be equal is absurd, thereby refuting vacuum. This passage introduces a key argument in classical Islamic physics and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Start by reading the author's initial claim that if vacuum existed, impossibilities follow. Then read the first impossibility: equality of resisting and non-resisting mediums.
Notice how the author sets up a conditional argument.
2
Analyze the three scenarios
The author describes three motions: in vacuum, in dense plenum, and in plenum of half density. Identify what time is assigned to each and why.
The key relationship is that time varies with resistance.
3
Study the objection and reply
An objector argues that motion itself requires time, so the time in the rare plenum is partly due to motion itself. Follow the dialogue to see how the author refutes this.
Pay attention to the distinction between motion abstracted and motion in individuals.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
The author concludes that each specific motion demands a base time determined by the mover or moved, which may be increased by resistance but not decreased below that base time. This shows the absurdity of vacuum.
This step connects the detailed argument back to the impossibility claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : حجة الامتناع . أنه لو وجد لزمتها محالات . الأول: تساوي وجود المعاوق وعدمه، فيما اذا فرضنا من جسم حركة، في فرسخ خلاء وليكن ساعة، وأخرى مثلها في فرسخ ملاء وليكن ساعتين، وأخرى مثلها في فرسخ ملاء قوامه نصف قوام الأول، فيكون أيضاً ساعة ضرورة أن تفاوت الزمان بحسب تفاوت المعاوق. واعترض بأن الحركة تستدعي بنفسها زماناً، ففي الملاء الأرق يكون ساعة بازاء نفس الحركة كما في الخلاء، ونصف ساعة بازاء القوام الذي هو نصف القوام الأول. فإن قيل : الحركة لا تخلو عن سرعة وبطء، لكونها في زمان، ينقسم لا إلى نهاية، فإن أريد بنفس الحركة المجردة عنهما فلا يوجد، فلا يستدعي شيئاً، أو التي في ضمن الجزئيات فلا تقتضي زماناً، وأن اقتضى كل حركة تعني في جزء منه، وهو محال . سلم . قلنا : قد لا ينقسم الزمان إلا وهماً ، فتستحيل الحركة في جزء منه، ولو فالمقصود أن نفس الحركة المخصوصة تستدعي قدراً من الزمان، بحسب حال المحرك أو المتحرك، ثم قد يزاد بحسب حال المعاوق وقد لا يزاد كما في الخلاء).
He said: The proof of impossibility. That if it [vacuum] existed, impossibilities would follow from it. The first: the equality of the existence of a resisting medium and its non-existence, when we suppose a motion of a body in a league of vacuum, let it be an hour, and another motion like it in a league of plenum, let it be two hours, and another like it in a league of plenum whose density is half the density of the first, so it would also be an hour, necessarily because the variation of time is according to the variation of resistance. And it was objected that motion by itself demands time, so in the rarer plenum it is an hour corresponding to the motion itself as in vacuum, and half an hour corresponding to the density which is half the first density. If it is said: Motion is not free from speed or slowness, because it occurs in time that is divisible ad infinitum; if by the motion itself is meant that which is abstracted from them, it does not exist, so it does not demand anything; or which is within individuals, then it does not imply time, and if every motion necessitates something in a part of it, that is impossible. Granted. We say: Time may not be divisible except in imagination, so motion in a part of it is impossible. And the intended meaning is that the specific motion itself demands a certain amount of time, according to the state of the mover or the moved, then it may be increased according to the state of the resistance, and may not be increased as in vacuum.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents the first impossibility that would follow if vacuum existed. The author begins by stating the proof: if vacuum existed, absurdities would follow. The first absurdity is the equality of time for motion with and without a resisting medium. The argument posits three scenarios: a body moving in vacuum (time t1), in a dense plenum (time t2 > t1), and in a plenum of half the density (time t3). If the density is halved, by the rule that time varies with resistance, t3 should equal t1. But t3 is in a plenum, so it should be greater than t1, a contradiction. An objection is raised: motion itself requires time, so the time in the rare plenum is partly due to the motion itself. The author responds via a dialogue: if motion abstracted from speed/slowness doesn't exist, then the premise fails; if motion within individuals, it doesn't imply time. The conclusion is that each specific motion demands a base time determined by the mover or moved, which may be increased by resistance but not decreased below that base time.

Misconception: One might think that motion itself always requires the same amount of time regardless of resistance.
Correction: The argument shows that time of motion varies with resistance; if vacuum existed, motion in vacuum would take the same time as motion through a resisting medium, which is absurd.

Checkpoint

A) That motion would cease altogether.
B) That time of motion with resistance would equal time of motion without resistance.
C) That bodies would move infinitely fast.

Key Takeaway
The first impossibility of vacuum is that it would imply equality of time for motion with and without resistance, an absurdity that refutes the existence of vacuum.

?

?

First Proof for Impossibility of Vacuum: Equality of Motion Times
First proof for impossibility of vacuum using equality of motion times

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that vacuum is impossible, which argues that if vacuum existed, motion times with and without resistance would be equal, a contradiction.

Core Idea
If vacuum existed, motion in a dense medium could be as fast as motion in empty space.

In this passage, proponents of the impossibility of vacuum present their first logical proof. They argue that assuming a vacuum leads to a contradiction: the time of motion through a resisting medium would equal the time through empty space. This clearly false conclusion shows that vacuum cannot exist. Let's follow their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of vacuum
The passage defines vacuum as a state where two bodies do not touch and no body lies between them, but a void exists that could be filled. This sets the stage for the proof.
Vacuum here means empty space without any medium.
2
Follow the three-scenario thought experiment
The proof uses three scenarios: motion in vacuum, in a dense plenum, and in a rarer plenum. Note how the times are set and why they lead to a contradiction.
The key is the proportionality between density and time.
3
Identify the contradictory conclusion
The argument shows that motion in a rarer plenum would take the same time as motion in vacuum, which is absurd because resistance should slow motion.
This contradiction proves the impossibility of vacuum.
4
Consider the objection
An objection is raised: it may not be possible to have a plenum with exactly the required density ratio, because density might have a minimum.
This objection questions the feasibility of the thought experiment's assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : احتج القائلون بامتناع الخلاء، أي كون الجسمين بحيث لا يتماسان ، ولا يكون بينهما جسم يماسهما ، بل فراغ يمكن أن يشغله شاغل موجوداً كان أو معدوماً بوجوه . الأول: أنه لو تحقق الخلاء لزم أن يكون زمان الحركة مع المعاوق، مساوياً لزمان تلك الحركة بدون المعاوق، واللازم ظاهر البطلان . بيان اللزوم : أنا نفرض حركة جسم في فرسخ من الخلاء، ولا محالة تكون في زمان ولنفرضه ساعة، ثم نفرض حركة ذلك الجسم بتلك القوة بعينها في فرسخ من الملاء، ولا محالة تكون في زمان أكثر لوجود المعائق، ولنفرضه ساعتين ، ثم نفرض حركته بتلك القوة في ملاء أرق قواماً من الملاء الأول على نسبة زمان حركة الخلاء إلى زمان حركة الملاء الأول، أي يكون قوامه نصف قوام الأول، فيلزم أن يكون زمان الحركة في الملاء الأرق ساعة ضرورة أنه إذا اتحدت المسافة والمتحرك، والقوى المحركة لم تكن السرعة والبطء . أعني قلة الزمان وكثرته، إلا بحسب قلة المعاوق وكثرته، فيلزم تساوي زمان حركة ذي المعاوق. أعني في الملاء الأرق، وزمان حركة عديم المعاوق أعني التي في الخلاء . واعترض أولاً : بمنع إمكان قوام تكون على نسبة زمان الخلاء إلى زمان الملاء، وإنما يتم لو لم يكن ينتهي القوام إلى ما لا قوام أرق منه، وهو ممنوع .
He said: Those who affirm the impossibility of vacuum argued, i.e., the state of two bodies such that they do not touch and there is no body between them that touches them, but rather a void that can be occupied by an occupant, whether existent or non-existent, with proofs. The first: that if vacuum were realized, it would necessarily follow that the time of motion with resistance equals the time of that motion without resistance, and the consequent is clearly false. Explanation of the implication: We posit a motion of a body in a league of vacuum, and inevitably it occurs in a time, let it be an hour; then we posit a motion of that body with the same force in a league of plenum, and inevitably it occurs in a longer time due to the presence of obstacles, let it be two hours; then we posit its motion with that force in a plenum whose density is less than the first plenum in the ratio of the time of motion in vacuum to the time of motion in the first plenum, i.e., its density is half the first density. Then it necessarily follows that the time of motion in the rarer plenum is an hour, because when the distance, the moved body, and the moving forces are the same, speed and slowness—I mean the paucity and abundance of time—are only according to the paucity and abundance of resistance. So it follows that the time of motion with resistance—i.e., in the rarer plenum—equals the time of motion without resistance—i.e., that in vacuum. And it was objected first: by denying the possibility of density being in the ratio of the time of vacuum to the time of plenum; that would only be complete if density did not terminate at a density that is rarer than which there is no rarer, and it is denied.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof from those who affirm the impossibility of vacuum. The author defines vacuum as a state where two bodies do not touch and no body lies between them, but there is a void that could be filled. The first proof is that if vacuum existed, the time for motion with resistance would equal the time for motion without resistance. This is absurd. The proof is explained by positing three scenarios: motion in vacuum (time t1), in a dense plenum (time t2 > t1), and in a plenum of half the density of the first plenum. By the ratio of times, the third scenario's density should be half, leading to time t3 = t1. But t3 is in a plenum, so it should be greater than t1. This contradiction shows the impossibility. An objection is raised: one may deny that a density can be exactly half or that such a ratio is possible, arguing that density may have a lower limit. The objection is noted but left unresolved in this passage.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that density can be arbitrarily reduced to match any ratio.
Correction: The objection points out that density may have a lower limit, so the required ratio may not be achievable.

Checkpoint

A) That motion in vacuum is impossible.

C) That the force might vary.

Key Takeaway
The first proof uses a thought experiment to show that if vacuum existed, motion through a resisting medium would take the same time as through vacuum, a contradiction, but the objection questions whether the required density ratio is possible.

?

Are you willing to examine your foundational assumptions with the same rigor as the objector?
?
This argument uses a reductio ad absurdum to prove that vacuum cannot exist. In your faith, do you ever encounter arguments that seem to prove something about Allah's creation through pure logic? How do you balance the authority of reason with the recognition that Allah's power may surpass our logical categories? What does this lesson teach you about the limits of rational proofs?

Objections on Resistance: Divisibility and Weakness
Two objections deny resistance divisibility and its possible equality to non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two objections raised against the divisibility of resistance (al-mu'aawiq) and the possibility of its weakening to the point of equality with non-existence.

Core Idea
Resistance is not simply divisible with the body, and it can weaken to the verge of non-existence.

This passage presents two objections that challenge the assumption that resistance (al-mu'aawiq) is divisible exactly like the body and that it cannot weaken to complete nonexistence. The objections argue that for resistance to be divisible, it must be a force permeating the body and not dependent on a specific amount of body. Furthermore, they claim that there is no impossibility for resistance to become so weak that its existence is equivalent to its non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two objections
Read the Arabic passage carefully and note the two objections marked by 'ثانياً' and 'ثالثاً'. Then read the translation.
2
Understand the first objection: denial of divisibility
The first objection denies that resistance is divided by the division of the body. It lists conditions that would need to hold for such divisibility.
The objector is not affirming these conditions; he is stating they would need to be proven.
3
Examine the conditions for divisibility
For resistance to be divisible into resistant parts, it would have to be a force permeating the body, divisible with it, and not dependent on a specific quantity of body.
4
Understand the second objection: possibility of extreme weakness
The second objection denies that it is impossible for resistance to weaken to the point where its existence equals its non-existence. This challenges a claim that resistance cannot become that weak.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these objections affect the original claim about resistance. They show that the properties of resistance are not straightforward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانياً: يمنع انقسام المعاوق بانقسام القوام، بحيث يكون جزء المعاوق معاوقاً، وإنما يتم لو ثبت أن المعاوقة قوة سارية في الجسم، منقسمة بانقسامه، غير متوقفة على قدر من القوام بحيث لا يوجد بدونه . وثالثاً : بمنع امتناع أن تنتهى المعاوق من الضعف، إلى حيث يساوي : وجوده عدمه.
Secondly: It is prevented that the resistance is divided by the division of the body, such that a part of the resistance is resistant. This would only be complete if it is established that resistance is a force permeating the body, divisible by its division, not dependent on a certain amount of body such that it does not exist without it. And thirdly: By preventing that it is impossible for the resistance to end in weakness to the point where its existence equals its non-existence.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage lists two objections against a previous claim (likely that resistance is divisible and that it can diminish to a point of equality with non-existence). The first objection denies that resistance is divisible by the division of the body (qiwām). The objection argues that for resistance to be divisible, it would have to be a force permeating the body, divisible with it, and not dependent on a specific quantity of body. The second objection denies that it is impossible for resistance to weaken to the point where its existence equals its non-existence; that is, it is possible for resistance to become so weak that it is effectively nonexistent, thereby contradicting the claim that resistance cannot reach that point.

Fourth Objection: Motion Itself Requires Time
Abu al-Barakat's objection: motion inherently requires time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection to the proportionality argument: that motion itself demands time, and how Abu al-Barakat's objection challenges the reliance on resistance alone.

Core Idea
Motion itself demands time, not just resistance.

The fourth objection is the most relied-upon prevention against the philosophers' premise that time is solely proportional to resistance. Abu al-Barakat argues that motion, by virtue of its own nature and the force involved, requires a definite time, even without any resistance. This lesson unpacks his objection and the subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the main argument. Notice the structure: objection, explanation, clarification, and reply.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the statement that motion itself demands time (الحركة بنفسها تستدعي شيئاً من الزمان). This is the pivot of the objection.
The claim challenges the idea that time is only due to resistance.
3
Understand the two components of time
Note that time is divided into a part due to motion itself and a part due to resistance. In a vacuum, only the motion time exists.
This distinguishes between intrinsic and extrinsic factors.
4
Examine the clarification and replies
See how the author clarifies 'motion itself' and then addresses potential objections about speed/slowness and indivisible time.
The discussion about infinite divisibility and impossibility is subtle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعاً : وهو المنع المعول عليه، إذ ربما يمكن إثبات المقدمات(۱)، سيما على أصول الفلاسفة أنه لا يلزم من كون المعاوقتين على نسبة الزمانين أن يكون (۲) زمان قليل المعاوق مساوياً لزمان ،عدميه وإنما يلزم لو لم يكن الزمان إلا بإزاء المعاوقة، وأما إذا كانت الحركة بنفسها تستدعي شيئاً من الزمان، كالساعة المفروضة في الخلاء فلا إذ في المعاوق القليل تكون ساعة بإزاء نفس الحركة، كما في الخلاء، ونصف ساعة بإزاء المعاوقة التي هي نصف المعاوقة الكثيرة، التي تقع ساعة بإزائها، وهذا الاعتراض لأبي البركات، ومعناه على ما يشعر به كلامه في المعتبر أن كل ما يقع من الحركة، وهي من جهة القوة المحركة والجسم المتحرك يستدعي زماناً محدوداً يجزم العقل بذلك، وإن لم يتصور معاوقة المخروق ثم يزداد الزمان إن تحققت المعاوقة، فيكون البعض منه بإزاء المعاوقة والبعض بإزاء ،الحركة وهو زمان الخلاء، ويتفاوت بحسب قوة المحرك وخاصية المتحرك، والمراد بنفس الحركة حركة (۳) ذلك الجسم بتلك القوة من غير اعتبار معاوقة المخروق لا ماهية الحركة من حيث هي هي، ليدفع الاعتراض بأنها لو اقتضت قدراً من الزمان لزم ثبوت ذلك القدر لكل من جزئيات الحركة لامتناع تخلف مقتضى الماهية واللازم باطل كما في الحركة المفروضة في جزء (٤) من ذلك (٥) القدر من الزمان، ولا الحركة المجردة من السرعة والبطء ليدفع بما ذكره بعض المحققين من أن الحركة تمتنع أن توجد إلا على (۱) حد السرعة والبطء، لأنها لا محالة تكون في مسافة وزمان ينقسم كل منها لا إلى نهاية . وإذا فرضنا وقوع أخرى تقطع تلك المسافة في نصف ذلك الزمان فالأولى أبطأ منها، أو في ضعفه فأسرع، فالحركة المجردة لا(۳) توجد وما لا يوجد لا يستدعي شيئاً، ويرد على الوجهين أن زمان الحركة قد يكون بحيث لا ينقسم إلا وهماً، فيكون (۳) الحركة في جزء منه محال، والمحال جاز أن يستلزم المحال، فلا يكفي في إثبات بطء الأولى ونفي كونها أسرع الحركات فرض وقوع الأخرى، ما لم يتبين إمكانها .
Fourthly: And it is the relied-upon prevention, because perhaps it is possible to prove the premises (1), especially according to the principles of the philosophers, that it does not follow from the two resistances being in proportion to the two times that the time of little resistance is equal to the time of its non-existence. It would only follow if time were only in proportion to the resistance. But if motion itself demands some time, like the supposed hour in a vacuum, then no; because in the case of little resistance, there is an hour in proportion to the motion itself, as in the vacuum, and half an hour in proportion to the resistance which is half of the great resistance that takes an hour in proportion to it. This objection is by Abu al-Barakat, and its meaning, as suggested by his words in al-Mu'tabar, is that every motion that occurs—while it is from the aspect of the moving force and the moving body—demands a limited time that the intellect decisively judges, even if no resistance of the thing pierced is imagined. Then time increases if resistance is realized, so a part of it is in proportion to the resistance and a part in proportion to the motion, which is the time of the vacuum, and it varies according to the strength of the mover and the property of the moved. What is meant by 'the motion itself' is the motion of that body with that force without considering the resistance of the pierced object, not the essence of motion in itself, in order to refute the objection that if it required a amount of time, then that amount would be established for each particular motion, because it is impossible for the requirement of the essence to lag behind—and the consequent is false, as in the motion supposed in a part of that amount of time—and also it is not the motion stripped of speed and slowness, in order to refute what some investigators mentioned: that motion is impossible to exist except in a certain degree of speed and slowness, because it inevitably exists in a distance and time each of which divides infinitely. And if we suppose another motion occurring that covers that distance in half that time, then the first is slower than it, or in double then faster, so the stripped motion does not exist, and what does not exist does not demand anything. And there is a reply to both aspects: that the time of motion may be such that it does not divide except in a notion, so motion in part of it is impossible, and the impossible may entail the impossible, so the supposition of another motion is insufficient to prove the slowness of the first and to negate that it is the fastest of motions, unless its possibility is shown.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth and main objection (المنع المعول عليه). The objector argues that from two resistances being proportional to two times, it does not necessarily follow that the time for a weaker resistance equals the time for no resistance. This would only follow if time were solely due to resistance. However, motion itself demands some time, like an hour in a vacuum. In the case of little resistance, part of the time is due to motion itself and part due to the resistance. This objection is attributed to Abu al-Barakat. The explanation continues: every motion, from the aspect of the moving force and the moving body, demands a definite time independent of resistance. Time increases with resistance. The phrase 'motion itself' means the motion of that specific body with that specific force, not the essence of motion in general. This avoids the objection that if motion as such required time, that time would apply to all motions. Also, motion is always with some speed or slowness, so bare motion does not exist. The passage then considers a possible reply: the time of motion might be indivisible except notionally, and motion in a part of it would be impossible, and impossibility can entail impossibility, so a mere supposition of another motion does not prove slowness or the non-ultimateness of speed.
