Objection on Speed and Answer Based on Slowness
Objection affirms speed is undeniable, answered by grounding the argument in slowness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that speed alone proves motion requires time is refuted by grounding the argument in the establishment of slowness.

Core Idea
Speed alone cannot prove motion requires time; slowness must first be established.

This passage presents a critical objection to the earlier argument about motion and time. The objector concedes that slowness may be absent but insists that speed is undeniable. The answer shows why the refutation must start from slowness, not speed, and clarifies that speed is mentioned only for contrast.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the objector's logic: they accept no slowness but claim speed is clear because another motion could take longer.
The objector is trying to prove that motion necessarily has a time, using speed.
2
Understand the answer's reliance on slowness
The answer states that the refutation is built on establishing slowness first. Without slowness, the argument about speed is not sufficient.
The key phrase: 'دفع الاعتراض إنما يبتني على ثبوت البطء'.
3
See the reference to al-Mawaqif
The answer ends with a reference to al-Mawaqif: if motion required time for its essence, the motion in that time would be the fastest. This reinforces the point.
This shows the continuity of the argument across works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : سلمنا انتفاء البطء لكن لا خفاء في ثبوت السرعة، لإمكان وقوع الأخرى في زمان أكثر ، فلا تثبت المجردة من السرعة والبطء : قلنا: دفع الاعتراض إنما يبتني على ثبوت البطء ليتفرع عليه كون الزمان بحسب المعاوقة ، وذكر السرعة إنما هو بحكم المقابلة، ولهذا عبر في المواقف عن هذا الدفع بأن الحركة لو اقتضت زماناً لذاتها لكانت الحركة الواقعة فيه أسرع الحركات على ما مر .
For it is said: We concede the absence of slowness, but there is no concealment regarding the establishment of speed, because of the possibility of another motion occurring in a longer time, so the stripped [motion] of speed and slowness is not established. We say: The refutation of the objection is only based on the establishment of slowness so that the consequence that time is according to resistance is based upon it; and the mention of speed is only by way of contrast. Hence, it was expressed in al-Mawaqif regarding this refutation that if motion required time for its essence, then the motion occurring in that time would be the fastest of motions, as has passed.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objector says: We accept that slowness is absent (i.e., the motion in question is not slow), but speed is clearly present because another motion could occur in a longer time, so the motion stripped of speed and slowness is not established. The answer is that the refutation relies on establishing slowness first, because then the time would be according to resistance. The mention of speed is only for contrast. The answer refers to al-Mawaqif, where it is stated that if motion required time for its essence, the motion occurring in that time would be the fastest of motions.

Motion Necessarily Has Speed or Slowness
Argument that motion requires speed or slowness, with objections and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that motion cannot exist without speed or slowness and the key objections and responses from classical kalam.

Core Idea
Motion cannot exist without speed or slowness because it involves time and distance, which are divisible.

This passage presents a foundational argument in classical physics and kalam: motion always occurs with some degree of speed or slowness. The author uses the infinite divisibility of time and distance to show that a motion without these attributes is impossible. He then addresses objections, including the notion of indivisible time and the possibility of speed without slowness, and discusses the role of resistance. By the end, you will see how this argument ties time to motion and resistance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the text's logical flow sink in before analyzing.
2
Identify the main claim and its proof
Find where the author states that motion must have speed or slowness. Notice how he uses the infinite divisibility of distance and time to argue that any motion can be compared to a faster or slower one.
The key phrase is 'لأنها لا محالة تكون في مسافة وزمان ينقسم كل منها لا إلى نهاية'.
3
Examine the objections and responses
Look for the two main objections: one about indivisible time and one that grants speed but denies slowness. See how the author responds, especially the dependence on establishing slowness first.
The response to the second objection uses the concept of resistance (المعاوقة) from al-Mawaqif.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمتنع أن توجد إلا على (۱) حد السرعة والبطء ، لأنها لا محالة تكون في مسافة وزمان ينقسم كل منها لا إلى نهاية .
وإذا فرضنا وقوع أخرى تقطع تلك المسافة في نصف ذلك الزمان فالأولى أبطأ منها، أو في ضعفه فأسرع، فالحركة المجردة لا(۳) توجد وما لا يوجد لا يستدعي شيئاً، ويرد على الوجهين أن زمان الحركة قد يكون بحيث لا ينقسم إلا وهماً، فيكون (۳) الحركة في جزء منه محال، والمحال جاز أن يستلزم المحال، فلا يكفي في إثبات بطء الأولى ونفي كونها أسرع الحركات فرض وقوع الأخرى، ما لم يتبين إمكانها .
فإن قيل : سلمنا انتفاء البطء لكن لا خفاء في ثبوت السرعة، لإمكان وقوع الأخرى في زمان أكثر ، فلا تثبت المجردة من السرعة والبطء :
قلنا: دفع الاعتراض إنما يبتني على ثبوت البطء ليتفرع عليه كون الزمان بحسب المعاوقة ، وذكر السرعة إنما هو بحكم المقابلة، ولهذا عبر في المواقف عن هذا الدفع بأن الحركة لو اقتضت زماناً لذاتها لكانت الحركة الواقعة فيه أسرع الحركات على ما مر .
وقد يقال في تقرير كلام المحقق إن الحركة لا توجد إلا مع وصف السرعة والبطء ) وهما بحسب المعاوقة فلا حركة إلا مع المعاوقة (ه)، فإذا كان الزمان بإزاء الحركة كان بإزاء المعاوقة، وإن لم يكن لها دخل في اقتضائه وحينئذ لا يرد الاعتراض بأن امتناع وجود الحركة بدون السرعة والبطء لا ينافي استدعاءها بنفسها شيئاً من الزمان، ولا النقض باللوازم التي تقتضيها الماهية مع امتناع أن توجد إلا مع شيء من العوارض لكنه لا يدفع اعتراض أبي البركات، ولا تثبت دعوى المحقق أن الحركة نفسها لا تستدعي شيئاً من الزمان .
It is impossible for [motion] to exist except with some degree of speed and slowness, because it necessarily occurs in a distance and time each of which is divisible infinitely. If we suppose another motion covering that distance in half that time, the first is slower than it; or in double [the time], then it is faster. So motion devoid [of speed and slowness] does not exist, and what does not exist does not require anything. It is objected on both sides that the time of motion might be such that it is not divisible except conceivably, so motion in a part of it is impossible, and the impossible may entail the impossible. Thus it is not sufficient to prove the slowness of the first and to negate its being the fastest of motions to suppose the occurrence of the other, unless its possibility is shown. If it is said: we grant the negation of slowness, but there is no doubt about the affirmation of speed, because it is possible for the other to occur in more time, so motion devoid of speed and slowness is not established. We say: the refutation of the objection is based only on establishing slowness, so that the time being according to resistance follows from it. Mention of speed is only by way of contrast. Hence it was expressed in al-Mawaqif that this refutation is that if motion necessitated time in itself, then the motion occurring in that time would be the fastest of motions, as previously mentioned. And it may be said in clarifying the speech of the investigator (al-Muhaqqiq) that motion does not exist except with the description of speed and slowness, and they are according to resistance, so there is no motion except with resistance. Thus if time corresponds to motion, it corresponds to resistance, even if resistance has no role in its necessitation. Then the objection that the impossibility of motion without speed and slowness does not contradict its necessitating something of time in itself does not stand, nor the refutation by the concomitants that the essence necessitates while being impossible to exist except with some accidents. But this does not repel the objection of Abu al-Barakat, nor does it establish the claim of the investigator that motion itself does not require any time.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed argument about motion and its relation to time, speed, and slowness. It begins by stating that motion cannot exist except with some degree of speed or slowness, because it necessarily occurs in a distance and time that are infinitely divisible. Then it considers hypothetical faster and slower motions to show that motion cannot be devoid of speed or slowness. Opponents object that time may be indivisible in some respects, making motion in a part impossible, and the impossible may imply impossibility. The author responds that mere supposition is insufficient unless possibility is established. Another objection affirms speed while denying slowness, to which the author replies that the refutation depends on establishing slowness, and speed is mentioned only for contrast. The passage then explains the position of 'the investigator' (al-Muhaqqiq) that motion only exists with speed or slowness, which are determined by resistance (mu'aawaqa). So time corresponds to resistance. However, this does not fully refute the objection of Abu al-Barakat and does not prove that motion itself requires no time. The argument is dense and assumes familiarity with concepts of motion, time, and the principle of no preponderance.

Correction: The passage argues that since motion occurs in continuous distance and time, any motion can be compared to another that is faster or slower, so motion devoid of these attributes is impossible.

The investigator's position is that motion only exists with resistance, linking even the most basic physical phenomenon to interaction and dependence. Reflect on the things you consider 'self-sufficient' in your life. In what ways are they actually sustained by forces outside themselves—ultimately by Allah? How does that change your sense of gratitude and reliance?

Third Argument on Void: Stone Thrown Upward
Argument that a stone thrown upward would reach the sky if no void, and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against the possibility of void and why its refutation shows the argument is not decisive.

Core Idea
Absence of resistance does not guarantee void's non-existence.

This lesson presents a brief argument against the void, followed by a concise refutation. The claim is that if void existed between earth and sky, a thrown stone would reach the sky unimpeded. The refutation points out that even without resistance, the stone's arrival does not prove void is absolutely impossible. We'll explore why this argument fails to settle the matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the translation. Note the structure: a claim and a refutation.
The passage is concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the argument and its refutation
The argument says a stone thrown upward would reach the sky if there were no resistance (i.e., void). The refutation says this does not imply the absolute non-existence of void. Why might that be?
Think about other possible factors that could stop the stone besides resistance.
3
Reflect on why the refutation works
The refutation denies that the argument proves void's impossibility. For example, the stone might still be affected by other forces or the sky might be unreachable for other reasons. Thus, the argument is not decisive.
This shows the importance of rigorous reasoning in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : وصول الحجر المرمي إلى السماء لعدم المعاوق . ورد بأنه لا يقتضي عدمه مطلقاً.
The third [argument]: The reaching of the thrown stone to the sky due to lack of resistance. It is refuted by [the fact] that it does not imply its absolute non-existence.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This is another argument against the possibility of void. The claim is that if there were void between earth and sky, a stone thrown upward would reach the sky because there is no resistance. The refutation states that this does not necessarily imply the absolute non-existence of void, because other factors may intervene. So the argument is not decisive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if void exists, then any object thrown upward would travel to infinity or reach the sky because there is no resistance, and therefore if we observe that objects do not do so, void cannot exist.
Correction: The refutation shows that the absence of resistance does not prove void's absolute non-existence. The stone may not reach the sky due to other reasons, such as loss of momentum or the structure of the heavens. Thus, the argument is not a decisive proof against void.

Checkpoint

B) By stating that the absence of resistance does not imply absolute non-existence of void.

Key Takeaway
The third argument against void is inconclusive because the absence of resistance does not prove void's absolute non-existence.

?
This argument and refutation show that even a plausible logical claim can fail to prove absolute non-existence. Is there an area in your life where you have dismissed something as impossible or false based on limited evidence? What would it cost you to honestly reconsider that dismissal?
?
The refutation does not outright disprove the argument, but shows it is inconclusive. How comfortable are you with uncertainty? In your faith or daily decisions, where are you demanding absolute proofs that may not be available—and how might that affect your relationship with truth?

Fourth Argument: Observed Phenomena and Their Refutation
Argument from observed phenomena against void, refuted by alternative causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why observed phenomena like cupping and water tubes do not necessarily prove the existence of void, as they can be explained by other causes.

Core Idea
Observed phenomena do not prove void; alternative causes are always possible.

This lesson presents the fourth argument against the existence of void, which relies on everyday observations. However, the author refutes this by pointing out that these observations could have other explanations. Understanding this refutation is crucial for grasping the broader debate on void in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the four examples given.
The passage is concise; focus on the structure of argument and refutation.
2
Identify the four phenomena
List the four observed phenomena presented by the proponents of void: meat rising in cupping glass, water in tube, water not descending from blocked jug, flask breaking outward/inward.
These are empirical observations used as evidence.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation states that these phenomena could be due to other causes, not necessarily void. This undercuts the argument's validity.
The key phrase is "ورد بجواز أن يكون لأسباب أخر" (it is refuted by the permissibility of other causes).
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider why this refutation is powerful: empirical evidence alone does not prove a specific cause if alternative explanations are plausible.
This connects to broader kalam methodology about causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: انتفاء ما يشاهد من ارتفاع اللحم في المحجمة (۱) ، والماء في الأنبوبة، وعدم نزول الماء من ثقبة الكوز المسدود (۲) الرأس، وانكسار القارورة التي في رأسها خشبة إلى خارج إن أدخلت وإلى داخل إن أخرجت . ورد بجواز أن يكون لأسباب أخر (۳)) .
The fourth [argument]: The non-existence of what is observed of the rising of meat in the cupping glass, and water in the tube, and the non-descent of water from the hole of the jug whose head is blocked, and the breaking of the flask which has a stick in its head outward if inserted and inward if extracted. It is refuted by the permissibility that it may be due to other causes.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This argument lists several observed phenomena that seem to depend on the existence of void: meat rising in a cupping glass, water rising in a tube, water not descending from a blocked jug, and a flask breaking outward or inward depending on insertion of a stick. The refutation states that these phenomena could be due to other causes, not necessarily void. So the argument does not prove the existence of void.

Key Terms

الماء في الأنبوبة

Misconception: Observed phenomena like cupping and water tubes necessarily prove the existence of void.
Correction: These phenomena can be explained by other causes, so they do not conclusively prove void.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument against void fails because its observed effects can be due to alternative causes.

Stone Reaches Sky in Void Due to Lack of Resistance
Third aspect of argument against void, using projectile motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against the existence of void, which claims that without the plenum's collisions, an object's motion would be unrestricted.

Core Idea
If void existed, a thrown stone would never stop.

This lesson presents the third aspect of the second argument against the existence of void. The argument extrapolates from everyday experience: a stone thrown upward eventually stops because it collides with air. If void replaced the air, there would be nothing to resist the stone's upward force, so it would continue to the sky. This leads to a conclusion the author finds absurd, thereby undermining the possibility of void.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation. Notice that it is a conditional argument: 'if void existed... then the stone would reach the sky.' Identify the key causal claim about resistance.
The passage is incomplete; it ends mid-sentence, suggesting an unstated conclusion.
2
Understand the causal reasoning
The thrower imparts an 'ascending force' to the stone. The only thing that stops this force, according to the argument, is collisions with the plenum (filled matter). Without those collisions (i.e., in void), the force continues unopposed.
This assumes that no other forces (like gravity or internal resistance) act on the stone.
3
Identify the unstated conclusion
The argument is left incomplete: it likely concludes that since the stone obviously does not reach the sky, void cannot exist. Reflect on why this conclusion is considered a reductio ad absurdum.
The author's broader context is defending the existence of a plenum against atomists who posit void.
4
Evaluate the argument's strength
Consider the hidden assumption that collisions are the sole source of resistance. Is this assumption justified? What other factors might slow a projectile?
This connects to the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : أنه لو وجد الخلاء بين الارض والسماء لزم في الحجر المرمي إلى فوق أن يصل إلى السماء لأن الرامي قد أحدث فيه قوة صاعدة، لا تقاومها الطبيعة إلا بمعونة مصادمات من الملاء.
The third aspect: That if void existed between the earth and the sky, it would necessarily follow that the stone thrown upward would reach the sky, because the thrower has produced in it an ascending force, which nature resists only with the assistance of collisions from the plenum.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This is the third aspect of the second argument against void. It claims that if there were void between earth and sky, a stone thrown upward would reach the sky without hindrance, because the only resistance to its ascending force comes from collisions with the plenum (the filled space). If void is present, those collisions are absent, so the stone would continue to the sky. This argument is left incomplete in the text, but it is part of a series of arguments against the existence of void.

Key Terms
الخلاء
الحجر المرمي
قوة صاعدة
مصادمات
الملاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that void cannot exist because a stone thrown upward would never stop.
Correction: The argument assumes that only collisions from the plenum resist motion. However, other forces (e.g., gravity, air resistance even in thin media, or intrinsic properties of the stone) could also slow it down. Thus, even in a void, the stone might not reach the sky due to other factors.

Checkpoint
What does the third aspect claim would happen if void existed between earth and sky?
A) The stone would fall to the ground.
B) The stone would reach the sky due to lack of resistance.
C) The stone would stop midway.
D) The stone would move sideways.

Key Takeaway
The third aspect argues that if void existed, a stone thrown upward would reach the sky because no collisions from the plenum would resist its ascending force, an outcome the author deems absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Begin now to pray specifically for Him to block paths of destruction.
?
The author concludes that void cannot exist. Where in your life have you assumed a 'void'—an area where you think God is absent or not in control? Three Objections to Vacuum and Their Refutations
Taftazani enumerates three objections to vacuum and their refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three common objections raised against the existence of vacuum and how they are refuted by allowing alternative explanations.

Core Idea
Empirical objections to vacuum are weak; other causes can explain the phenomena.

Al-Taftazani now addresses three objections that might be raised by those who argue for the impossibility of vacuum. These objections range from metaphysical puzzles about body placement to everyday phenomena like cupping and siphons. The refutations consistently point out that these phenomena do not necessarily require vacuum to be impossible; other causes could equally explain them. This lesson walks through each objection and its brief refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three objections
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that the author enumerates these as 'second', 'third', and 'fourth' objections (continuing from a previous discussion).
The numbering indicates these are part of a larger series of arguments.
2
Examine each refutation's logic
For each objection, see how the refutation appeals to 'external causes' or 'other causes'. Understand that this shifts the burden of proof: the opponent must show that no other cause is possible.
3
Reflect on the strength of empirical evidence
Consider why the empirical phenomena (cupping, siphon, etc.) are not conclusive proofs. The author suggests they could have causes other than the impossibility of vacuum.
This is a key principle in kalam: observable phenomena can have multiple explanations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قال): الثاني : امتناع حصول الأجسام اذ لا أولوية لبعض الجوانب. ورد بجواز استناد الاختصاص إلى أسباب خارجة . الثالث : وصول الحجر المرمي إلى السماء لعدم المعاوق . ورد بأنه لا يقتضي عدمه مطلقاً. الرابع: انتفاء ما يشاهد من ارتفاع اللحم في المحجمة (۱) ، والماء في الأنبوبة، وعدم نزول الماء من ثقبة الكوز المسدود (۲) الرأس، وانكسار القارورة التي في رأسها خشبة إلى خارج إن أدخلت وإلى داخل إن أخرجت . ورد بجواز أن يكون لأسباب أخر.
He said: Second: The impossibility of bodies occurring [in vacuum] because there is no priority of some sides [over others]. It was answered by allowing the attribution [of priority] to external causes. Third: The arrival of a thrown stone to the sky due to the absence of an obstacle. It was answered by saying that it does not necessitate its absolute non-existence. Fourth: The non-occurrence of what is observed of the rising of flesh in the cupping-glass (1), and water in the pipe, and the non-descending of water from the hole of the jug whose head is stopped (2), and the breaking of the flask which has a wooden stick in its head outward if inserted and inward if extracted. It was answered by allowing that it could be due to other causes.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage enumerates three objections that opponents of vacuum might raise, along with brief refutations. The first objection is that if vacuum existed, bodies could not come to be in it because no particular location would be preferred over another; the refutation is that preference can be due to external causes. The second objection is that a stone thrown upward would reach the sky if there were no resisting medium; the refutation is that this does not prove the stone would actually reach the sky without any resistance. The third objection cites empirical phenomena that seem to require the avoidance of vacuum (cupping, siphon, jug, flask); the refutation is that these could be due to other causes, not necessarily the impossibility of vacuum. The author presents these as weak arguments that do not conclusively prove the existence of vacuum.

Key Terms

امتناع
أولوية
مرجح
معاوق
محجمة
أنبوبة
كوز
قارورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Empirical proofs like cupping and siphons decisively prove the impossibility of vacuum.
Correction: These phenomena can be explained by other causes; they do not conclusively prove that vacuum is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the common thread in the refutations of the three objections?
A) They all appeal to divine intervention.
B) They all allow that observable phenomena might have causes other than the impossibility of vacuum.
C) They all deny the empirical observations.
D) They all assert that vacuum is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Empirical phenomena can have multiple explanations; they do not definitively prove the impossibility of vacuum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Second Argument Against Vacuum: No Preponderance
Al-Taftazani details the second argument against vacuum and its response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refined argument that a body cannot occupy a specific location in a uniform vacuum without a preponderator, and how the response rescues the possibility by invoking external causes.

Core Idea
Uniform vacuum offers no reason for a body to be here rather than there, but external factors break the symmetry.

Al-Taftazani presents a more nuanced objection to the existence of vacuum: if vacuum were uniform, a body would have no basis for being in one spot rather than another. However, the response shows that this objection only holds if vacuum is finite and we consider the entire universe as a whole. In other cases, external causes like God's will or natural dispositions can determine location.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the response. Pay attention to the key terms like 'pure negation' and 'homogeneous extension'.
2
Identify the core objection
The objection states that if vacuum existed, a body would be in a specific place without a preponderator because vacuum is uniform (either pure negation or homogeneous extension). Ask yourself why uniformity leads to impossibility.
The key assumption is that like things differ only by materials; vacuum has no material differentiation.
3
Analyze the response
The response concedes uniformity but distinguishes two scenarios: (1) finite vacuum containing the entire world — there is no preponderance needed for the whole, (2) infinite vacuum or a single body — external causes like God's will or natural dispositions can provide preponderance. See how this undermines the objection.
The response does not refute the objection entirely but shows it is not universally applicable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الثاني : الوجه الثاني أنه لو وجد الخلاء لامتنع حصول الجسم فيه، لأن اختصاصه بحيز من دون حيز ترجح بلا مرجح ، لكونه نفياً صرفاً، أو بعداً متشابهاً، ليس فيه اختلاف أصلا، لكون اختلاف الأمثال بالمواد، وأجيب بعد تسليم المتشابه : بأنه لا رجحان بالنسبة إلى جميع العالم على تقدير تناهي الخلاء، لأنه في جميع الأجزاء، وأما على تقدير لا تناهيه أو بالنسبة إلى جسم فيجوز أن يكون الرجحان بأسباب خارجة كإرادة المختار، وكون طبيعة بعض الأجسام مقتضية للإحاطة بالكل، وبعضها للقرب من المحيط أو البعد عنه .
He said, the second: The second aspect: that if vacuum existed, the occurrence of a body in it would be impossible, because its specification to one place without another would be a preponderance without a preponderator, since it is pure negation, or a homogeneous extension, having no difference at all, because the difference of like things is due to materials. It was answered, after conceding the homogeneity, that there is no preponderance with respect to the entire world on the supposition of the finitude of vacuum, because it is in all parts. But on the supposition of its infinitude or with respect to a body, it is possible that the preponderance be due to external causes, such as the will of the Free Agent, and that the nature of some bodies requires encompassing the whole, and some [require] proximity to or distance from the encompassing.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents a more refined argument against vacuum: if vacuum existed and was uniform, a body placed in it would have no reason to be in one location rather than another, so its occurrence would be impossible. The answer distinguishes two cases: if vacuum is finite and contains all parts, there is no preponderance needed for the whole universe (it is equally related). But if vacuum is infinite, or for a single body in finite vacuum, external factors like God's will or natural dispositions could determine position. Thus the objection does not hold.

Key Terms
خلاء
حيز
ترجح
مرجح
نفي محض
بعد متشابه
إرادة المختار
طبيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uniformity of vacuum makes any location equally likely, so a body cannot be placed anywhere.
Correction: The response shows that external factors (God's will, natural dispositions) can break the symmetry, making a specific location possible.

Checkpoint
On what condition does the response concede that there is no preponderance in a finite vacuum?
A) When the vacuum is infinite.
B) When considering the entire world equally.
C) When a single body is placed.
D) When the vacuum is homogeneous.

Key Takeaway
The second argument against vacuum fails because external causes can provide the needed preponderance for a body's location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where no internal reason seems to decide your path—such as two equally good choices—do you trust that God's will can break the deadlock? Or do you assume you are stuck and cannot move forward? What would it look like for you to actively rely on the 'external cause' of God's choice today, rather than demanding perfect internal clarity?
?
The argument assumes that without a preponderator, nothing can happen. Are there areas of your life where you demand a clear reason before acting, forgetting that God's will can be the preponderator? How might you surrender your need for complete internal clarity and instead step out in trust that God will guide you?
?
In a world that often expects uniform rules and explanations, how do you react when things happen without apparent internal reason? Does the idea that God's will can override symmetry unsettle you or bring you comfort? What is one decision today where you can consciously acknowledge God as the sole preponderator?

Third aspect: Stone thrown upward would reach the sky
Al-Taftazani refutes the argument that vacuum would let a stone reach the sky.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that vacuum would allow a stone to reach the sky is not conclusive against the possibility of vacuum.

Core Idea
The stone argument proves nothing about vacuum beyond the region between earth and sky.

Al-Taftazani presents the third argument against the possibility of vacuum, but then shows its limitation. The argument assumes only natural causes, and even then it only addresses the region between earth and sky. This lesson examines the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the argument claims that vacuum would let a stone reach the sky due to lack of resistance.
2
Understand the refutation
The response points out two weaknesses: the argument assumes no Free Agent, and it only denies that the space between earth and sky is pure vacuum—it does not disprove vacuum elsewhere or mixed with air.
Note the logical structure: the argument is based on a specific scenario and its conclusion is limited.
3
Grasp the broader implication
Consider why this refutation is important for the overall discussion of vacuum. It shows that the argument is not a decisive proof against vacuum, only against a specific imagined scenario.
This connects to the principle of divine transcendence: the argument assumes natural causation without a free agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : أنه لو وجد الخلاء بين الارض والسماء لزم في الحجر المرمي إلى فوق أن يصل إلى السماء لأن الرامي قد أحدث فيه قوة صاعدة، لا تقاومها الطبيعة إلا بمعونة مصادمات من الملاء. وأجيب بأنه مع ابتنائه على تقدير نفي الفاعل المختار، إنما ينفي كون ما بين الأرض والسماء خلاء صرفاً ، ولا ينفي وجود خلاء خارج عما بينهما أو مختلط بالأجزاء الهوائية .
The third aspect: that if vacuum existed between the earth and the sky, then the stone thrown upward would necessarily reach the sky, because the thrower has produced in it an upward force that nature does not resist except with the help of impacts [collisions] from the plenum. It was answered that, while it is based on the supposition of denying the Free Agent, it only denies that what is between the earth and sky is pure vacuum, and does not deny the existence of vacuum outside what is between them or mixed with the air particles.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This argument claims that if vacuum existed between earth and sky, a stone thrown upward would encounter no resistance and thus reach the sky. The refutation points out two flaws: first, the argument assumes no free agent (i.e., it assumes natural causation only), but even so, it only disproves that the region between earth and sky is pure vacuum; it does not disprove vacuum elsewhere or vacuum mixed with air. Thus the argument is not conclusive against the possibility of vacuum in some form.

Key Terms
خلاء
ملاء
قوة صاعدة
مصادمات
فاعل مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the stone argument proves that vacuum is impossible altogether.
Correction: The argument only shows that if vacuum existed specifically between earth and sky, then the stone would behave differently; but it does not disprove vacuum in other places or forms. Moreover, it assumes natural causation without a Free Agent.

Checkpoint
What is the main limitation of the third argument according to the response?
A) It denies the existence of a Free Agent.
B) It only addresses the region between earth and sky.
C) It assumes the stone has no upward force.
D) It ignores the possibility of air particles.

Key Takeaway
The stone argument fails to disprove vacuum because it only addresses a specific region and assumes natural causation without a Free Agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if the 'collisions' of your day (setbacks, obstacles, even successes) are merely natural events, or do you see the hand of the Free Agent behind every one? What habit could you start to remind yourself that God is the true Cause?
?
When you throw yourself into a project or goal, do you assume your effort alone will determine the outcome, forgetting that without God's will it leads nowhere? How can you anchor your plans in trust in the Free Agent today?
?
The answer says the objection only denies vacuum in one specific region. In your life, are there 'regions'—parts of your heart, relationships, or ambitions—where you assume God has no power or involvement? What would it mean to let Him into that space?

Empirical Phenomena Claimed to Avoid Vacuum
List of empirical phenomena used as arguments against vacuum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the empirical phenomena that some argue prove the impossibility of vacuum.

Core Idea
Observed phenomena like cupping and siphons seem to require the impossibility of vacuum.

Al-Taftazani presents the fourth argument against vacuum based on everyday observations. He lists several examples where air or water behaves in a way that suggests vacuum cannot exist. Let's examine these examples carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the examples come through before you interpret.
Notice the pattern: each phenomenon is described as occurring 'lest vacuum occur'.
2
Identify each empirical example
List the four examples: cupping glass, pipe, jug, flask. For each, understand what happens and why it is taken to show avoidance of vacuum.
3
Note the underlying reasoning
For each example, ask: why does this phenomenon supposedly require the impossibility of vacuum? The author's language indicates a causal link.
The phrase 'lest vacuum occur' shows the assumed purpose.
4
Consider the overall argument
These examples are presented as evidence. Before moving to the refutation, notice how they form an inductive argument from multiple observations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : أن لو وجد الخلاء لزم انتفاء أمور نشاهدها، ونحكم بوجودها قطعاً كارتفاع اللحم في المحجمة عند المص، فإنه لما انجذب الهواء بالمص تبعه اللحم لئلا يلزم الخلاء، وكارتفاع الماء في الأنبوبة، إذا غمس أحد طرفيها الطرف الآخر، وكبقاء الماء في الكوز، الذي في أسفله ثقبة ضيقة، من غير أن ينزل من الثقبة عند سد رأس الكوز، لئلا يبقى حيز خالياً، ونزوله على ما هو مقتضى طبعه عند فتح الرأس لدخول الهواء، وكانكسار القارورة التي جعلت في رأسها خشبة وشدت بحيث لا يدخل فيها ولا يخرج عنها هواء، ثم أخرجت الخشبة، فإن القارورة تنكسر إلى الداخل لئلا يبقى حيز الخشبة خالياً، وإذا دخلت تنكسر القارورة إلى الخارج، لما أن فيها ملاء لا يجامع الخشبة، وأجيب بأنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك لأسباب أخر، فإن غاية هذه الأمور لزومها لانتفاء الخلاء، واللازم قد يكون أعم فلا يصح بوجوده على وجود الملزوم . نعم : ربما يفيد يقيناً حدسياً للناظر لكن لا يقوم به حجة على المناظر.
The fourth aspect: that if vacuum existed, there would follow the non-existence of things we observe and of which we know with certainty they exist, such as the rising of flesh in the cupping-glass when sucking, for when the air is drawn out by suction, the flesh follows it so that vacuum does not occur; and the rising of water in the pipe when one immerses its one end [and sucks] the other; and the staying of water in the jug which has a narrow hole at its bottom, without descending from the hole when the head of the jug is stopped, so that no space remains empty, and its descending according to its natural inclination when the head is opened for air to enter; and the breaking of the flask which has a wooden stick inserted in its head and tied such that no air enters or exits, then the stick is pulled out, then the flask breaks inward so that the space of the stick does not remain empty, and if it is inserted, the flask breaks outward, because it contains a plenum that does not accommodate the stick. It was answered that it is possible that this be due to other causes, for the utmost of these matters is that they are necessary consequences of the non-existence of vacuum, but the necessary consequence may be more general [than the cause], so its existence does not prove the existence of the implicans. Yes: perhaps it provides intuitive certainty for the observer, but it does not constitute a proof against the disputant.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth and most detailed empirical argument against vacuum, citing observable phenomena that seem to require the impossibility of vacuum (e.g., cupping, siphons, jug, flask). The refutation is twofold: first, these phenomena could have other causes; second, even if they are necessary consequences of the non-existence of vacuum, the logical relationship is that the consequent may be broader than the antecedent, so the existence of the consequent does not guarantee the antecedent. The added remark distinguishes between intuitive certainty for the observer and a rigorous proof for debate—the phenomena may convince an individual but do not constitute a logical demonstration against an opponent who denies the impossibility of vacuum.

Key Terms
محجمة
أنبوبة
كوز
قارورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It immediately descends through the hole.
B) It stays and does not descend from the hole.
C) It rises to the top of the jug.
D) It evaporates.

Key Takeaway
Several everyday phenomena appear to depend on the impossibility of vacuum as an explanatory principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author uses everyday observations to argue for a metaphysical truth. Is there a belief you hold about Allah or the unseen that you have never questioned because it seems 'obvious' from your experience? What would it take for you to examine that belief with the same rigor as these phenomena?
?

Have you ever acted in a certain way simply because you assumed the alternative was impossible, without testing it? What might happen if you tested that assumption today?

Refutation of Empirical Arguments for Vacuum
Al-Taftazani's refutation of the empirical proofs for the impossibility of vacuum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the empirical phenomena do not conclusively prove the impossibility of vacuum.

Core Idea
Observed consequences do not force a single cause; logical implication is not reversible.

After presenting the empirical phenomena, Al-Taftazani responds with a twofold critique: these phenomena could have other causes, and even if they are necessary consequences of the absence of vacuum, that does not prove the absence itself. He also distinguishes between intuitive certainty and logical proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on the logical argument.
Notice the phrase 'it was answered' indicating a response.
2
Understand the first point: other causes
The phenomena may be due to other causes, not necessarily the impossibility of vacuum. The same effect can have different causes.
3
Understand the second point: logical generality
Even if the phenomena are necessary consequences (lazim) of the non-existence of vacuum, the lazim may be more general than the malzum, so its existence does not prove the malzum.
This is a key logical principle: the consequent can be broader.
4
Distinguish between certainty and proof
The phenomena may provide intuitive certainty (yaqin hadsi) to an observer, but not a rigorous proof (hujja) against a disputant.

The Fourth Argument against the Void and Its Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the fourth argument against the void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against the void, the observed phenomena used to support it, and why al-Taftazani argues it is not decisive proof.

Core Idea
Observed phenomena do not prove the impossibility of a void; they may have other causes.

Al-Taftazani continues defending the possibility of a void by first clarifying that an earlier objection only negates a pure void between earth and sky, not any void. He then presents the fourth argument against the void, which relies on everyday observations like cupping and water in tubes. The author refutes this argument by showing that the observed effects are necessary concomitants of the void's absence, but the concomitant may be more general, so the inference is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without translating in your head. Notice the flow: an answer to an objection, then 'the fourth aspect' with examples, then an answer to that, then a final remark.
The text contains arguments and counter-arguments; try to identify each part.
2
Identify the structure: objection, response, argument, refutation
The passage begins with a response to a prior objection, then presents a new argument (the fourth) with several examples, then gives a refutation using logical terminology (lāzim/malzūm), and ends with an epistemic note.
This is a typical kalam dialectical structure.
3
Examine the examples of the fourth argument
The fourth argument lists observable phenomena: flesh rising in a cupping glass, water rising in a tube, water remaining in a jug, and a flask breaking. These are said to show that nature abhors a void. Analyze how each example supposedly relies on the impossibility of a void.
These examples are meant to be intuitive and empirical.
4
Understand the refutation using lāzim and malzūm
The refutation points out that the observed effects are only necessary consequences of the negation of the void, but the consequence (lāzim) may be more general than the antecedent (malzūm). Thus, the existence of these effects does not prove the impossibility of a void.
This is a logical fallacy: affirming the consequent.
5
Grasp the final distinction: ḥadsī certainty vs. formal proof
The author concedes that such arguments might give intuitive certainty (ḥadsī) to a pondering individual, but they do not constitute a rigorous proof (ḥujja) against an opponent in debate.
This shows the importance of method in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب بأنه مع ابتنائه على تقدير نفي الفاعل المختار، إنما ينفي كون ما بين الأرض والسماء خلاء صرفاً، ولا ينفي وجود خلاء خارج عما بينهما أو مختلط بالأجزاء الهوائية .
الوجه الرابع : أن لو وجد الخلاء لزم انتفاء أمور نشاهدها، ونحكم بوجودها قطعاً كارتفاع اللحم في المحجمة عند المص، فإنه لما انجذب الهواء بالمص تبعه اللحم لئلا يلزم الخلاء، وكارتفاع الماء في الأنبوبة، إذا غمس أحد طرفيها الطرف الآخر، وكبقاء الماء في الكوز، الذي في أسفله ثقبة ضيقة، من غير أن ينزل من الثقبة عند سد رأس الكوز، لئلا يبقى حيز خالياً، ونزوله على ما هو مقتضى طبعه عند فتح الرأس لدخول الهواء، وكانكسار القارورة التي جعلت في رأسها خشبة وشدت بحيث لا يدخل فيها ولا يخرج عنها هواء، ثم أخرجت الخشبة، فإن القارورة تنكسر إلى الداخل لئلا يبقى حيز الخشبة خالياً، وإذا دخلت تنكسر القارورة إلى الخارج، لما أن فيها ملاء لا يجامع الخشبة، وأجيب بأنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك لأسباب أخر، فإن غاية هذه الأمور لزومها لانتفاء الخلاء، واللازم قد يكون أعم فلا يصح بوجوده على وجود الملزوم .
نعم : ربما يفيد يقيناً حدسياً للناظر لكن لا يقوم به حجة على المناظر.
And it is answered that, while it is based on the supposition of negating the free agent, it only negates that what is between the earth and sky is a pure void, and does not negate the existence of a void outside what is between them or mixed with aerial parts.
The fourth aspect: that if the void existed, then the negation of things that we observe and judge to exist with certainty would follow, such as the rising of flesh in the cupping glass during suction, for when the air is drawn by suction the flesh follows it so that a void does not occur, and such as the rising of water in a tube when one of its ends is immersed in the other, and such as the remaining of water in a jug which has a narrow hole in its bottom, without descending from the hole when the top of the jug is closed, so that a space does not remain empty, and its descending according to its nature when the top is opened for the entry of air, and such as the breaking of a flask in whose mouth a piece of wood was placed and tightened such that no air enters it or exits from it, then the wood is removed, for the flask breaks inward so that the space of the wood does not remain empty, and if it enters, the flask breaks outward, because there is a fullness in it that does not accommodate the wood. And it is answered that it is possible that this is due to other causes, for the utmost of these matters is their necessary connection to the negation of the void, and the necessary (al-lāzim) may be more general, so it is not valid to infer the existence of the necessary condition (al-malzūm) from its existence.
Yes: perhaps it provides certain knowledge intuitively (ḥadsī) for the observer, but it does not constitute a proof against the opponent.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage contains a refutation of arguments for the existence of a void (al-khalāʾ). It begins by responding to an earlier objection: the argument based on denying a free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) only negates a pure void between earth and sky, not other voids. Then the author presents a fourth argument against the void: observed phenomena like flesh rising in a cupping glass, water in a tube, water staying in a jug, and a flask breaking when a wooden rod is removed—all these supposedly show that nature abhors a void. The answer to this is that these phenomena could have other causes; the observed effects are only necessary concomitants of the negation of the void, but the concomitant may be more general than the thing inferred, so one cannot conclude the existence of the void from them. Finally, the author notes that such arguments might yield intuitive certainty (ḥadsī) for the observer, but they do not constitute decisive proof (ḥujja) against an opponent in debate.

Key Terms
الخلاء (al-khalāʾ)
الفاعل المختار (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār)
الوجه الرابع (al-wajh al-rābiʿ)
المحجمة (al-miḥjama)
الأنبوبة (al-ʾunbūba)
الكوز (al-kūz)
القارورة (al-qārūra)
اللازم (al-lāzim)
الملزوم (al-malzūm)
حدسي (ḥadsī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The observed phenomena (e.g., water rising in a tube) are direct proof that a void cannot exist.
Correction: The author argues that these phenomena are necessary effects of the negation of the void but could also be caused by other factors; therefore, they do not constitute decisive proof.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main weakness of the fourth argument (al-wajh al-rābiʿ) against the void?
A) The examples are not real phenomena.
B) The observed effects could be due to other causes, and the logical connection is not certain.
C) The void does not exist anywhere.
D) The argument only works if one believes in a free agent.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument against the void fails because observable effects do not uniquely imply the impossibility of a void; they may have alternative explanations and thus do not constitute proof in theological debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been absolutely certain about something based on observed evidence, only to later realize other causes were possible? What does this lesson teach you about the limits of your own knowledge?
?
The author says the fourth argument may provide intuitive certainty for the observer but not proof against the opponent. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold with intuitive certainty that you cannot prove to others? How does that affect your interactions?
?
Reflect on a time you used a 'necessary consequent' argument to justify a position. Did you consider alternative causes? What does this lesson call you to change in how you argue?

The Four Primary Tangible Qualities
Al-Sanusi enumerates the four primary tangible qualities and refutes the notion that cold is the absence of heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the four primary tangible qualities—heat, cold, wetness, dryness—are essential properties of elemental bodies and that cold is a positive quality, not merely the absence of heat.

Core Idea
Cold is a real quality, not the absence of heat.

Al-Sanusi begins by identifying the four fundamental tangible qualities that every elemental body possesses. These qualities are the first to be perceived by the senses. He then addresses a common misconception that cold is simply the absence of heat, affirming that cold has its own real existence and essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the four qualities
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice how the author defines the four qualities and immediately refutes a misconception.
The author uses a very concise style; don't get stuck on every word.
2
Identify the four qualities and their significance
The text says these are 'usul al-kayfiyyat al-malmusa' – the origins of tangible qualities. Note that no elemental body is free from them.
These four are the primary ones; all other tangible qualities derive from them.
3
Understand the refutation of cold as absence
The author explicitly says that the claim that cold is the absence of heat is 'nothing'. He insists that cold has its own quiddity (mahiyya).
This is a key doctrinal point in kalam: qualities are real, not privations.
4
Reflect on the role of definitions
The author states that definitions only point to properties, not the essence. This shows the limitation of conceptual knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصول الكيفيات الملموسة، أي التي لا يخلو عنها شيء من الأجسام العنصرية، ويقع للإحساس بها أولا بين الحرارة والبرودة، والرطوبة واليبوسة. ولا خفاء في وجودها فما يقال : إن البرودة عدم الحرارة ليس بشيء ولا في ماهيتها، فما يذكر في معرض التعريف لها تنبيه على بعض مالها من الخواص لا إفادة لتصوراتها والمشهور من خواص الحرارة هي التحريك إلى فوق على ما قال في الحدود: الحرارة كيفية فعلية بحركة لما تكون فيه إلى فوق لإحداثها الخفة فيعرض أن تجمع المتجانسات، وتفرق المختلفات وتحدث بتخليها الكشف تخلخلاً من باب الكليف أي رقـه قوام ومقابله التكاثف بمعنى غلظ القوام، وبتصعيدها اللطيف تكاثفاً من باب الوضع أي اجتماعاً للأجزاء الوجدانية الطبع لخروج الجسم الغريب عما بينها أو يقابله التخلخل بمعنى انتقاش الأجزاء بحيث يخالطها جرم غريب، ومعنى الفعلية ما سبق من جعل الغير شبيهاً لا مجرد إفادة أثر ما أعم من الحركة وغيرها، ليكون قولنا فعلية محركة، بمنزلة قولنا : جسم حيوان، على ما زعم الإمام. وبالجملة فالخاصة الأولية للحرارة هي إحداث الخفة والميل المصعد، ثم يترتب على ذلك بحسب اختلاف القوابل آثار مختلفة من الجمع والتفريق والتنجير وغير ذلك. وتحقيقه : أن ما يتأثر عن الحرارة فإن كان بسيطاً استحال أولاً في الكيف ثم أفضى به ذلك إلى انقلاب الجوهر فيصير الماء هواء، والهواء ناراً، وربما يلزمه تفريق المتشاكلات بأن يميز الأجزاء الهوائية من الماء، ويتبعها ما يخالطها من الأجزاء الصغار المائية، وإن كان مركباً فإن لم يشتد التحام بسائطه ولا خفاء في أن الألطف أقبل للصعود لزم تفريق الأجزاء المختلفة وتبعه انضمام كل إلى ما يشاكله بمقتضى الطبيعة، وهو معنى جمع المتشاكلات وإن اشتد التحام البسائط، فإن كان اللطيف والكثيف قريبين من الاعتدال حدثت من الحرارة القوية حركة دورية لأنها كلما مال اللطيف إلى التصعد جذبه الكثيف إلى الانحدار وإلا فإن كان الغالب هو اللطيف يصعد بالكلية كالنوشادر، وإن كان هو الكثيف فإن لم يكن غالباً جداً حدث تسييل كما في الرصاص، أو تليين كما في الحديد، وإن كان غالباً جداً كما في الطلق حدث مجرد سخونة، واحتيج في تليينه إلى الاستعانة بأعمال أخر، وعدم حصول التصعد أو التفرق بناء على المانع، لا ينافي كون خاصتها التصعد وتفريق المختلفات المتشاكلات.
The origins of the tangible qualities, i.e., those from which no elemental body is free, and which are first perceived by sense, are heat, cold, wetness, and dryness. There is no obscurity in their existence; so what is said that cold is the absence of heat is nothing, nor in their quiddity. What is mentioned in the context of defining them is a reminder of some of their properties, not a benefit for their conceptualizations. The well-known among the properties of heat is that it causes motion upward, as is said in the Hudud: Heat is an active quality (kayfiyya fi‘liyya) with motion of that in which it exists upward, because it produces lightness, so it happens that it brings together similars, separates dissimilars, and by its causing [?] rarefaction (takhalkhul) from the category of [?] i.e., fineness of consistency, and its opposite is condensation (takathuf) meaning thickness of consistency, and by its sublimating the subtle, condensation from the category of placement (wad‘), i.e., gathering of essential parts by nature due to the exit of the foreign body from what is between them, or its opposite is rarefaction meaning expansion of parts so that a foreign body mixes with them. The meaning of 'active' is what preceded: making another similar, not merely producing some effect, whether motion or otherwise, so that our saying 'an active mover' is like our saying 'an animal body', according to what the Imam claimed. In summary, the primary property of heat is producing lightness and upward inclination; then depending on the differences of recipients, various effects follow from it, such as uniting, separating, solidifying, and others. Its verification: that which is affected by heat — if it is simple, it first changes in quality, then that leads to transformation of substance, so water becomes air, air becomes fire, and perhaps it necessitates separating similars by distinguishing the airy parts from water, and what mixes with them of small watery parts follows; if it is compound and the cohesion of its simples is not strong, and there is no doubt that the subtler is more receptive to ascent, then separation of different parts necessarily occurs, followed by each joining its like by natural necessity, and that is the meaning of gathering similars; if the cohesion of the simples is strong, then if the subtle and the dense are close to balance, a strong heat produces a circular motion because whenever the subtle tends to ascend, the dense pulls it down; otherwise, if the subtle is predominant, it ascends entirely like sal ammoniac; if the dense is predominant but not very much, liquefaction occurs like in lead, or softening like in iron; if it is very predominant like in talc, mere heating occurs, and aid from other operations is needed for its softening. The non-occurrence of ascent or separation due to an obstruction does not contradict its property being ascent and separation of dissimilars and similars.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the four primary tangible qualities (al-kayfiyyat al-malmusa) and an in-depth analysis of the properties of heat. The author begins by stating that these qualities—heat, cold, wetness, and dryness—are the first perceptible qualities that no elemental body lacks. He then dismisses the notion that cold is merely the absence of heat, asserting that these qualities have real existence and distinct quiddities. Definitions of them serve only to highlight some of their properties, not to convey their essences. 

The focus then shifts to heat. The author cites a definition from 'al-Hudud': heat is an active quality (kayfiyya fi‘liyya) that causes upward motion by producing lightness. This leads to effects like bringing similar things together (jam‘ al-mutajanisat) and separating dissimilar ones (tafriq al-mukhtalifat), and also causes rarefaction and condensation depending on the context. The term 'active' (fi‘liyya) is explained as making another similar to itself, not just producing any effect. 

Moving to a more detailed verification (tahqiq), the author explains the effects of heat on different kinds of bodies. For simple bodies, heat first changes their quality and then transforms their substance (e.g., water to air, air to fire), and may separate similar parts. For compound bodies, if the components are loosely bound, heat separates them and then each component joins its like. If the components are tightly bound, the outcome depends on the balance of subtle and dense: if balanced, circular motion results; if subtle predominates, complete ascent; if dense but not too dense, liquefaction or softening; if very dense, only heating occurs, requiring external aid. The author concludes that the non-occurrence of ascent or separation due to an obstruction does not negate that heat's essential property is ascent and separation of dissimilar and similar things.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think cold is simply the absence of heat.
Correction: The author asserts that cold is a real, positive quality with its own existence and quiddity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about the four primary tangible qualities?
A) They are only heat and cold.
B) They are heat, cold, wetness, and dryness.
C) They are not found in all elemental bodies.
D) Cold is the absence of heat.

Key Takeaway
The four primary tangible qualities—heat, cold, wetness, dryness—are real, essential properties of all elemental bodies, and cold is not the absence of heat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that cold is a real quality, not merely the absence of heat. In your daily life, do you tend to treat some negative experiences (like loneliness or sadness) as mere privations of good things? How would it change your spiritual state to see them as real, created entities that God uses to teach you? Commit to thanking Allah for a difficult experience this week as a real sign.
?
The author says definitions only point to properties, not essences. When you describe your faith to others, do you rely on simplistic definitions? How can you instead point to the properties of faith through your actions? This week, use one specific action to show what faith means rather than reciting a definition.
?
This passage teaches that four qualities are fundamental to all material things. How often do you reflect on the fact that every physical object you interact with is a sign of Allah's power, containing these realities? Choose one physical object you use daily and resolve to think of its qualities as Allah's creation each time you touch it.

Heat as an Active Quality and Its Primary Property
Al-Sanusi defines heat as an active quality and explains its primary property of upward motion and resultant effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of heat as an active quality and its primary property of causing upward motion and lightness.

Core Idea
Heat is an active quality that makes things rise and separates dissimilar things.

After establishing the four qualities, al-Sanusi focuses on heat, the most dynamic of them. He cites a definition from the book al-Hudud, describing heat as an active quality that moves the body upward by producing lightness. This primary property leads to other effects such as gathering similars and separating dissimilars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on heat's properties
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the definition of heat from al-Hudud and the explanation of 'active quality'.
The author is drawing from earlier authorities.
2
Understand 'active quality' (kayfiyya fi‘liyya)
The author explains that 'active' means making another similar to itself, not just producing any effect. This is a technical term in kalam physics.
Think of fire making something else fire-like.
3
Identify the effects of heat
Heat causes upward motion, rarefaction, condensation, gathering similars, and separating dissimilars. List them from the text.
These effects are secondary to the primary property.
4
Consider the analogy with 'animal body'
The author says saying 'active mover' is like saying 'animal body' – a qualification that distinguishes it from other types.

How Heat Transforms Simple and Compound Bodies
Al-Sanusi verifies the effects of heat on simple and compound bodies, detailing various outcomes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how heat affects simple bodies (changing substance) and compound bodies (various outcomes based on cohesion and balance).

Core Idea
Heat's effect depends on the body's composition, but its essential property remains ascent and separation.

Having established heat's primary property, al-Sanusi now provides a detailed verification (tahqiq) of how heat affects different bodies. He distinguishes between simple and compound bodies, and within compounds, various scenarios based on the cohesion and balance of components. This analysis shows the range of heat's effects while affirming its fundamental nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the tahqiq section
This is a longer, more detailed passage. Read it carefully, following the classification of bodies.
The author uses a cause-and-effect structure.
2
Understand the effect on simple bodies
Heat first changes the quality of a simple body, then transforms its substance (e.g., water to air, air to fire). It may also separate similar parts.
Simple bodies here mean homogenous ones like water.
3
Analyze compound bodies with loose cohesion
If the components are not tightly bound, heat separates the different parts, and each joins its like. This is gathering similars.
4
Analyze compound bodies with strong cohesion
When components are tightly bound, the outcome depends on the balance of subtle and dense: if balanced, circular motion; if subtle dominates, ascent; if dense dominates, liquefaction/softening or just heating.
The author gives examples like lead, iron, talc.
5
Note the conclusion about obstruction
Even if ascent or separation doesn't occur due to an obstacle, it doesn't negate heat's essential property.
This reinforces that properties are real even if not always manifest.

أصول الكيفيات الملموسة، أي التي لا يخلو عنها شيء من الأجسام العنصرية، ويقع للإحساس بها أولا بين الحرارة والبرودة، والرطوبة واليبوسة. ولا خفاء في وجودها فما يقال : إن البرودة عدم الحرارة ليس بشيء ولا في ماهيتها، فما يذكر في معرض التعريف لها تنبيه على بعض مالها من الخواص لا إفادة لتصوراتها والمشهور من خواص الحرارة هي التحريك إلى فوق على ما قال في الحدود: الحرارة كيفية فعلية بحركة لما تكون فيه إلى فوق لإحداثها الخفة فيعرض أن تجمع المتجانسات، وتفرق المختلفات وتحدث بتخليها الكشف تخلخلاً من باب الكليف أي رقـه قوام ومقابله التكاثف بمعنى غلظ القوام، وبتصعيدها اللطيف تكاثفاً من باب الوضع أي اجتماعاً للأجزاء الوجدانية الطبع لخروج الجسم الغريب عما بينها أو يقابله التخلخل بمعنى انتقاش الأجزاء بحيث يخالطها جرم غريب، ومعنى الفعلية ما سبق من جعل الغير شبيهاً لا مجرد إفادة أثر ما أعم من الحركة وغيرها، ليكون قولنا فعلية محركة، بمنزلة قولنا : جسم حيوان، على ما زعم الإمام. وبالجملة فالخاصة الأولية للحرارة هي إحداث الخفة والميل المصعد، ثم يترتب على ذلك بحسب اختلاف القوابل آثار مختلفة من الجمع والتفريق والتنجير وغير ذلك. وتحقيقه : أن ما يتأثر عن الحرارة فإن كان بسيطاً استحال أولاً في الكيف ثم أفضى به ذلك إلى انقلاب الجوهر فيصير الماء هواء، والهواء ناراً، وربما يلزمه تفريق المتشاكلات بأن يميز الأجزاء الهوائية من الماء، ويتبعها ما يخالطها من الأجزاء الصغار المائية، وإن كان مركباً فإن لم يشتد التحام بسائطه ولا خفاء في أن الألطف أقبل للصعود لزم تفريق الأجزاء المختلفة وتبعه انضمام كل إلى ما يشاكله بمقتضى الطبيعة، وهو معنى جمع المتشاكلات وإن اشتد التحام البسائط، فإن كان اللطيف والكثيف قريبين من الاعتدال حدثت من الحرارة القوية حركة دورية لأنها كلما مال اللطيف إلى التصعد جذبه الكثيف إلى الانحدار وإلا فإن كان الغالب هو اللطيف يصعد بالكلية كالنوشادر، وإن كان هو الكثيف فإن لم يكن غالباً جداً حدث تسييل كما في الرصاص، أو تليين كما في الحديد، وإن كان غالباً جداً كما في الطلق حدث مجرد سخونة، واحتيج في تليينه إلى الاستعانة بأعمال أخر، وعدم حصول التصعد أو التفرق بناء على المانع، لا ينافي كون خاصتها التصعد وتفريق المختلفات المتشاكلات.
The origins of the tangible qualities, i.e., those from which no elemental body is free, and which are first perceived by sense, are heat, cold, wetness, and dryness. There is no obscurity in their existence; so what is said that cold is the absence of heat is nothing, nor in their quiddity. What is mentioned in the context of defining them is a reminder of some of their properties, not a benefit for their conceptualizations. The well-known among the properties of heat is that it causes motion upward, as is said in the Hudud: Heat is an active quality (kayfiyya fi‘liyya) with motion of that in which it exists upward, because it produces lightness, so it happens that it brings together similars, separates dissimilars, and by its causing [?] rarefaction (takhalkhul) from the category of [?] i.e., fineness of consistency, and its opposite is condensation (takathuf) meaning thickness of consistency, and by its sublimating the subtle, condensation from the category of placement (wad‘), i.e., gathering of essential parts by nature due to the exit of the foreign body from what is between them, or its opposite is rarefaction meaning expansion of parts so that a foreign body mixes with them. The meaning of 'active' is what preceded: making another similar, not merely producing some effect, whether motion or otherwise, so that our saying 'an active mover' is like our saying 'an animal body', according to what the Imam claimed. In summary, the primary property of heat is producing lightness and upward inclination; then depending on the differences of recipients, various effects follow from it, such as uniting, separating, solidifying, and others. Its verification: that which is affected by heat — if it is simple, it first changes in quality, then that leads to transformation of substance, so water becomes air, air becomes fire, and perhaps it necessitates separating similars by distinguishing the airy parts from water, and what mixes with them of small watery parts follows; if it is compound and the cohesion of its simples is not strong, and there is no doubt that the subtler is more receptive to ascent, then separation of different parts necessarily occurs, followed by each joining its like by natural necessity, and that is the meaning of gathering similars; if the cohesion of the simples is strong, then if the subtle and the dense are close to balance, a strong heat produces a circular motion because whenever the subtle tends to ascend, the dense pulls it down; otherwise, if the subtle is predominant, it ascends entirely like sal ammoniac; if the dense is predominant but not very much, liquefaction occurs like in lead, or softening like in iron; if it is very predominant like in talc, mere heating occurs, and aid from other operations is needed for its softening. The non-occurrence of ascent or separation due to an obstruction does not contradict its property being ascent and separation of dissimilars and similars.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the four primary tangible qualities (al-kayfiyyat al-malmusa) and an in-depth analysis of the properties of heat. The author begins by stating that these qualities—heat, cold, wetness, and dryness—are the first perceptible qualities that no elemental body lacks. He then dismisses the notion that cold is merely the absence of heat, asserting that these qualities have real existence and distinct quiddities. Definitions of them serve only to highlight some of their properties, not to convey their essences. 

The focus then shifts to heat. The author cites a definition from 'al-Hudud': heat is an active quality (kayfiyya fi‘liyya) that causes upward motion by producing lightness. This leads to effects like bringing similar things together (jam‘ al-mutajanisat) and separating dissimilar ones (tafriq al-mukhtalifat), and also causes rarefaction and condensation depending on the context. The term 'active' (fi‘liyya) is explained as making another similar to itself, not just producing any effect. 

Moving to a more detailed verification (tahqiq), the author explains the effects of heat on different kinds of bodies. For simple bodies, heat first changes their quality and then transforms their substance (e.g., water to air, air to fire), and may separate similar parts. For compound bodies, if the components are loosely bound, heat separates them and then each component joins its like. If the components are tightly bound, the outcome depends on the balance of subtle and dense: if balanced, circular motion results; if subtle predominates, complete ascent; if dense but not too dense, liquefaction or softening; if very dense, only heating occurs, requiring external aid. The author concludes that the non-occurrence of ascent or separation due to an obstruction does not negate that heat's essential property is ascent and separation of dissimilar and similar things.

Key Takeaway
Heat's effects vary widely depending on the type of body, but its essential property of causing ascent and separation remains constant.

Types of Hot Things and the Innate Heat
Author classifies things that produce heat and reports opinions on the nature of innate heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam scholars classify 'hot' things and the different opinions about the nature of innate heat that sustains life.

Core Idea
Heat is not just fire—scholars debated its types and the source of life-giving warmth.

This passage from al-Maqasid presents a concise classification of what we call 'hot' and then turns to a more profound question: what is the innate heat that keeps us alive? The author reports three conflicting views, showing that this was a live debate in Islamic theology. Understanding these distinctions is essential for grasping later arguments about life, soul, and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the two distinct parts: a classification of 'hot' things and a discussion of innate heat.
Pay attention to the phrasing 'وقيل' which indicates reported opinions.
2
Identify the two categories of hot things
Find where the author divides things that produce heat. One category requires contact with the body (like foods and medicines), the other does not (like smells). Ask yourself why this distinction matters.
3
List the three opinions on innate heat
After the classification, the author mentions three views on the nature of innate heat (al-harara al-ghariziyya): fiery, celestial, or different in essence. Write them down and notice the phrase 'اختلاف الآثار' (difference of effects).
The last opinion seems to argue against the first two based on effects.
4
Reflect on the contested nature
Consider why scholars would debate the nature of innate heat. The passage hints that its effects are different—such as repelling itself? This suggests it cannot be ordinary fire or celestial heat.
The text ends abruptly; the point is that innate heat has unique properties.
5
Connect to broader kalam
How does this relate to arguments about the soul or creation? Debates about heat and elements often underlie discussions of life and the divine attributes. This passage is a building block.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وقد يقال الحار لما يحدث حرارة إما بشرط ملاقاة البدن كالأغذية والأدوية . أولاً : كالمشمومات . وأما الحرارة الغريزية التي بها قوام الحياة فقيل نارية، وقيل: سماوية، وقيل مخالفة لهما بالحقيقة لاختلاف الآثار حتى إنها تدفع الحرارة الغريزية .
He said: It may be said 'hot' for that which produces heat, either conditionally on contact with the body, like foods and medicines, or not, like things smelled. As for the innate heat (al-harara al-ghariziyya) by which life subsists, it is said to be fiery, and said to be celestial, and said to be different from both in reality due to difference of effects, so that it repels the innate heat.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a classification and discussion. It begins by distinguishing two types of things that are called 'hot' (al-harr) based on how they produce heat: those that require direct contact with the body, such as foods and medicines, and those that do not, such as things that are smelled (e.g., pungent scents). Then the author turns to the concept of innate heat (al-harara al-ghariziyya), which is the heat that sustains life. He reports three opinions about its nature: some say it is fiery (nariyya), some say it is celestial (samawiyya), and others say it is different in essence from both, because its effects are distinct—for instance, it repels (or perhaps 'dispels') the innate heat? The text ends somewhat abruptly, but the point is that innate heat has unique properties that set it apart from ordinary elemental heat.
