The Imam's Answer on Heat Resistance
Explanation of heat resistance and the Imam's answer regarding natural heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of natural heat in resisting external temperatures and the Imam's explanation for this resistance.

Core Idea
Heat resistance depends on context, composition, and how heat interacts with the body.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid examines a common objection: if natural heat is hot, how does it resist incoming heat? The Imam’s answer reveals that resistance is not simply a matter of temperature but of how heat operates within a balanced structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the distinction between natural heat (حرارة غريزية) and strange heat (حرارة غريبة).
2
Identify the two types of heat
The passage presents natural heat as an instrument of nature and strange heat as an external force. Find how each behaves.
3
Understand the Imam's mechanism
The Imam explains that resistance comes from natural heat's ability to separate parts, gained through maturation and cooking, similar to strange heat's action.
This answers the objection about how hot resists hot.
4
Consider the basis of effects
The specific effects of heat depend on its occurrence in a balanced body or its becoming part of a special temperament. Reflect on why this matters.
This shows that causality is context-dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحرارة الغريزية، فإنها آلة للطبيعة تدفع ضرر الحار الوارد بتحريك الروح إلى دفعه وضرر البارد الوارد بالمضادة . وأجاب الإمام : بأن تلك المقاومة إنما هي من جهة أن الحرارة الغريبة تحاول التفريق، والغريزية أفادت من النضج والطبخ ما يتعبر عنده كالحرارة الغريبة تفريق تلك الأجزاء، وبالجملة يجوز أن تكون هي الحرارة السماوية أو النارية، ومستند آثاره المختصة بها إلى خصوصية حصولها في البدن المعتدل، أو صيرورتها جزءاً من المزاج الخاص.
The natural heat, for it is an instrument of nature driving away the harm of incoming heat by moving the spirit to repel it, and the harm of incoming cold by opposition. And the Imam answered: that this resistance is only due to the fact that the strange heat tries to separate, and the natural heat has gained from maturation and cooking what is considered, according to him, like the strange heat separates those parts. In short, it is possible that it is the celestial heat or the fiery heat, and the basis of its specific effects is due to the particularity of its occurrence in the balanced body, or its becoming part of the special temperament.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of natural heat and its role in repelling external heat and cold. It then presents the Imam's answer to an objection about resistance. The Imam argues that the resistance is due to the natural heat's ability to separate parts, gained from maturation and cooking, similar to the action of strange heat. He concludes that this heat could be celestial or fiery, and its specific effects depend on its occurrence in a balanced body or becoming part of a special temperament.

Correction: The Imam shows that resistance depends on composition and context: natural heat's ability to separate parts, gained from maturation, and its specific effects based on the body's balance or temperament.

Objections to Defining Wetness
Objections against definitions of wetness as adhesion and ease of shape, with answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections against defining wetness by adhesion and ease of shape, and how classical kalam defends these definitions.

Core Idea
Wetness is not just adhesion; it requires ease of separation.

This passage examines two objections to the definitions of wetness in kalam. The first objection argues that honey is more adhesive than water, but not wetter, challenging the definition by adhesion. The second objection argues that fire is easily shaped but not wet, challenging the definition by ease of shape. The text presents the answers that refine these definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the objections are presented and answered.
The passage is dense with technical terms.
2
Identify the first objection and its answer
Find the objection about honey and the definition of wetness as adhesion. Then see how the answer clarifies that ease of adhesion is meant, but also with ease of separation.
This shows that wetness involves both stickiness and ease of letting go.
3
Identify the second objection and its answer
Find the objection about fire and the definition of wetness as ease of shape. Note the answer: simple fire does not have ease of shape, and softness is defined separately.
4
Reflect on softness and hardness
The passage ends with a note on softness and hardness—whether they are tangible qualities or aptitudes. Consider why this matters for the definition of wetness.
This adds nuance to the discussion of wetness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأورد على اعتبار الالتصاق أنه يوجب كون العسل أرطب من الماء فدفع بأن المراد سهولة الالتصاق، بل مع سهولة الانفصال، وعلى اعتبار سهولة التشكل أنه يوجب رطوبة النار ، وكون اللين في الرطوبة . وأجيب بمنع سهولة التشكل في النار البسيطة، وبأن اللين كيفية تقتضي قبول الغمز إلى الباطن مع عسر تفرق الأجزاء، وفي كون اللين والصلابة من الملموسات، أو الاستعدادات تردد .
He said: And it was objected against considering adhesion that it necessitates that honey is wetter than water. So he repelled by saying that what is meant is ease of adhesion, even with ease of separation. And considering ease of shape, it necessitates the wetness of fire, and that softness is in wetness. And it was answered by denying ease of shape in simple fire, and that softness is a quality requiring acceptance of pressing inward with difficulty of separation of parts, and there is hesitation about softness and hardness being among the tangible qualities or aptitudes.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against definitions of wetness (al-rutuba). The first objection is against defining wetness as adhesion, because honey is more adhesive than water but is not wetter. The answer clarifies that what is meant is ease of adhesion together with ease of separation. The second objection is against defining wetness as ease of shape, because fire is easily shaped but is not wet. The answer denies that simple fire has ease of shape, and discusses softness (al-layer) as a quality of accepting pressure with difficulty of separation. There is also hesitation about whether softness and hardness are tangible qualities or aptitudes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Honey is wetter than water because it is more adhesive.
Correction: Wetness requires ease of separation in addition to adhesion; honey does not have ease of separation relative to water.

Defining wetness requires considering both adhesion and ease of separation, and softness is a distinct quality.

Ibn Sina's Objection and Imam's Defense on Wetness
Ibn Sina's correction of the wetness definition and the Imam's defense of adhesion-based definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate between Ibn Sina and the Imam over the definition of wetness and how the Imam defends the adhesion-based definition against Ibn Sina's objection.

Core Idea
Wetness is about ease of adhesion and separation, not just adhesion alone.

This passage presents a critical exchange between Ibn Sina and the Imam regarding the correct definition of wetness. Ibn Sina objects to the earlier definition that equates wetness with adhesion, offering an alternative definition based on shape. The Imam responds by refining the definition to include both ease of adhesion and ease of separation, defending the original position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the literal translation to ensure clarity. Notice that Ibn Sina objects to a definition attributed to some predecessors.
Pay attention to the key terms: الرطوبة (wetness), الالتصاق (adhesion).
2
Identify Ibn Sina's objection and alternative definition
Ibn Sina argues that if adhesion defined wetness, honey would be wetter than water, which is false. He proposes that wetness is ease of accepting and leaving shape (سهولة قبول الشكل وتركه).
Understand his logic: wetter means more adhesive, but honey is more adhesive than water, yet not wetter, so adhesion cannot be the criterion.
3
Understand the Imam's response
The Imam redefines wetness as ease of adhesion (سهولة الالتصاق) which necessarily entails ease of separation (سهولة الانفصال). He argues that honey is not easier in separation, so it is not wetter. This defends the original definition by adding a necessary condition.
Notice how the Imam uses the honey example to show that longer adhesion does not imply wetness; only ease of adhesion and separation matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأورد المذكور في كلام بعض المتقدمين أن الجسم إنما كان رطباً إذا كان بحيث يلتصق بما يلامسه وفهم منه أن الرطوبة كيفية تقتضي التصاق الجسم، ورده ابن سينا بأن الالتصاق لو كان للرطوبة لكان الأشد رطوبة أشد التصاقاً، فيكون العسل أرطب من الماء، بل المعتبر في الرطوبة سهولة قبول الشكل وتركه فهي كيفية بها يكون الجسم سهل التشكل، وسهل الترك للشكل . وأجاب الإمام بأن المعتبر فيها سهولة الالتصاق ويلزمها بالغير سهولة الانفصال فهي كيفية بها يستعد الجسم بسهولة الالتصاق بالغير، وسهولة الانفصال عنه، ولا نسلم أن العسل أسهل ولا نسلم أن العسل أسهل التصاقاً من الماء، بل أدوم وأكثر ملازمة، ولا عبرة بذلك في الرطوبة، كيف وظاهر أنه ليس أسهل انفصالاً فيلزم أن لا يكون أسهل التصاقاً من الماء .
He said: And what is mentioned in the words of some predecessors was objected: that a body is only wet if it is such that it adheres to what touches it, and it was understood from this that wetness is a quality requiring adhesion of the body. Ibn Sina refuted it by saying that if adhesion were due to wetness, the wetter would be more adhesive, and thus honey would be wetter than water. Rather, what is considered in wetness is ease of accepting shape and leaving it, so it is a quality by which the body is easy to shape and easy to leave shape. And the Imam answered: what is considered in it is ease of adhesion, and it necessarily entails, by something else, ease of separation. So it is a quality by which the body is prepared for ease of adhesion with another and ease of separation from it. And we do not concede that honey is easier, and we do not concede that honey is easier in adhesion than water, rather it is more lasting and more adhering, and no regard is given to that in wetness. How is that? It is apparent that it is not easier in separation, so it follows that it is not easier in adhesion than water.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed objection by Ibn Sina against the earlier definition of wetness as adhesion. Ibn Sina argues that if adhesion were the criterion, honey would be wetter than water, which is false. He proposes instead that wetness is ease of shape acceptance and departure. The Imam responds by redefining wetness as ease of adhesion which necessarily entails ease of separation. He argues that honey may be more adhesive but not easier in separation, so it is not wetter. The Imam insists that honey's longer adhesion does not make it wetter.

Key Terms
الرطوبة
الالتصاق
سهولة الانفصال
ابن سينا
الإمام
سهولة قبول الشكل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina and the Imam agree on the definition of wetness.
Correction: They disagree: Ibn Sina proposes an alternative definition (ease of shape change), while the Imam defends and refines the original definition (ease of adhesion and separation).

Checkpoint
Why does the Imam argue that honey is not wetter than water?
A) Honey is not adhesive
B) Honey is not easier in separation
C) Honey has less water content
D) The definition does not apply to honey

Key Takeaway
The debate shows that defining wetness requires considering both ease of adhesion and ease of separation, not adhesion alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define yourself by a single characteristic (e.g., 'I am patient'), do you also consider its opposite (e.g., 'I also need ease of releasing frustration')? Where might such a one-sided definition lead you astray, as Ibn Sina's honey example showed? Will you ask Allah to help you see the full picture?
?
The Imam refined the definition of wetness by adding ease of separation. Is there a belief about yourself or others that you hold as absolute, but which actually needs refinement by considering a complementary truth? What step will you take today to adjust that view?
?
Ibn Sina's critique used a counterexample (honey) to expose a flaw. Think of a time you clung to a simplistic understanding. Did you resist correction like the predecessors might have, or did you allow a deeper truth to emerge? Will you commit to listening to counterexamples in your faith and character?

Objections on Defining Wetness as Ease of Adhesion and Shape Acceptance
Additional objections against definitions based on adhesion and shape acceptance, with answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections to defining wetness (rutuba) as ease of adhesion and ease of shape acceptance, and the responses given by the theologians.

Core Idea
Wetness is not simply ease of adhesion or shape acceptance; objections reveal deeper philosophical commitments.

The debate over the definition of wetness (rutuba) continues with specific objections raised by critics like Ibn Sina. The theologians defend their definition by clarifying that ease of adhesion and shape acceptance are not sufficient criteria when considered without context. This lesson examines two major objections: one concerning fine dry powder and another concerning fire, along with the theologians' responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two main objections
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Identify the two distinct objections: one about fine dry powder (ease of adhesion) and one about fire (ease of shape acceptance).
Notice the structure: each objection is followed by an answer.
2
Analyze the first objection about dry powder
The objection says that if wetness is ease of adhesion, then fine dry powder (like burnt bones) would be wet. The answer involves mixing with air, but only on a certain view of air's wetness.
The answer is conditional on the view of the Imam (who does not hold air as wet).
3
Analyze the second objection about fire
The objection says that fire, being the most subtle, would be wettest if wetness is ease of shape acceptance. The answer denies that pure fire easily accepts strange shapes; observed fire is mixed with air.
A further rebuttal about oven turning air into fire is answered by noting air intermingling persists.

And the intention of the Imam was to interpret their words with what was mentioned. Otherwise, Ibn Sina's objection is only about what he transmitted from their words, not about the interpretation of wetness as ease of adhesion and separation, as is suggested by the words of al-Mawaqif, and it is based on the fact that there is no mention in their words of separation at all, nor of ease on the side of adhesion, though what is mentioned of the entailment of ease of adhesion and ease of separation is denied. This passage contains additional objections and answers. Abu Ishaq's Atomism and Its Kalam Refutation
Abu Ishaq's atomism presented and refuted via divine omnipotence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Ishaq's theory that atoms are uniform and weight depends on quantity, and how the refutation challenges this by appealing to divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
Quantity alone does not determine weight; divine will can override natural patterns.

This lesson presents a classic kalam debate about the nature of atoms and weight. Abu Ishaq argues that all atoms are identical, so weight differences must come from the number of atoms. The refutation grants atomic uniformity but denies that quantity is the sole cause, invoking God's freedom to create heaviness or lightness directly. This demonstrates how kalam thinkers used divine omnipotence to counter naturalistic explanations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the two parts: Abu Ishaq's claim and the refutation.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بعد تسليم التجانس' (after conceding homogeneity).
2
Analyze Abu Ishaq's argument
Abu Ishaq says all individual atoms (jawahir farda) are homogeneous, so differences in heaviness and lightness are due to quantity. Understand this as a naturalistic explanation.
3
Understand the refutation's logic
The refutation concedes homogeneity but argues that God (al-Mukhtar) could create heaviness in a compound with few atoms and lightness in one with many. This shows that divine will can produce effects contrary to expected natural correlations.
The refutation uses the analogy of other accidents like colors and tastes, which also can vary independently of atomic quantity.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this argument matters in kalam: it defends the contingency of the universe and God's absolute freedom, against attempts to reduce physical phenomena to inherent properties of matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب الأستاذ أبو إسحاق إلى أن الجواهر الفردة متجانسة لا تتفاوت في الثقل والخفة، وإنما تفاوت الأجسام في ذلك عائد إلى كثرة الجواهر الفردة في الثقيل، وقلتها في الخفيف. ورد بعد تسليم التجانس بأنه يجوز أن يحدث في المركب من الأجزاء القليلة صفة الثقل، والكثيرة صفة الخفة لمحض إرادة المختار، أو بغيرها من الأسباب كسائر الأعراض من الألوان والطعوم وغيرها.
Abu Ishaq held that individual atoms (jawahir farda) are homogeneous, not differing in heaviness or lightness; rather, the differences in bodies are due to the abundance of atoms in the heavy and scarcity in the light. It was refuted, after conceding homogeneity, that it is possible for a compound of few parts to acquire the quality of heaviness, and many parts to acquire lightness, simply by the will of the Free Chooser (al-Mukhtar), or by other causes like other accidents such as colors, tastes, etc.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
Abu Ishaq al-Asfarayini argued that all atoms are equal, so heaviness/lightness must come from the number of atoms. The refutation grants the premise of homogeneity but denies the conclusion: heaviness/lightness could be produced by divine will or other causes, not necessarily by quantity. This is a classic kalam appeal to divine omnipotence.

Key Terms
أبو إسحاق
الجواهر الفردة
متجانسة
المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation denies the atomic theory entirely or claims atoms are not homogeneous.
Correction: The refutation explicitly concedes homogeneity (بعد تسليم التجانس); it only denies that quantity alone determines weight.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the refutation of Abu Ishaq's view?
A) Atoms are not homogeneous
B) Heaviness could be due to divine will or other causes
C) Quantity of atoms always determines weight
D) Atoms have different weights

Key Takeaway
In kalam, even plausible natural explanations like atomic quantity are subordinate to God's absolute freedom to create effects directly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the weight of an object, do you automatically assume a natural cause, or do you see it as a direct act of Allah? How would your daily perception change if you truly believed that every property is a creation of Allah moment by moment?
?
Abu Ishaq offered a plausible explanation based on quantity. The refutation did not deny its plausibility but placed divine will above it. In your own life, when you face a situation where natural causes seem inevitable—like a financial crisis or a health issue—do you resign to 'how things work,' or do you genuinely expect Allah to override those causes? What is one area where you need to trust Allah's ability to act contrary to the usual pattern?
?
The refutation uses colors and tastes as examples of accidents that are not tied to atomic quantity. Is there any fixed characteristic in your life—your personality, your circumstances, your habits—that you consider unchangeable? Will you allow Allah to change it without requiring a natural process?

The Waterskin Argument Against Both Theories of Heaviness
Presents a proof using a waterskin of mercury and water to disprove both theories of heaviness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single waterskin experiment disproves both the humidity-based and density-based theories of weight.

Core Idea
A simple waterskin proves both theories of heaviness wrong at once.

This passage presents a clever physical argument that simultaneously refutes two competing theories about why some objects are heavier than others. By comparing mercury and water in the same container, the author shows that neither humidity nor atomic density can explain the observed difference without leading to absurd consequences. The argument also introduces the hypothetical concept of empty space (khala') as a necessary condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the waterskin argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument begins by stating the falsehood of both views.
Pay attention to the conditional 'هذا بعد تسليم وجود الخلاء'.
2
Understand the experimental setup
The argument imagines filling a waterskin first with water, then with mercury. Mercury is heavier despite water being more humid.
This challenges the humidity theory.
3
See the logical implication
If mercury were heavier due to more parts, then the water must have empty spaces. The argument then addresses possible objections.
This challenges the density theory.
4
Analyze the rebuttal
The author anticipates an objection that empty spaces might be too small to sense, due to mixing with material parts.
The argument remains conditional on accepting emptiness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يستدل على بطلان الرأيين بأن الزق الواحد يسع من الزئبق أضعاف ما يسع من الماء، فالزئبق أثقل من الماء بكثير، مع زيادة الماء في الرطوبة بالاتفاق، وتساويهما في الأجزاء في الصورة المفروضة وهي أن تملأ الزق ماء ثم يفرغ فيملأ زئبقاً، إذ لو كان أجزاء الزئبق أكثر لزم أن يكون فيما بين أجزاء الماء فرج خلاء بقدر زيادة وزن الزئبق على وزن الماء، وأن يحس في زق الماء بالأحياز الفارغة أضعاف ما يحس به من المملوءة . هذا بعد تسليم وجود الخلاء، وعدم انحدار الماء بالطبع إلى الحيز الخالي بناء على إرادة القادر المختار أو أن في الخلاء قوة دافعة، ويمكن أن يقال: لا يحس بها لغاية الصغر، مع فرط الامتزاج بالأجزاء الماهية.
And one may argue for the falsehood of both views by saying that a single waterskin holds several times as much mercury as water, so mercury is much heavier than water, despite water's greater humidity by agreement, and assuming equal parts in the imagined case: that one fills the skin with water, then empties it, then fills it with mercury. For if the parts of mercury were more, then there would be empty spaces between the parts of water equal to the increase in weight of mercury over water, and one would sense in the skin of water that the empty spaces are many times more than the filled ones. This is after conceding the existence of empty space, and that water does not naturally descend into the empty space due to the will of the Powerful Chooser, or that empty space has a repelling force. It is possible to say: one does not sense them due to extreme smallness, along with intensive mixing with the material parts.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This is a proof against both al-Jubbai and Abu Ishaq. If heaviness were due to humidity, water should be heavier, but mercury is heavier despite being less humid. If heaviness were due to more parts, then mercury would have more atoms, implying empty spaces in water. The argument is conditional on accepting emptiness (khala') and then addresses potential objections: maybe emptiness is not sensed, or water does not flow into it due to divine will or repelling force. The author leaves it as a possible rebuttal.

Key Terms
الزق
الزئبق
الخلاء
القادر المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The waterskin argument proves that empty space actually exists.
Correction: The argument only assumes the existence of empty space for the sake of refuting the theories; it does not itself prove khala'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the waterskin argument is conditional upon:
A) The existence of humidity
B) The existence of empty space
C) The homogeneity of atoms
D) The will of God

Key Takeaway
The waterskin argument refutes both theories of heaviness by showing that unless empty space exists, neither humidity nor atomic count can explain weight differences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author uses a thought experiment to show that two opposing theories can both be wrong. Think of a conflict in your own beliefs where you have assumed one side must be true. Are you willing to entertain the possibility that both are false? What would that mean for how you live today?
?
The argument conditionally assumes empty space to explain weight differences. In your own faith, do you ever assume something exists (like God's grace, or the effectiveness of your deeds) simply because it explains your experiences? What would it mean to examine those assumptions as carefully as this theologian examined weight?
?
The author mentions that water does not descend into empty space due to the will of the Powerful Chooser. When you experience natural events, do you deeply feel that God is directly involved, or do you mentally substitute 'laws of nature'? This week, pause before one daily happening and consciously attribute it to God's immediate will. How does that shift your sense of connection to Him?

Abū Isḥāq's View on Atom Homogeneity
Abū Isḥāq's claim on atom homogeneity and weight determination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abū Isḥāq's claim that individual atoms are homogeneous and that differences in heaviness and lightness are due to the quantity of atoms.

Core Idea
Heaviness and lightness are not intrinsic to atoms, but depend on their count.

The nature of atoms was a central topic in kalām cosmology. Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī proposed that all atoms are identical in essence (homogeneous) and that weight differences in bodies arise solely from the number of atoms aggregated. This view simplifies the ontology of matter by reducing weight to a quantitative property.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the first Arabic sentence of the source, which presents Abū Isḥāq's view. Then read the translation. Note the key terms: jawāhir farda and mutajānisa.
The passage is concise; each word is important.
2
Identify the core claim
Abū Isḥāq asserts that atoms are homogeneous (mutajānisa), meaning they do not differ in themselves. Find the phrase that states this.
The term for atoms is 'jawāhir farda'.
3
Understand the explanation for weight
According to Abū Isḥāq, the heaviness or lightness of a body is explained by the number of atoms: more atoms = heavy, fewer atoms = light. This is a quantitative, not qualitative, difference.
This view implies that weight is an accidental property, not essential.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this view matters: it attempts to explain physical properties without invoking intrinsic differences in atoms, preserving the simplicity of atomism.
This theory was influential in kalām debates on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب الأستاذ أبو إسحاق إلى أن الجواهر الفردة متجانسة لا تتفاوت في الثقل والخفة، وإنما تفاوت الأجسام في ذلك عائد إلى كثرة الجواهر الفردة في الثقيل، وقلتها في الخفيف. ورد بعد تسليم التجانس بأنه يجوز أن يحدث في المركب من الأجزاء القليلة صفة الثقل، والكثيرة صفة الخفة لمحض إرادة المختار، أو بغيرها من الأسباب كسائر الأعراض من الألوان والطعوم وغيرها.
And Master Abū Isḥāq held that individual atoms are homogeneous, not differing in heaviness and lightness, but the difference of bodies in that regard is due to the multitude of individual atoms in the heavy and their scarcity in the light. And it was refuted, after conceding homogeneity, that it is permissible for the quality of heaviness to occur in the compound of few parts, and the quality of lightness in the compound of many, by sheer will of the Free Chooser, or by other causes like other accidents such as colors and tastes and others.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Abū Isḥāq on the nature of atoms and a refutation. Abū Isḥāq argues that atoms (jawāhir farda) are homogeneous (mutajānisa): they do not differ in heaviness or lightness in themselves. Rather, the heaviness or lightness of a body is determined by the quantity of atoms: a heavy body has more atoms, a light body has fewer. The refutation concedes homogeneity but objects: it is possible for God (al-Mukhtār, the Free Chooser) to create heaviness in a compound with few atoms and lightness in a compound with many, simply by His will, or through other causes, just as He creates other accidents like colors and tastes. Thus, the quantity of atoms is not necessarily linked to heaviness or lightness; it is contingent upon divine will.

Key Terms
جواهر فردة (jawāhir farda: individual atoms)
متجانسة (mutajānisa: homogeneous)
ثقل (thiqal: heaviness)
خفة (khiffa: lightness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Abū Isḥāq believes atoms themselves have varying degrees of heaviness.
Correction: He explicitly states that atoms do not differ in heaviness or lightness; they are homogeneous.

Checkpoint
According to Abū Isḥāq, what determines whether a body is heavy or light?
A) The inherent quality of each atom
B) The quantity of atoms in the body
C) The element composition
D) The will of the observer

Key Takeaway
Abū Isḥāq explains weight variation by atom quantity, not by intrinsic differences in atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all atoms are homogeneous, then your physical body and the body of a person you consider inferior are made of the exact same stuff. Does this change how you view others? What would it take for you to truly internalize this equality at the atomic level and let it affect your daily interactions?
?
You often measure your worth by what you accumulate—wealth, knowledge, status. But this lesson teaches that heaviness is just a matter of quantity, not quality. Are you chasing more 'atoms' while ignoring the essence of what truly matters? What one thing can you let go of today to lighten your load in the sight of Allah?

Refutation: Divine Will Overrides Atom-Weight Link
Refutation of Abū Isḥāq using divine omnipotence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation concedes atom homogeneity but argues that heaviness and lightness are contingent accidents that God can attach to any compound by His will.

Core Idea
Quantity does not necessitate weight; God can create heaviness in few atoms.

After presenting Abū Isḥāq's view, the author introduces a refutation that concedes the premise of homogeneity but attacks the necessary connection between atom quantity and weight. The refutation invokes divine omnipotence: God, as the Free Chooser (al-Mukhtār), can create heaviness in a compound of few atoms and lightness in many, just as He creates other accidents like colors and tastes.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the second part of the Arabic source, beginning with 'وورد بعد تسليم التجانس'. This is the refutation. Read the translation alongside.
The refutation starts by conceding homogeneity ('after conceding homogeneity').
2
Identify the concession
Notice that the refutation does not deny Abū Isḥāq's claim that atoms are homogeneous. It accepts it for the sake of argument ('after conceding homogeneity').
This is a common dialectical technique: you grant a premise to show that the conclusion does not follow.
3
Understand the core argument
The refutation asserts that it is permissible (yajūz) for heaviness to occur in a compound with few atoms, and lightness in a compound with many, by the sheer will of God (al-Mukhtār). This breaks the necessary link between quantity and weight.
The term 'al-Mukhtār' emphasizes God's absolute freedom.
4
Compare with other accidents
The refutation draws an analogy with other accidents like colors and tastes, which are not necessarily tied to the number of atoms. This reinforces the contingency of weight.
This shows that the refutation relies on the kalām principle that all accidents are created directly by God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب الأستاذ أبو إسحاق إلى أن الجواهر الفردة متجانسة لا تتفاوت في الثقل والخفة، وإنما تفاوت الأجسام في ذلك عائد إلى كثرة الجواهر الفردة في الثقيل، وقلتها في الخفيف. ورد بعد تسليم التجانس بأنه يجوز أن يحدث في المركب من الأجزاء القليلة صفة الثقل، والكثيرة صفة الخفة لمحض إرادة المختار، أو بغيرها من الأسباب كسائر الأعراض من الألوان والطعوم وغيرها.
And Master Abū Isḥāq held that individual atoms are homogeneous, not differing in heaviness and lightness, but the difference of bodies in that regard is due to the multitude of individual atoms in the heavy and their scarcity in the light. And it was refuted, after conceding homogeneity, that it is permissible for the quality of heaviness to occur in the compound of few parts, and the quality of lightness in the compound of many, by sheer will of the Free Chooser, or by other causes like other accidents such as colors and tastes and others.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Abū Isḥāq on the nature of atoms and a refutation. Abū Isḥāq argues that atoms (jawāhir farda) are homogeneous (mutajānisa): they do not differ in heaviness or lightness in themselves. Rather, the heaviness or lightness of a body is determined by the quantity of atoms: a heavy body has more atoms, a light body has fewer. The refutation concedes homogeneity but objects: it is possible for God (al-Mukhtār, the Free Chooser) to create heaviness in a compound with few atoms and lightness in a compound with many, simply by His will, or through other causes, just as He creates other accidents like colors and tastes. Thus, the quantity of atoms is not necessarily linked to heaviness or lightness; it is contingent upon divine will.

Key Terms
المختار (al-Mukhtār: the Free Chooser, i.e., God)
ثقل (thiqal: heaviness)
خفة (khiffa: lightness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the refutation denies atom homogeneity.
Correction: The refutation explicitly concedes homogeneity; it only attacks the necessary link between quantity and weight.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the refutation against Abū Isḥāq?
A) Atoms are not homogeneous
B) Heaviness and lightness are inherent in atoms
C) God can create heaviness in few atoms and lightness in many
D) Quantity of atoms always determines weight

Key Takeaway
Divine omnipotence, not atom count, is the ultimate cause of physical properties like heaviness and lightness.

?

Or do you still feel limited by the quantity of your resources? What would you attempt if you fully embraced this truth?
?
You likely acknowledge God's power in theory, but when you face a situation where natural logic says 'this cannot happen,' do you still pray for the impossible? What is one area in your life where you are trusting the number of atoms more than the Free Chooser?

The Mercury Argument Against Both Views on Heaviness
Uses a mercury and water experiment to refute both views on atom homogeneity and heaviness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mercury and water experiment challenges both views on heaviness in atomist kalam and why the argument depends on assuming the existence of void.

Core Idea
Empirical arguments in kalam often rest on metaphysical assumptions, such as the existence of void.

In classical atomist debates, the question of why objects have different weights was a point of contention. Some said weight depends on the number of atoms; others said it is directly created by God. The argument you are about to read uses a simple experiment with a waterskin, mercury, and water to raise a difficulty for both views. It is a classic example of how kalam thinkers used physical reasoning under specific assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views being refuted
Read the passage and note which two views on heaviness are being argued against. The commentary clarifies that these are the atom homogeneity view and the divine creation view.
2
Follow the logical steps of the argument
Trace how the experiment with a waterskin, mercury, and water leads to an inference about void spaces. Pay attention to the assumption that the waterskin is first filled with water, then with mercury.
3
Examine the conditions and concessions
The argument concedes the existence of void to make its point. Consider why this is necessary. Also note the two possibilities regarding why water does not descend into empty space.
The two possibilities: God's will (al-Mukhtār) or a repelling force (quwwa dāfi'a).
4
Consider the rebuttal
The argument ends with a possible objection: the void may not be sensed due to extreme smallness. Reflect on whether this weakens the argument.
This shows the argument is not conclusive but raises a plausible difficulty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يستدل على بطلان الرأيين بأن الزق الواحد يسع من الزئبق أضعاف ما يسع من الماء، فالزئبق أثقل من الماء بكثير، مع زيادة الماء في الرطوبة بالاتفاق، وتساويهما في الأجزاء في الصورة المفروضة وهي أن تملأ الزق ماء ثم يفرغ فيملأ زئبقاً، إذ لو كان أجزاء الزئبق أكثر لزم أن يكون فيما بين أجزاء الماء فرج خلاء بقدر زيادة وزن الزئبق على وزن الماء، وأن يحس في زق الماء بالأحياز الفارغة أضعاف ما يحس به من المملوءة . هذا بعد تسليم وجود الخلاء، وعدم انحدار الماء بالطبع إلى الحيز الخالي بناء على إرادة القادر المختار أو أن في الخلاء قوة دافعة، ويمكن أن يقال: لا يحس بها لغاية الصغر، مع فرط الامتزاج بالأجزاء الماهية.
And one may argue for the invalidity of both views by [the fact that] a single waterskin holds many times more mercury than it does water, so mercury is much heavier than water, despite the agreement that water has greater moisture, and their equality in parts in the assumed scenario, which is that the waterskin is filled with water, then emptied and filled with mercury. For if the parts of mercury were more, it would necessitate that there be among the parts of water gaps of void equal to the increase of the weight of mercury over the weight of water, and that one would sense in the waterskin of water empty spaces many times the sensed filled ones. This is after conceding the existence of void, and that water does not naturally descend into the empty space based on the will of the Powerful Free Chooser, or that there is a repelling force in the void, and it can be said: it is not sensed due to extreme smallness, along with extreme mixing with the water parts.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against both views (the view that heaviness is due to atom quantity, and the view that it is created by God arbitrarily). The argument uses an empirical test: a single waterskin (ziqq) can hold many times more mercury (zībaq) than water, yet mercury is much heavier. If we assume the skin is filled with water, then emptied and filled with mercury, if the number of mercury atoms were greater to account for the extra weight, then there would be void spaces (khalā') between the water atoms to accommodate the extra mercury atoms. But then we should sense these empty spaces, which we do not. The argument concedes the existence of void (khalā') as a prerequisite, and qualifies by either attributing the lack of water descent to God's will or a repelling force. It also suggests that maybe the void is not sensed because the empty spaces are extremely small and mixed with the water atoms. Thus, the argument does not definitively prove the falsity of both views but raises a plausible difficulty.

Key Terms
زق (ziqq: waterskin)
زئبق (zībaq: mercury)
خلاء (khalā': void)
قوة دافعة (quwwa dāfi'a: repelling force)
المختار (al-Mukhtār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mercury argument is a definitive proof that the atom homogeneity view is false.
Correction: The argument depends on the assumption of void and is not conclusive; it raises a plausible difficulty but allows for the rebuttal that void may be too small to sense.

Checkpoint
What does the mercury argument rely on as a key assumption?
A) The homogeneity of atoms
B) The existence of void
C) The absence of repelling force
D) The equivalence of water and mercury atoms

Key Takeaway
The mercury-water experiment illustrates how kalam arguments often rely on metaphysical assumptions like the existence of void, and how physical reasoning can be used to challenge but not necessarily refute competing theories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read an argument that relies on an unprovable assumption (the existence of void) to challenge a theological view. What is one hidden assumption in your own understanding of God's actions that you have never critically examined? How would you feel if that assumption were false?
?
The argument concedes that physical reasoning may not disprove divine causation ('based on the will of the Powerful Free Chooser'). When you encounter a natural explanation that seems to contradict a belief you hold, do you automatically dismiss it, or do you honestly consider the metaphysical baggage your belief carries? Which response is harder for you?
?
The counter-rebuttal says the void is 'not sensed due to extreme smallness.' Are there aspects of your faith that you have ignored or dismissed because they seem 'too small' or subtle to affect your daily life? What might you be missing by not attending to those 'small' truths?

The Debate on I'timad: Qadi's View and the Truth about Natural Forces
Presents Qadi's denial, objection, truth about natural i'timad, and debate on unnatural.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qadi's denial of multiple i'timad, the objection based on observed examples, and the correct position on natural vs. unnatural i'timad.

Core Idea
Natural i'timad are opposed, but natural and unnatural can coexist.

This passage dives into a nuanced debate in kalam about the nature of forces (i'timad) in bodies. Al-Qadi argued for a single force, but others pointed to examples like a rope pulled from both sides to argue for multiplicity. The text then clarifies the truth: natural forces are indeed opposed, but unnatural forces can coexist with natural ones. We'll walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Qadi's position
Read the first part of the passage where al-Qadi claims i'timad is a single quality, named heaviness or lightness based on direction. Note that he denies multiplicity.
This is a reductionist view: one attribute with relational names.
2
Explore the objection
Look at the objection: some scholars say i'timad can be multiple because we observe a rope pulled from above and below simultaneously. This challenges al-Qadi's view.
The rope example shows opposite forces coexisting in one body.
3
Identify the truth about natural i'timad
The text states the truth: two natural i'timad (heaviness and lightness) are opposed and cannot coexist in the same respect. But a natural and an unnatural i'timad can coexist, as in a stone being lifted.
Natural i'timad are intrinsic; unnatural are imposed.
4
Analyze the debate on unnatural i'timad
Read the argument that two different unnatural i'timad should be opposed because they would cause motion in opposite directions. Then see the rebuttal: they can combine in a rope pulled both ways, and the response that this is like a stationary object preventing motion, not true mutual resistance.
This shows the complexity of the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ومنع القاضي تعدد الاعتمادات حتى زعم أن في الجسم كيفية واحدة تسمى بالنسبة إلى السفل ثقلا ، وإلى العلو خفة، وبعضهم تضادها لما أنها تجتمع كما في الحجز المجاذب علواً وسفلاً، والجبل المتجاذب يميناً وشمالاً . والحق أن الطبيعيين متضادان وأن تضاد بين الطبيعي وغيره، كما في الحجز الذي يرفع، وأما غير الطبيعي فقيل المختلفان منه متضادان لماله من أن المبدأ القريب مبدأ للحركة، فيلزم من اجتماع الاعتمادين المختلفين اجتماع الحركتين بالذات إلى جهتين، وهو محال . ورد بأنه ليس من تمام العلة، كيف وقد اجتمعا في الحبل المتجاذب إلى الجانبين، وقد يقال لا مدافعة، وإنما هو كالساكن الذي يمتنع عن التحرك .
He said: And al-Qāḍī denied the multiplicity of i'timādāt such that he claimed that in a body there is a single quality called, relative to the lower, heaviness, and relative to the higher, lightness. And some scholars opposed him because they can combine, as in a rope pulled upward and downward, and a mountain pulled to the right and left. And the truth is that the two natural [i'timādāt] are opposed, and there is no opposition between the natural and the unnatural, as in the stone that is lifted. As for the unnatural, it is said that the two different ones from it are opposed because the proximate principle is a principle of motion, so combining two different i'timādāt would necessitate the combination of two motions intrinsically to two directions, which is impossible. And it was refuted that it is not a complete cause; indeed they have combined in a rope pulled to both sides. And it may be said: there is no mutual resistance; rather it is like a stationary object that prevents motion.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of i'timād (a technical term often translated as 'reliance' or 'force' in a body). It begins with al-Qāḍī's view: i'timād in a body is a single quality that receives different names based on direction—relative to downward it is called heaviness (thiqal), relative to upward it is called lightness (khiffa). Some scholars objected that i'timād can be multiple, as seen when a rope is pulled from above and below simultaneously, or a mountain pulled from right and left. The author states the truth: the two natural i'timādāt (heaviness and lightness) are indeed opposed and cannot coexist in one subject in the same respect. However, there is no opposition between a natural and an unnatural i'timād, e.g., a stone being lifted has a natural downward i'timād (heaviness) and an unnatural upward i'timād (from the lifter), and they coexist. For unnatural i'timād, some say that two different ones (e.g., to the left and right) are opposed because they are proximate principles (mabda' qarīb) of motion, so if they combined, two motions in opposite directions would occur simultaneously, which is impossible. But this is refuted: it is not a complete cause, because they do combine in a rope pulled from both sides. A response: that is not actual mutual resistance but like a stationary object that prevents motion.

Key Terms
اعتماد (i'timād)
تعدد (ta'addud)
طبيعي (tabī'ī)
غير طبيعي (ghayr tabī'ī)
مبدأ قريب (mabda' qarīb)
مدافعة (mudāfa'a)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural and unnatural i'timad cannot coexist in a body.
Correction: The text clarifies that natural and unnatural i'timad are not opposed; they can coexist, as in a stone being lifted.

Checkpoint
According to the truth stated in this passage, which two types of i'timād are opposed?
A) Natural and unnatural i'timād
B) Two natural i'timādāt (heaviness and lightness)
C) Two unnatural i'timādāt to different directions
D) All i'timādāt are opposed

Key Takeaway
Natural i'timad are opposed to each other, but natural and unnatural i'timad can coexist, as illustrated by the example of a lifted stone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself pulled in two opposing directions—whether by desires, duties, or beliefs—do you recognize that such tension may come from forces of different natures (one natural, one imposed)? How does this lesson's distinction help you avoid concluding that all conflict is destructive?
?
The passage shows that equal opposing forces can result in stillness, not motion. Is there an area of your life where you have remained stationary—not because of a lack of strength, but because of conflicting pulls? Will you examine whether one of those pulls is unnatural and reconsider surrendering to it?
?
Al-Qāḍī reduced the multiplicity of forces to a single quality with different names. In your own spiritual life, do you sometimes rename your desires (e.g., calling 'fear' 'caution') to avoid confronting their contradiction with your faith? Are you ready to admit the real multiplicity of your inner forces and submit them to the One who alone creates motion and stillness?

Al-Qadi's View: I'timad as a Single Reality
Al-Qadi's view on the unity of i'timad and its naming

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qadi's position that i'timad is a single matter with different names based on directional consideration, and what he meant by the permissibility of combining i'timads.

Core Idea
I'timad is one; its names vary, but it does not multiply.

The discussion of i'timad begins with a foundational view from al-Qadi: that this quality is essentially one, but acquires different names (heaviness, lightness) depending on the direction considered. This lesson unpacks that view and clarifies a common misinterpretation about combining i'timads.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Qadi's position
Read the Arabic text where al-Qadi states that i'timad in every body is a single matter. Notice how he connects it to the names 'lightness' and 'heaviness' based on direction.
The text emphasizes that the name 'i'timad' is general, but specific terms arise from relation to up/down.
2
Understand the 'combination' claim
Examine the phrase about 'permissibility of combination of i'timads'. Al-Qadi explains it means supposing different considerations and relations to the six directions, not actual multiple i'timads.
This clarifies that combination is conceptual, not real.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why would al-Qadi insist on the unity of i'timad? This relates to avoiding multiplicity in divine attributes and the simplicity of created bodies.
This connects to broader kalām principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومنع القاضي . اختلف أصحابنا القائلون بالاعتماد. فذهب القاضي إلى أن الاعتماد في كل جسم أمر واحد ربما يتعدد أسماؤه بحسب الاعتبار، حتى يسمى بالنسبة إلى العلو خفة، وإلى السفل ثقلاً. وليس له بالنسبة إلى الجهات الأخر اسم خاص، فما نقل عنه من جواز اجتماع الاعتمادات فمعناه جواز أن يفرض لذلك الأمر الاعتبارات المختلفة والإضافة إلى الجهات الست، وذهب بعضهم إلى أنها متعددة متضادة لا يقوم بجسم اعتقادان بالنسبة إلى جهتين، وبعضهم إلى أنها متعددة غير متضادة، لأن من جذب حجراً ثقيلاً إلى جهة العلو فإنه يحس منه اعتماد إلى جهة السفل، ولو جذبه غيره إلى جهة السفل يحس منه اعتماد إلى جهة العلو، ولأن كلاً من المتجاذبين جبلا على التقاوم، والتساوي في القوة يحس من الحمل، اعتماداً إلى خلاف جهته، والحق أن الاعتمادين الطبيعين أعني الثقل والخفة متضادان، لا اجتماعهما في شيء واحد، باعتبار واحد وأنه لا تضاد بین الاعتماد الطبيعي وغير الطبيعي، كما في الحجر الذي يرفع إلى فوق، فإن الرفع يحس هابطة والرفع مدافعة صاعدة، وأما غير الطبيعي من الاعتماد فقيل : المختلفان منه متضادان كالاعتماد يمنة ويسره لأنه مبدأ قريب للحركة، فلو جاز الاعتمادان معاً لجاز الحركتان بالذات معاً لاستلزام وجود المؤثر وجود الأثر ويلزم منه جواز كون الجسم في آن واحد في حيزين، وإنما قيد بالذات لأنه لا يمتنع حركتان إلى جهتين إذا كانت إحداهما بالذات والأخرى بالعرض كراكب السفينة يتحرك إلى خلاف الجهة التي تتحرك إليها السفينة، وهذا معنى ما قال الجبائي : إن الحركتين إلى جهتين متضادتان. وكذا يتصور مدافعة.
He said: And al-Qāḍī denied. Our associates who affirm i'timād differed. Al-Qāḍī held that i'timād in every body is a single matter, whose names may multiply according to consideration, so it is called relative to the high 'lightness' and relative to the low 'heaviness.' It has no specific name relative to the other directions. So what is transmitted from him about the permissibility of combination of i'timādāt means the permissibility of supposing for that matter different considerations and relations to the six directions. Some held that they are multiple and opposed, so that two i'timādāt cannot subsist in a single body relative to two directions. Some held that they are multiple but not opposed, because when someone pulls a heavy stone upward, he senses from it an i'timād toward the downward direction, and if another pulls it downward, he senses from it an i'timād toward the upward direction, and because each of the two pulling parties are disposed to mutual resistance, and equality in strength senses from the load an i'timād contrary to its own direction. The truth is that the two natural i'timādāt, namely heaviness and lightness, are opposed; they cannot coexist in one thing in one respect. And there is no opposition between natural and unnatural i'timād, as in a stone that is lifted upward, for the lifting senses a downward [i'timād] and the lifting is an upward mutual resistance. As for unnatural i'timād, it is said: the two different ones from it are opposed, like i'timād to the right and left, because it is a proximate principle of motion. For if the two i'timādāt were permissible together, then two motions intrinsically would be permissible together, due to the entailment of existence of the effect from the existence of the cause, and it would necessitate permissibility of the body being in two places at once. It is qualified by 'intrinsically' because two motions to two directions are not impossible if one is intrinsic and the other accidental, like a ship passenger moving opposite to the ship's direction. This is the meaning of what al-Jubbā'ī said: two motions to two directions are opposed. And likewise mutual resistance is conceivable.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the different positions on i'timād among the proponents (ashābunā). It starts by repeating al-Qāḍī's view that i'timād is one thing called by different names depending on direction (heaviness downward, lightness upward). It clarifies that his claim of 'permissibility of combination' refers to different considerations, not actual multiple i'timādāt. Then it presents three opinions: (1) Some say i'timādāt are multiple and opposed, so a body cannot have two i'timādāt to two directions. (2) Some say they are multiple but not opposed, giving examples of pulling a stone: the puller senses a counter i'timād from the stone. (3) The truth (al-ḥaqq): natural i'timādāt (heaviness/lightness) are opposed, but natural and unnatural are not opposed. Then it discusses unnatural i'timād: some say two different ones (e.g., left-right) are opposed because they are proximate principles of motion. If they coexisted, two motions in opposite directions would occur simultaneously, implying a body in two places at once. However, this is qualified: motions can coexist if one is intrinsic and the other accidental, as in the ship passenger example. The passage concludes by referencing al-Jubbā'ī's statement that two motions to two directions are opposed, and that mutual resistance is conceivable.

Key Terms
اعتماد (i'timād)
باعتبار (bi al-iʿtibār: by consideration)
خفة (khiffa: lightness)
ثقل (thiqal: heaviness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Qadi believed in multiple i'timads subsisting together in a body.
Correction: Al-Qadi holds that i'timad is one, and 'combination' refers to considering it in relation to different directions, not multiple distinct i'timads.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qadi, what is i'timad in every body?
A) Multiple distinct i'timads
B) A single matter with multiple names
C) Heaviness only
D) Lightness only

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadi's i'timad is one reality; its multiple names do not indicate multiplicity of the attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have one soul, but you wear many masks. Are you the same person in private as you are in public? Or do you multiply your i'timad depending on direction?
?
Al-Qadi insisted that different names do not change the one reality. What are the 'lightness' and 'heaviness' in your life that are actually one neglected duty—wearing different labels to avoid confronting it?

Three Opinions on Multiplicity of I'timad
Three positions on whether i'timad is one or multiple and whether they oppose

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three positions on whether i'timad are multiple and mutually opposed, based on the arguments of their proponents.

Core Idea
I'timad can be one, multiple-opposed, or multiple-non-opposed.

After presenting al-Qadi's unitary view, the text introduces two other opinions within the school. Some hold that i'timad are multiple and opposed, while others argue they are multiple but not opposed. This lesson examines the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
The text lists three views: al-Qadi's (one), some say multiple and opposed, some say multiple and not opposed. Mark each clearly.
2
Understand the 'multiple and opposed' view
Read the phrase: 'they are multiple and opposed, so two i'timads cannot subsist in a body relative to two directions.' Consider why opposition would prevent coexistence.
Opposition here means they cannot be in the same subject at the same time.
3
Analyze the 'multiple and not opposed' argument
Examine the example of pulling a stone: the stone has i'timad downward when pulled up, and upward when pulled down. The example shows that opposing pulls can coexist in one body.
The argument uses empirical observation of tension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومنع القاضي . اختلف أصحابنا القائلون بالاعتماد. فذهب القاضي إلى أن الاعتماد في كل جسم أمر واحد ربما يتعدد أسماؤه بحسب الاعتبار، حتى يسمى بالنسبة إلى العلو خفة، وإلى السفل ثقلاً. وليس له بالنسبة إلى الجهات الأخر اسم خاص، فما نقل عنه من جواز اجتماع الاعتمادات فمعناه جواز أن يفرض لذلك الأمر الاعتبارات المختلفة والإضافة إلى الجهات الست، وذهب بعضهم إلى أنها متعددة متضادة لا يقوم بجسم اعتقادان بالنسبة إلى جهتين، وبعضهم إلى أنها متعددة غير متضادة، لأن من جذب حجراً ثقيلاً إلى جهة العلو فإنه يحس منه اعتماد إلى جهة السفل، ولو جذبه غيره إلى جهة السفل يحس منه اعتماد إلى جهة العلو، ولأن كلاً من المتجاذبين جبلا على التقاوم، والتساوي في القوة يحس من الحمل، اعتماداً إلى خلاف جهته، والحق أن الاعتمادين الطبيعين أعني الثقل والخفة متضادان، لا اجتماعهما في شيء واحد، باعتبار واحد وأنه لا تضاد بین الاعتماد الطبيعي وغير الطبيعي، كما في الحجر الذي يرفع إلى فوق، فإن الرفع يحس هابطة والرفع مدافعة صاعدة، وأما غير الطبيعي من الاعتماد فقيل : المختلفان منه متضادان كالاعتماد يمنة ويسره لأنه مبدأ قريب للحركة، فلو جاز الاعتمادان معاً لجاز الحركتان بالذات معاً لاستلزام وجود المؤثر وجود الأثر ويلزم منه جواز كون الجسم في آن واحد في حيزين، وإنما قيد بالذات لأنه لا يمتنع حركتان إلى جهتين إذا كانت إحداهما بالذات والأخرى بالعرض كراكب السفينة يتحرك إلى خلاف الجهة التي تتحرك إليها السفينة، وهذا معنى ما قال الجبائي : إن الحركتين إلى جهتين متضادتان. وكذا يتصور مدافعة.
He said: And al-Qāḍī denied. Our associates who affirm i'timād differed. Al-Qāḍī held that i'timād in every body is a single matter, whose names may multiply according to consideration, so it is called relative to the high 'lightness' and relative to the low 'heaviness.' It has no specific name relative to the other directions. So what is transmitted from him about the permissibility of combination of i'timādāt means the permissibility of supposing for that matter different considerations and relations to the six directions. Some held that they are multiple and opposed, so that two i'timādāt cannot subsist in a single body relative to two directions. Some held that they are multiple but not opposed, because when someone pulls a heavy stone upward, he senses from it an i'timād toward the downward direction, and if another pulls it downward, he senses from it an i'timād toward the upward direction, and because each of the two pulling parties are disposed to mutual resistance, and equality in strength senses from the load an i'timād contrary to its own direction. The truth is that the two natural i'timādāt, namely heaviness and lightness, are opposed; they cannot coexist in one thing in one respect. And there is no opposition between natural and unnatural i'timād, as in a stone that is lifted upward, for the lifting senses a downward [i'timād] and the lifting is an upward mutual resistance. As for unnatural i'timād, it is said: the two different ones from it are opposed, like i'timād to the right and left, because it is a proximate principle of motion. For if the two i'timādāt were permissible together, then two motions intrinsically would be permissible together, due to the entailment of existence of the effect from the existence of the cause, and it would necessitate permissibility of the body being in two places at once. It is qualified by 'intrinsically' because two motions to two directions are not impossible if one is intrinsic and the other accidental, like a ship passenger moving opposite to the ship's direction. This is the meaning of what al-Jubbā'ī said: two motions to two directions are opposed. And likewise mutual resistance is conceivable.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the different positions on i'timād among the proponents (ashābunā). It starts by repeating al-Qāḍī's view that i'timād is one thing called by different names depending on direction (heaviness downward, lightness upward). It clarifies that his claim of 'permissibility of combination' refers to different considerations, not actual multiple i'timādāt. Then it presents three opinions: (1) Some say i'timādāt are multiple and opposed, so a body cannot have two i'timādāt to two directions. (2) Some say they are multiple but not opposed, giving examples of pulling a stone: the puller senses a counter i'timād from the stone. (3) The truth (al-ḥaqq): natural i'timādāt (heaviness/lightness) are opposed, but natural and unnatural are not opposed. Then it discusses unnatural i'timād: some say two different ones (e.g., left-right) are opposed because they are proximate principles of motion. If they coexisted, two motions in opposite directions would occur simultaneously, implying a body in two places at once. However, this is qualified: motions can coexist if one is intrinsic and the other accidental, as in the ship passenger example. The passage concludes by referencing al-Jubbā'ī's statement that two motions to two directions are opposed, and that mutual resistance is conceivable.

Key Terms
متضادة (mutadāddah: opposed)
غير متضادة (ghayr mutadāddah: not opposed)
تجاذب (tajādhub: mutual attraction/pulling)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which opinion uses the example of pulling a heavy stone to argue that i'timad are multiple but not opposed?
A) Al-Qadi's view
B) The view that i'timad are multiple and opposed
C) The view that i'timad are multiple and not opposed
D) The definitive truth

Key Takeaway
Among the proponents of i'timad, there are three positions: unity, multiplicity with opposition, and multiplicity without opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you felt pulled in two opposite directions—like a desire to relax versus a duty to work. Did you assume that only one could truly 'exist' at a time? How does the concept of non-opposed multiplicity of i'timad challenge your assumption that these inner pulls must be in conflict? What would it mean for your spiritual life if you could accept that multiple intentions can coexist without opposition?
?
In your own heart, do you have contradictory inclinations—like love of this world and love of the afterlife—that you assume cannot coexist? How might the argument about non-opposed i'timad open a path to integrate these competing pulls rather than suppress one? What concrete step can you take today to let both impulses exist without forcing a false choice?
?
If i'timad can be multiple and non-opposed, what does that imply about your responsibility for your actions? Are you ever truly 'neutral' or are you always leaning in some direction? This lesson suggests that multiple leanings can coexist—so what is the state of your heart's i'timad right now? Are you leaning toward Allah or away? Or both? What will you do about it before the end of this day?

The Definitive Truth: Natural vs. Unnatural I'timad
Definitive truth on natural and unnatural i'timad and their opposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitive truth that natural i'timad (heaviness/lightness) are opposed, while natural and unnatural i'timad are not opposed, and why unnatural i'timad to opposite directions are opposed due to implications for motion.

Core Idea
Natural i'timads oppose; natural and unnatural can coexist.

After presenting the differing views, the text gives the definitive position (al-ḥaqq). It distinguishes between natural and unnatural i'timad, and explains their interaction. This lesson clarifies the conditions under which i'timad can coexist and introduces al-Jubbā'ī's related view on motion.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish natural vs unnatural i'timad
Natural i'timad are heaviness and lightness; unnatural are any other directional dependencies. Read the text to see how they are defined.
Natural i'timad are inherent, while unnatural are acquired through external force.
2
Understand the opposition rule
The text states: natural i'timads (heaviness and lightness) are opposed and cannot coexist in one body in one respect. But a natural and unnatural can coexist, like a stone being lifted: it has natural heaviness downward and unnatural lightness upward from the lift.
3
Examine the argument for unnatural i'timad opposition
It is argued that two different unnatural i'timads (e.g., left and right) are opposed because they are proximate principles of motion. If they coexisted, two contrary motions would occur, leading to absurdity of a body being in two places at once.
The text qualifies 'intrinsically' (bi al-dhāt) to allow accidental coexistence (ship passenger example).
4
Connect to al-Jubbā'ī's statement
The passage ends by relating this to al-Jubbā'ī's view that two motions to two directions are opposed, and that mutual resistance (mudāfa'a) is conceivable.
This shows the broader kalām context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومنع القاضي . اختلف أصحابنا القائلون بالاعتماد. فذهب القاضي إلى أن الاعتماد في كل جسم أمر واحد ربما يتعدد أسماؤه بحسب الاعتبار، حتى يسمى بالنسبة إلى العلو خفة، وإلى السفل ثقلاً. وليس له بالنسبة إلى الجهات الأخر اسم خاص، فما نقل عنه من جواز اجتماع الاعتمادات فمعناه جواز أن يفرض لذلك الأمر الاعتبارات المختلفة والإضافة إلى الجهات الست، وذهب بعضهم إلى أنها متعددة متضادة لا يقوم بجسم اعتقادان بالنسبة إلى جهتين، وبعضهم إلى أنها متعددة غير متضادة، لأن من جذب حجراً ثقيلاً إلى جهة العلو فإنه يحس منه اعتماد إلى جهة السفل، ولو جذبه غيره إلى جهة السفل يحس منه اعتماد إلى جهة العلو، ولأن كلاً من المتجاذبين جبلا على التقاوم، والتساوي في القوة يحس من الحمل، اعتماداً إلى خلاف جهته، والحق أن الاعتمادين الطبيعين أعني الثقل والخفة متضادان، لا اجتماعهما في شيء واحد، باعتبار واحد وأنه لا تضاد بین الاعتماد الطبيعي وغير الطبيعي، كما في الحجر الذي يرفع إلى فوق، فإن الرفع يحس هابطة والرفع مدافعة صاعدة، وأما غير الطبيعي من الاعتماد فقيل : المختلفان منه متضادان كالاعتماد يمنة ويسره لأنه مبدأ قريب للحركة، فلو جاز الاعتمادان معاً لجاز الحركتان بالذات معاً لاستلزام وجود المؤثر وجود الأثر ويلزم منه جواز كون الجسم في آن واحد في حيزين، وإنما قيد بالذات لأنه لا يمتنع حركتان إلى جهتين إذا كانت إحداهما بالذات والأخرى بالعرض كراكب السفينة يتحرك إلى خلاف الجهة التي تتحرك إليها السفينة، وهذا معنى ما قال الجبائي : إن الحركتين إلى جهتين متضادتان. وكذا يتصور مدافعة.
He said: And al-Qāḍī denied. Our associates who affirm i'timād differed. Al-Qāḍī held that i'timād in every body is a single matter, whose names may multiply according to consideration, so it is called relative to the high 'lightness' and relative to the low 'heaviness.' It has no specific name relative to the other directions. So what is transmitted from him about the permissibility of combination of i'timādāt means the permissibility of supposing for that matter different considerations and relations to the six directions. Key Terms
طبيعي (tabī'ī: natural)
غير طبيعي (ghayr tabī'ī: unnatural)
بالذات (bi al-dhāt: intrinsically)
بالعرض (bi al-'araḍ: accidentally)
الجبائي (al-Jubbā'ī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that all i'timads are opposed if they are in different directions.
Correction: Only natural i'timads (heaviness/lightness) are opposed; natural and unnatural can coexist, and unnatural i'timads to opposite directions are opposed only if they would cause two intrinsic motions.

Checkpoint
According to the definitive truth, which i'timad can coexist in one body?
A) Two natural i'timads (heaviness and lightness)
B) A natural and an unnatural i'timad
C) Two unnatural i'timads to opposite directions
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The definitive truth distinguishes natural versus unnatural i'timad: natural ones oppose each other, but natural and unnatural can coexist; opposite unnatural i'timads are opposed only if both are intrinsic principles of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, are there areas where you assume two opposing forces or qualities cannot coexist, when in fact they might? For example, can you be both humble and confident? What false dichotomies might you be holding that this lesson challenges you to reconsider?
?
The passage distinguishes between intrinsic and accidental motions. When you act, are your motivations intrinsic to your character or accidental circumstances? Can you identify a recent decision where you confused an accidental impulse with a core intention? How might recalibrating that distinction change your choices?
?
Like the stone that retains its natural heaviness while being lifted, what natural burdens or responsibilities do you carry that you cannot shed, even as you strive for unnatural heights? Are you resisting reality by pretending those burdens aren't there, or are you learning to carry them while reaching upward?

Objection and Answer on I'timad and Movement
Reply to al-Amidi's objection on i'timad and movement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's objection to using movement as proof of i'timad's multiplicity and the reply that distinguishes different meanings of 'proximate principle' and counters with the rope example.

Core Idea
Contradiction of effects does not prove contradiction of causes.

Al-Amidi challenges an earlier argument that two contradictory movements must arise from two contradictory i'timads. He argues that movement is an effect of i'timad, but contradictory effects do not necessarily imply contradictory causes. This passage presents that objection and a nuanced response that refines the notion of causality and introduces a physical counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read al-Amidi's objection carefully. He claims that the analogy between movements and i'timads fails because contradiction of effects does not prove contradiction of causes.
Notice how he uses the example of nature causing movement or rest under different conditions.
2
Identify the two parts of the answer
The answer first distinguishes two meanings of 'proximate principle' (complete cause vs. more general), then counters with the rope example even if the argument were granted.
The answer is structured as a conditional: if meaning A, then we deny; if meaning B, we still deny necessity.
3
Examine the rope example
The rope pulled by equal opposing forces is used to show that two i'timads can coexist without contradicting each other, contrary to the objection's implication.
There is a sub-debate about whether the rope feels a repulsion or is merely like a stationary object.
4
Reflect on the dialectical style
Notice how the answer uses concessions ('we accept it') and counterexamples to refute the objection without fully conceding the premise.
This is typical of kalam argumentation: engaging the opponent's reasoning step by step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتمادان الموجبان لهما، وحينئذ لا يرد ما قال الأمدي : إن هذا تمثيل بلا جامع، كيف والحركة أثر الاعتماد وتضاد الآثار لا يوجب اختلاف المؤثر فضلاً عن تضاده كالطبيعة توجب الحركة، وبشرط الخروج عن الحيز الطبيعي، والسكون بشرط الحصول فيه على أن الفرق قائم. فإن اجتماع الحركتين إلى جهتين إنما امتنع لاستلزامه حصول الجسم في حالة واحدة في حيزين، ولا كذلك الاعتمادان . الجواب : أنه إن أريد بالمبدأ القريب تمام العلة، فلا نسلم أن الاعتماد كذلك، بل لا بد من انتفاء المانع . وإن أريد الأعم فلا نسلم أنه يوجب وجود الأثر على أنه لو تم هذا الدليل تضاد الطبيعي وغير الطبيعي لجريانه فيه سلمنا لكنه معارض بأنهما لو كانا متضادين لما جاز اجتماعهما واللازم باطل لأن الحبل المتجاذب بقوتين متساويتين إلى جهتين متقابلتين يجد فيه كل من الجاذبين مدافعة إلى خلاف جهته. وقد يقال : لا ، بل هو كالساكن الذي يمتنع عن التحريك لا مدافعة فيه أصلا.
The two i'timads that necessitate them [the two movements]. Then what al-Amidi said is not refuted: 'This is an analogy without a common ground; how so? Movement is the effect of i'timad, and the contradiction of effects does not necessitate difference in the cause, let alone its contradiction—like nature necessitates movement under condition of leaving its natural place, and rest under condition of being in it, and the difference stands. For the coming together of two movements to two directions is impossible only because it necessitates the body being in two places at one state, and not so for two i'timads.' The answer: If by 'the proximate principle' is meant the complete cause, we do not grant that i'timad is like that; indeed, the absence of an obstacle is necessary. And if the more general [meaning] is intended, we do not grant that it necessitates the existence of the effect. Even if this argument were valid for the contradiction of natural and unnatural i'timad, we accept it, but it is countered by [the fact] that if they were contradictory, their coming together would not be permissible, and the consequent is false, because the rope pulled by two equal forces to two opposite directions: each of the two pullers finds in it a repulsion opposite to his direction. And it is said: No, rather it is like the stationary object that refuses to be moved; there is no repulsion in it at all.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Amidi and an answer. Al-Amidi argues that the example of two movements does not apply to i'timad because movement is the effect of i'timad; contradiction of effects does not prove contradiction of causes. He gives the analogy of nature causing movement or rest under different conditions. The impossibility of two movements arises from the body being in two places, which does not apply to i'timads. The answer distinguishes two meanings of 'proximate principle': if it means the complete cause, then i'timad is not a complete cause—obstacles matter. If it means a broader cause, we do not grant necessity of the effect. Even if the argument proved contradiction of natural and unnatural i'timad, it is countered by the rope example where equal forces result in no movement but a sense of repulsion; others deny even that repulsion, comparing it to an object that simply refuses to move.

Key Terms
الأمدي
المبدأ القريب
تمام العلة
الحركة
الاعتماد
تضاد الآثار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility of two simultaneous movements directly proves the contradiction of i'timads.
Correction: Al-Amidi shows that the impossibility arises from spatial constraints, not from any inherent contradiction in the causes. The answer further shows that coexistence is possible, as in the rope example.

Checkpoint
What is al-Amidi's main objection?
A) I'timad is not a cause
B) Movement is not an effect of i'timad
C) Contradiction of effects does not prove contradiction of causes
D) The rope example is invalid

Key Takeaway
Contradictory effects do not necessarily imply contradictory causes; the answer refines causality and uses the rope example to permit coexistence of i'timads.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe conflicting outcomes in your life—like two prayers seemingly unanswered—do you instinctively assume contradictory causes (e.g., one prayer accepted, the other rejected)? How does this lesson's principle that contradictory effects can come from a single cause challenge your assumption? Will you now pause before concluding that God is treating you inconsistently?
?
The lesson shows that i'timad is not a complete cause; it requires absence of obstacle. Think of a spiritual struggle you face—do you assume that just because you have 'faith' (like i'timad) the result should follow automatically? What 'obstacle' might you be neglecting that prevents the effect? Name one concrete obstacle you will remove this week.
?
Al-Amidi argued that the impossibility of two movements proves nothing about i'timads. Are there areas in your life where you have mistakenly analogized two things that lack a common ground? For example, assuming that if something is difficult in one context, it must be impossible in another? Where do you need to stop drawing false analogies and examine the actual conditions?
