beginner
5 min
Al-Jubba'i's Proof of Upward I'timad in Air
Al-Jubba'i argues air has an inherent upward i'timad and defends against objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i's empirical argument for an inherent upward i'timad in air and his rebuttal to the counter-explanation of water pressure.

Core Idea
Air has an inherent upward inclination, proven by the bladder experiment and defended against pressure objections.

Al-Jubba'i, a prominent Mu'tazili theologian, advanced a unique argument for an inherent property (i'timad) in air that causes it to rise. He bases this on a simple physical observation involving a submerged bladder. This passage presents his proof and his response to a natural objection, illustrating the rational-empirical method of kalam. By examining this argument, you'll see how theologians used everyday phenomena to infer underlying metaphysical principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its English translation to get a sense of the argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: اعتماد, صاعد, لازم, زق, ضغط.
2
Identify the empirical observation
Locate the description of the bladder experiment. What happens when the bladder under water is pierced?
The phrase 'إذا شق خرج الهواء صاعداً' is central.
3
Understand the objection
Find the sentence beginning with 'لا يقال'. What alternative explanation is proposed?
The objection attributes the ascent to water pressure (ضغط الماء).
4
Analyze the rebuttal
Examine the response starting with 'لأنا نقول'. How does al-Jubba'i refute the objection?
He argues that pressure would cause stability, not ascent, especially if the bladder is tied.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why al-Jubba'i's reasoning is considered a proof for inherent i'timad.
Think about the logical necessity: if external pressure were the cause, the effect would be downward, not upward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الاعتماد منها : ما قال الجبائى : إن في الهواء اعتماداً صاعداً لازماً لما يشاهد في الزق المفتوح المقسور تحت الماء، أنه إذا شق خرج الهواء صاعداً ، وشق الماء بل لو زال القاصر الهواء بالزق، وما اتعلق به من الثقل . لا يقال : يجوز أن لا يكون ذلك لصعوده اللازم، بل لضغط الماء إياه، وإخراجه من حيزه بثقل وطأته، لأنا نقول : لولم يكن في طبعه الصعود والطفو على الماء لما زاد ثقل وطأة الماء إلا استقراراً وثباتاً كسائر الأثقال، سيما إذا بقي الزق مشدوداً .
From the chapter on i'timad: Among them is what al-Jubba'i said: That in air there is a necessary upward i'timad, due to what is observed in an open bladder forced under water, that if it is pierced, the air comes out upward, and tears the water, but if the restrainer were removed, the air would exit with the bladder and what is attached to it of weight. It cannot be said: It is possible that this is not due to its necessary ascent, but rather due to the water's pressure on it, expelling it from its place by the weight of its pressure, because we say: If it were not in its nature to ascend and float on water, increased weight of water's pressure would only cause it to settle and remain stable like other heavy things, especially if the bladder remains tied.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents al-Jubba'i's argument for an inherent upward i'timad in air. He observes that a submerged bladder, when pierced, releases air that rises upward, even tearing the water. He anticipates an objection: the movement could be caused by water pressure, not inherent ascent. The rebuttal states that pure external pressure would push the air down, not up, so the upward movement must be due to an intrinsic property. The phrase 'سيما إذا بقي الزق مشدودا' (especially if the bladder remains tied) emphasizes that even when the bladder is secured, the air still exerts upward force when released.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The upward movement of air in the bladder is solely caused by the pressure of water.
Correction: Al-Jubba'i argues that if pressure were the cause, the air would be pushed downward, not upward; thus, the upward movement indicates an inherent property.

Checkpoint
What objection does al-Jubba'i address regarding the bladder experiment?
A) The air might be escaping due to chemical reaction.
B) The movement could be due to water pressure, not inherent ascent.
C) The bladder might be porous.
D) The air is lighter than water.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i uses a bladder experiment to argue that air possesses an inherent upward i'timad, independent of external forces like water pressure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Jubba'i says that if air were not inherently ascending, pressure would only make it settle. In your life, when external pressures increase, do you find yourself rising in faith or sinking into worldly concerns? What would change if you acknowledged your soul's natural upward inclination toward God?
?
The bladder experiment shows that the air's true nature is revealed when the constraint is removed. If all social and worldly constraints were suddenly lifted, which way would your heart move? What does that tell you about the direction you are cultivating within yourself?

Abu Hashim's Refutation of Inherent Upward I'timad in Air
Abu Hashim refutes al-Jubba'i by arguing air does not have necessary i'timad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's argument that air does not have a necessary upward i'timad and his refutation of al-Jubba'i's theory.

Core Idea
Air does not inherently rise; its apparent ascent is due to physical entrapment.

This lesson presents Abu Hashim's critique of al-Jubba'i's theory that air has an inherent upward tendency (i'timad). Abu Hashim uses the example of wood floating in water to argue that if air truly desired to ascend, it would separate from the wood. He then anticipates and refutes an objection about mixture causing adhesion.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice Abu Hashim's claim that air lacks necessary i'timad and his evidence from floating wood.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection starting with 'لا يقال' and understand the opponent's reasoning that mixture might create a bond.
3
Analyze the response
See how Abu Hashim distinguishes between air particles that are part of the mixture versus those merely trapped between wood fibers, showing that physical impediment, not inherent attachment, keeps the air with the wood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو هاشم : ليس للهواء اعتماد لازم ، ولو كان في طبعه صعوداً لانفصل عن أجزاء الخشبة التي في الماء، وصعد دون الخشبة ، إذ لا سبب عند الجبائي لصعودها وطفوها سوى تخلخل أجزائها وتشبت الهواء بها، لا يقال: يجوز أن يفيده التركيب حالة موجبة للتلازم وعدم الانفصال، سيما وهو هواء لم يبق على كيفيته المقتضية للانفصال لانكسار سورتها بالامتزاج، لأنـا نقول : الكلام في الأجزاء الهوائية المجاورة للأجزاء الخشبية لا التي صارت جزء الممتزج كما في سائر المركبات على ما يراه الفلاسفة، فالأقرب أن يقال : إن احتباسها فيما بين الأجزاء الخشبية منعها الانفصال، وأوجب الاستتباع
Abu Hashim said: Air does not have a necessary i'timad. If it were in its nature to ascend, it would separate from the parts of wood that are in water, and rise without the wood, since according to al-Jubba'i there is no cause for their rising and floating except the rarefaction of their parts and the attachment of air to them. It cannot be said: It is possible that the combination gives it a state necessitating adhesion and non-separation, especially since it is air that has not remained in its quality requiring separation because its force is broken by mixing, because we say: The discussion concerns the airy parts adjacent to the wooden parts, not those that have become part of the mixture as in other composites according to the philosophers. So the closest is to say: Their retention among the wooden parts prevented separation and necessitated following.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
Here Abu Hashim presents his counterargument. He observes that if air had an inherent upward tendency, logs floating in water would have the air bubbles separate from the wood and rise independently, but they do not. He then refutes a potential objection: perhaps the mixture of air and wood creates a bond that prevents separation. Abu Hashim responds that the air in question is not chemically mixed but merely trapped between wood fibers, which physically blocks its escape, not due to any inherent adhesion. The term 'الاستتباع' (being drawn along) describes how the air is carried with the wood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Air naturally adheres to wood, causing it to be carried upward.
Correction: Abu Hashim argues that air is merely physically trapped between wood fibers, not inherently attached.

Checkpoint
What is Abu Hashim's main evidence against inherent upward i'timad in air?
A) Air does not separate from wood in water.
B) Air is lighter than water.
C) Air always rises.
D) Wood sinks in water.

Key Takeaway
The force that air appears to have is not intrinsic but due to external constraints.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If even the physical world can mislead us into thinking air 'wants' to rise, how much more careful must we be when discerning our own motives and desires? Are you willing to question what you think you 'naturally' incline toward, and see if it's actually imposed from outside?

Is I'timad Lasting? Al-Jubba'i vs. Abu Hashim
Debate on the lastingness of i'timad between al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate between al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim over the persistence of i'timad, and the logical moves each makes.

Core Idea
The persistence of i'timad hinges on the distinction between necessary and variable types.

This lesson examines a classic kalam debate on whether i'timad (a mode of being) lasts over time. Al-Jubba'i argues that no i'timad persists, while Abu Hashim insists that necessary i'timad does, citing sensory evidence. Their exchange reveals deep assumptions about causality and analogy in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the debate passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the contrasting positions.
Pay attention to the technical term 'i'timad'.
2
Identify the two positions
Note what al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim each claim about i'timad.
3
Trace al-Jubba'i's two arguments
Understand the stone example and the analogy to sounds.
4
Examine the refutations
See how Abu Hashim's defenders counter each argument.
5
Ask why this debate matters
Reflect on how this debate relates to broader kalam discussions on attributes and persistence.
Consider the role of analogy (tamthil) in kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما قال الجبائي : إن الاعتماد غير باق لازماً كان أو مختلفاً، وقال أبو هاشم بل اللازم باق بحكم المشاهدة، كما في الألوان والطعوم، تمسك الجبائي بأن الإنسان إذا تحامل على حجر هابط فاعتماد المختلف غير باق فكذا اللازم لاشتراكهما في أخص أوصاف النفس، أعني كونه اعتماداً هابطاً، وبأن ما لا يبقى من الأعراض كالأصوات وغيرها، لا فرق فيها بين المقدور وغير المقدور ، فكذا في الاعتمادات التي مختلفها مقدوراً، ولازمها غير مقدور. ورد الأول بمنع كون أخص الأوصاف الاعتماد الهابط، بل الاعتماد اللازم، والثاني بأنه تمثيل بلا جامع
Among them is what al-Jubba'i said: I'timad is not lasting, whether necessary or variable. Abu Hashim said: Rather, the necessary kind is lasting, by the judgment of observation, as in colors and tastes. Al-Jubba'i argued that if a person leans on a descending stone, the variable i'timad is not lasting, so too the necessary, due to their sharing in the most specific description of the self, i.e., being a descending i'timad. And he also argued that among attributes that are not lasting, like sounds and others, there is no difference between the voluntary and involuntary, so too in i'timads: their variable kind is voluntary, and their necessary kind is involuntary. The first argument is refuted by denying that the most specific description is the descending i'timad; rather, it is the necessary i'timad. The second is refuted by saying it is an analogy without a commonality.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage addresses the persistence (baqa') of i'timad. Abu Hashim asserts that necessary i'timad (like the weight of a body) is lasting, comparable to colors and tastes. Al-Jubba'i rebuts with two arguments: (1) when a person leans on a descending stone, the variable i'timad (the lean) disappears, and since both necessary and variable share the property of 'descending i'timad', the necessary should also be non-lasting; (2) non-lasting accidents like sounds do not differentiate between voluntary and involuntary, so the same should apply to i'timads. The refutations are: (1) the shared property is not 'descending' but 'necessary'; (2) the analogy lacks a common feature, making it invalid.

Key Terms
اعتماد
باق
لازم
مختلف
حجر هابط
أخص الأوصاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All i'timad is equally non-lasting.
Correction: Abu Hashim argues that necessary i'timad (like weight) persists, while variable i'timad (like leaning) does not, depending on the type.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Abu Hashim use to argue that necessary i'timad is lasting?
A) Sounds and tastes.
B) Colors and tastes.
C) Motion and rest.
D) Light and heat.

Key Takeaway
The debate over i'timad's persistence reveals that logical analogy in kalam requires a genuine commonality, not just surface similarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you rely on things that you assume are permanent—like health, wealth, or relationships—without considering that even 'necessary' things might be transient? How does this debate challenge you to examine the foundations of your reliance?
?
Al-Jubba'i's argument failed because his analogy lacked a genuine commonality. Are there analogies you make about God or faith that you have not critically examined? What would it take for you to honestly test their validity?
?
When you observe the world, do you trust your senses to lead you to theological truth, as Abu Hashim did? What are the limits of empirical observation in matters of faith, and how do you guard against over-reliance on it?

Al-Jubba'i vs. Abu Hashim on I'timad as Cause
Al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim disagree on i'timad as generator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing views on whether i'timad generates motion and rest, and why both claims of exclusivity are considered weak.

Core Idea
Both motion and i'timad can be causes—no single cause monopolizes generation.

Sanusi presents a classical dispute between al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim over the causal role of i'timad. Al-Jubba'i insists only motion generates motion and rest, while Abu Hashim argues that i'timad does. Sanusi critiques both for lacking evidence, opening the door for multiple possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the views of al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how al-Jubba'i uses the example of opening a door or throwing a stone to argue that motion alone generates. Then see Abu Hashim's counterexample of a leaning pillar falling when its support is removed.
Both examples are meant to show exclusivity of causation.
2
Understand the critique of exclusivity
Sanusi dismisses both claims as weak because there is no evidence for exclusivity. The phrase 'إذ لا دلالة على الانحصار' is key: there is no proof that only one is the generator.
This critique opens the door to the possibility that either motion or i'timad can generate.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: if causality is not strictly one-way, then the relationship between i'timad, motion, and rest is more flexible than either scholar claimed.
This connects to broader debates about causality in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما قال الجبائي : إن الاعتماد لا يولد حركة ولا سكوناً، وإنما يولدهما الحركة، فان من فتح باباً، أو رمى حجراً إن لم تتحرك يده لم يتحرك المفتاح ولا الحجر ثم حركة المفتاح أو الحجر تولد سكونه في القصد. وقال أبو هاشم بل المولد لهما الاعتماد ، لأنه إذا نصب عمود قائم وادعم بدعامة واعتمد عليه إنسان إلى جهة الدعامة ثم أزيلت الدعامة فإن العمود يتحرك إلى جهتها ويسقط، وإن لم يتحرك المعتمد وكلاهما ضعيف، إذ لا دلالة على الانحصار، فيجوز أن يكون المولد هو الحركة تارة والاعتماد أخرى، وكذا ما قيل في أن المتولد هو الاعتماد دون الحركة، إن حركة الرامي متأخرة عن حركة الحجر، لأنه ما لم يندفع عن حيزه امتنع انتقال يد الرامي إليه، لاستحالة تداخل الجسمين، لأنه إن أريد التأخر بالزمان فاستحالة التداخل لا توجبه لجواز أن يكون اندفاع هذا وانتقال ذاك في زمان واحد، كما في أجزاء الحلقة التي تدور على نفسها، بل الأمر كذلك، وإلا لزم الانفصال، وإن أريد بالذات فالأمر بالعكس إذ ما لم تتحرك اليد لم يتحرك الحجر، ولهذا أن يقال : تحركت اليد فتحرك الحجر دون العكس، فالأقرب أن المولد للحركة والسكون قد يكون هو الحركة، وقد يكون الاعتماد فإنه يولد
Among them is what al-Jubba'i said: I'timad does not generate motion or rest; rather, motion generates them. For if someone opens a door or throws a stone, if his hand did not move, the key nor the stone would move, then the motion of the key or the stone generates its rest at the aim. Abu Hashim said: Rather, the generator of both is i'timad, because if an upright pillar is erected and supported by a support, and a person leans on it toward the support, then the support is removed, the pillar moves toward it and falls, even though the one leaning did not move. Both are weak, because there is no evidence for exclusivity, so it is possible that the generator is sometimes motion and sometimes i'timad. Similarly, what is said about that which is generated being i'timad and not motion: the motion of the archer is posterior to the motion of the stone, because as long as it has not moved from its place, it is impossible for the archer's hand to transfer to it, due to the impossibility of interpenetration of two bodies. If what is meant is temporal posteriority, impossibility of interpenetration does not necessitate it, because it is possible that this one's propulsion and that one's transfer occur in one moment, as in the parts of a ring that rotates on itself; indeed it is so, otherwise separation would be necessary. If what is meant is essential posteriority, then the opposite is the case, because as long as the hand did not move, the stone did not move, and for this reason it is said: the hand moved so the stone moved, not the opposite. So the closest is that the generator of motion and rest may be motion, and may be i'timad, for it generates.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage examines whether i'timad or motion is the cause (muwallid) of motion and rest. Al-Jubba'i argues that motion alone is causally effective: hand motion moves a door or stone, and that motion later generates rest. Abu Hashim counters with a pillar leaning on a support: when the support is removed, the pillar falls due to its i'timad (leaning), without any external motion. The author judges both as claiming exclusivity without proof, allowing that either can be the generator. The discussion then turns to an argument that the generated effect (mutawallad) is i'timad, not motion, using the archer's hand and stone. The author refutes this: temporally, both can occur simultaneously (as in a rotating ring); essentially, the hand moves first. Thus, motion can be the generator. The final conclusion remains that both motion and i'timad can generate, depending on the case.

Key Terms
الاعتماد
يولد
حركة
سكون
انحصار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think causality is strictly one-way, e.g., only motion causes motion/rest.
Correction: The author shows no proof for exclusivity; both motion and i'timad can be causes.

Checkpoint
What example does Abu Hashim use to show i'timad can generate motion?
A) A door opening.
B) A stone thrown by hand.
C) A leaning pillar falling when its support is removed.
D) An archer shooting an arrow.

Key Takeaway
The claim that only motion or only i'timad generates motion and rest is not proven; either can be the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the events of your life, do you ever assume that there is only one possible cause for what happens? How does this lesson's rejection of 'exclusivity' challenge your tendency to oversimplify God's actions?
?
In your own journey of faith, have you ever insisted that a certain means (like i'timad) cannot be used by God, only to later realize your limitation? What does Sanusi's humble critique teach you about admitting what you do not know?
?
Consider a recent blessing or trial: did you immediately attribute it to a single cause (e.g., your effort, someone's action)? Are you willing to see that God may work through multiple, even unexpected, causes—and does that change how you respond to Him?

The Archer's Hand and the Possibility of Simultaneous Causation
Refutes that i'timad is always generated, concludes both can generate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the argument that i'timad is always generated by motion, using simultaneous causation and essential priority.

Core Idea
Temporal simultaneity of cause and effect is possible, undermining exclusive claims about i'timad.

The discussion continues with a new argument that the generated effect is i'timad, not motion. The author refutes this by analyzing the archer's hand and stone. He shows that temporal posteriority is not necessary—both can occur at the same moment—and that essential posteriority actually supports motion as cause. The lesson concludes that both motion and i'timad can generate.

Guided Path
1
Read the archer argument and its refutation
Read the Arabic passage. Notice the claim that the archer's hand motion is posterior to the stone's motion, and then the author's response distinguishing temporal and essential posteriority.
The key point is the rotating ring example showing simultaneity.
2
Understand temporal vs. essential posteriority
Temporal posteriority means later in time; essential posteriority means dependent in existence. The author argues that impossibility of interpenetration does not force temporal posteriority, and that essentially the hand must move first.
This clarifies the causal order.
3
See the final conclusion
The author concludes that the generator can be motion or i'timad, depending on the case. This resolves the debate by allowing both possibilities.
The phrase 'فالأقرب أن المولد للحركة والسكون قد يكون هو الحركة، وقد يكون الاعتماد' is the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما قال الجبائي : إن الاعتماد لا يولد حركة ولا سكوناً، وإنما يولدهما الحركة، فان من فتح باباً، أو رمى حجراً إن لم تتحرك يده لم يتحرك المفتاح ولا الحجر ثم حركة المفتاح أو الحجر تولد سكونه في القصد. وقال أبو هاشم بل المولد لهما الاعتماد ، لأنه إذا نصب عمود قائم وادعم بدعامة واعتمد عليه إنسان إلى جهة الدعامة ثم أزيلت الدعامة فإن العمود يتحرك إلى جهتها ويسقط، وإن لم يتحرك المعتمد وكلاهما ضعيف، إذ لا دلالة على الانحصار، فيجوز أن يكون المولد هو الحركة تارة والاعتماد أخرى، وكذا ما قيل في أن المتولد هو الاعتماد دون الحركة، إن حركة الرامي متأخرة عن حركة الحجر، لأنه ما لم يندفع عن حيزه امتنع انتقال يد الرامي إليه، لاستحالة تداخل الجسمين، لأنه إن أريد التأخر بالزمان فاستحالة التداخل لا توجبه لجواز أن يكون اندفاع هذا وانتقال ذاك في زمان واحد، كما في أجزاء الحلقة التي تدور على نفسها، بل الأمر كذلك، وإلا لزم الانفصال، وإن أريد بالذات فالأمر بالعكس إذ ما لم تتحرك اليد لم يتحرك الحجر، ولهذا أن يقال : تحركت اليد فتحرك الحجر دون العكس، فالأقرب أن المولد للحركة والسكون قد يكون هو الحركة، وقد يكون الاعتماد فإنه يولد
Among them is what al-Jubba'i said: I'timad does not generate motion or rest; rather, motion generates them. For if someone opens a door or throws a stone, if his hand did not move, the key nor the stone would move, then the motion of the key or the stone generates its rest at the aim. Abu Hashim said: Rather, the generator of both is i'timad, because if an upright pillar is erected and supported by a support, and a person leans on it toward the support, then the support is removed, the pillar moves toward it and falls, even though the one leaning did not move. Both are weak, because there is no evidence for exclusivity, so it is possible that the generator is sometimes motion and sometimes i'timad. Similarly, what is said about that which is generated being i'timad and not motion: the motion of the archer is posterior to the motion of the stone, because as long as it has not moved from its place, it is impossible for the archer's hand to transfer to it, due to the impossibility of interpenetration of two bodies. If what is meant is temporal posteriority, impossibility of interpenetration does not necessitate it, because it is possible that this one's propulsion and that one's transfer occur in one moment, as in the parts of a ring that rotates on itself; indeed it is so, otherwise separation would be necessary. If what is meant is essential posteriority, then the opposite is the case, because as long as the hand did not move, the stone did not move, and for this reason it is said: the hand moved so the stone moved, not the opposite. So the closest is that the generator of motion and rest may be motion, and may be i'timad, for it generates.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage examines whether i'timad or motion is the cause (muwallid) of motion and rest. Al-Jubba'i argues that motion alone is causally effective: hand motion moves a door or stone, and that motion later generates rest. Abu Hashim counters with a pillar leaning on a support: when the support is removed, the pillar falls due to its i'timad (leaning), without any external motion. The author judges both as claiming exclusivity without proof, allowing that either can be the generator. The discussion then turns to an argument that the generated effect (mutawallad) is i'timad, not motion, using the archer's hand and stone. The author refutes this: temporally, both can occur simultaneously (as in a rotating ring); essentially, the hand moves first. Thus, motion can be the generator. The final conclusion remains that both motion and i'timad can generate, depending on the case.

Key Terms
رامي
تأخر بالزمان
تأخر بالذات
تداخل
اندفاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The archer argument proves i'timad is always generated by motion.
Correction: The author shows the argument fails because temporal posteriority is not necessary, and essential posteriority actually favors motion as cause. So i'timad can also be a generator.

Checkpoint

A) A door opening.
B) A stone thrown by hand.
C) Parts of a rotating ring.
D) A leaning pillar.

Key Takeaway
The final position is that both motion and i'timad can generate motion and rest, depending on the situation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows that sometimes cause and effect can be simultaneous, like the parts of a rotating ring. Are there relationships in your life (e.g., with family, work, worship) where you assume a delay between cause and effect, when in fact they occur together? ?
The archer's hand moves the stone—yet the hand's motion is dependent on the stone's motion. Do you ever feel stuck in a circle of causation, where you think your efforts need a prior condition to be effective? How can this lesson about simultaneous causation free you from waiting for the 'right time' to act?

Philosophers' Classification of Mayl
Classification of mayl into three types with properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how philosophers classify mayl (inclination) into three types and the key properties of each.

Core Idea
Mayl has three sources: nature, external force, and consciousness.

Philosophers analyze motion through the concept of inclination (mayl). They distinguish three types based on the origin of motion: natural tendency, forced push, and psychic or voluntary action. This passage explains these categories and some of their logical implications, including debates about whether mayl is identical to repulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Note the three types: natural, forced, psychic.
The passage uses logical conditionals to define each type.
2
Identify the three definitions
Find the criteria: if principle from outside → forced; if from within with consciousness → psychic; otherwise → natural.
Some philosophers expand psychic to include plants.
3
Examine the properties of natural mayl
Note that natural mayl disappears when body is in its natural place, and it cannot coexist with forced mayl in opposite directions.
Examples: stone pushed downward has both natural and forced mayl, speeding up motion.
4
Understand the debate about mudafa'a
The passage mentions hesitation whether mayl is the same as repulsion (mudafa'a) or its principle.
This is a finer point; not all learners need to master it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والفلاسفة يسمونه الميل، ويجعلونه طبيعياً وقسرياً ونفسانياً، لأن مبدأه إن كان من خارج فقسري، وإلا فإن كان مع شعور فنفساني وإلا فطبيعي، وبعضهم يخص الشعور بالإرادي ويجعل النفساني أعم منه لتناوله میل النبات التبرز والتزيد، ويدل على أن (۱) ترددهم في أن الميل نفس المدافعة، أو مبدأهما ما قالوا إن الطبيعي لا يوجد عند كون الجسم في حيزه، وإلا لكان مائلاً عنه لا إليه، وأنه لا يجامع القسري(۲) عند اختلاف الجهة لامتناع المدافعة إلى جهة مع (۳) التنحي عنها ، ويجامع عند اتحادها كما في الحجر المدفوع إلى أسفل )، ولذا كانت حر )، ولذا كانت حركته أسرع، وأن الحركة الصاعدة للحجر المرمى إلى فوق تشتد ابتداء وتضعف عند القرب
He said: The philosophers call it mayl, and they make it natural, forced, and psychic. Because its principle if from outside then forced; otherwise if with consciousness then psychic; otherwise natural. Some of them restrict consciousness to voluntary and make psychic more general, as it includes the mayl of plants for excretion and growth. It indicates that their hesitation regarding whether mayl is the same as repulsion (mudafa'a) or its principle is indicated by what they said: that the natural [mayl] does not exist when the body is in its natural place, otherwise it would be inclined away from it not towards it; and that it does not coexist with forced [mayl] when the direction differs, due to the impossibility of repulsion in one direction with retreat from it; and it coexists when they are united, as in the stone pushed downwards, and therefore its motion is faster; and that the upward motion of a stone thrown upwards intensifies initially and weakens when near [the peak].
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage summarizes the philosophers' concept of mayl (inclination). They classify it into three types based on origin: natural (innate tendency, e.g., downward), forced (imposed from outside, e.g., upward throw), and psychic (from consciousness, e.g., voluntary motion). Some expand psychic to include plant movements. The passage then discusses debates about whether mayl is identical to mudafa'a (repulsion) or its principle. Key properties: natural mayl is absent when a body is in its natural place; forced and natural mayl cannot coexist in opposite directions but can combine in the same direction (e.g., a stone pushed downward has both natural and forced mayl, resulting in faster motion). Additionally, upward motion of a thrown stone is described as accelerating initially but decelerating near the top.

?

The Muwallad: Movement or Reliance?
Debates what the muwallad (generated cause) is and concludes it can be movement or reliance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate among mutakallimun about whether the muwallad (generated cause) is movement or reliance, and the arguments for each position.

Core Idea
The generator of effects can be either movement or reliance—neither is exclusive.

This lesson examines a key debate in Islamic causal theory: what exactly is the 'generator' (muwallid) that produces generated effects (muwallad)? The classical view from Abu Hashim argues it is reliance (i'timād), while others point to movement. The passage from Sanusi's Sharh al-Maqasid critically analyzes both positions and concludes that either can serve as generator, depending on the case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the examples used: key/stone and column/leaning.
2
Identify the two competing views
Find where the passage presents the view that generator is movement, and where Abu Hashim presents reliance as generator. Note the arguments given for each.
The critique of exclusivity is central.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author rejects exclusivity and concludes that both movement and reliance can be muwallid. Reflect on why this matters for kalam causation theory.
The archer example refines the temporal vs essential posteriority distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حجراً إن لم تتحرك يده لم يتحرك المفتاح ولا الحجر ثم حركة المفتاح أو الحجر تولد سكونه في القصد. وقال أبو هاشم بل المولد لهما الاعتماد، لأنه إذا نصب عمود قائم وادعم بدعامة واعتمد عليه إنسان إلى جهة الدعامة ثم أزيلت الدعامة فإن العمود يتحرك إلى جهتها ويسقط، وإن لم يتحرك المعتمد وكلاهما ضعيف، إذ لا دلالة على الانحصار، فيجوز أن يكون المولد هو الحركة تارة والاعتماد أخرى، وكذا ما قيل في أن المتولد هو الاعتماد دون الحركة، إن حركة الرامي متأخرة عن حركة الحجر، لأنه ما لم يندفع عن حيزه امتنع انتقال يد الرامي إليه، لاستحالة تداخل الجسمين، لأنه إن أريد التأخر بالزمان فاستحالة التداخل لا توجبه لجواز أن يكون اندفاع هذا وانتقال ذاك في زمان واحد، كما في أجزاء الحلقة التي تدور على نفسها، بل الأمر كذلك، وإلا لزم الانفصال، وإن أريد بالذات فالأمر بالعكس إذ ما لم تتحرك اليد لم يتحرك الحجر، ولهذا أن يقال: تحركت اليد فتحرك الحجر دون العكس، فالأقرب أن المولد يصح للحركة والسكون قد يكون هو الحركة، وقد يكون الاعتماد فإنه يولد.
If a stone, if his hand does not move, the key and the stone do not move, then the movement of the key or the stone generates its stillness in the intention. Abu Hashim said: Rather, the generator for them both is reliance, because when a vertical column is erected and supported by a support, and a human leans on it toward the direction of the support, then the support is removed, the column moves toward its direction and falls, even though the one who leaned did not move. Both are weak, since there is no evidence for exclusivity, so it is permissible that the generator is sometimes movement and sometimes reliance. Likewise, what was said that the generated thing is reliance, not movement: that the movement of the archer is temporally posterior to the movement of the stone, because as long as the stone has not left its place, the transfer of the archer's hand to it is impossible due to the impossibility of interpenetration of bodies. If temporal posteriority is intended, the impossibility of interpenetration does not necessitate it, because it is possible that the stone's departure and that hand's transfer occur in one time, as in the parts of a ring that rotates on itself. Indeed, the matter is so, otherwise separation would be necessary. If essential posteriority is intended, the matter is the opposite, since unless the hand moves, the stone does not move. Therefore it is said: The hand moved, so the stone moved, not the reverse. The closest view is that the generator is valid for movement and stillness: sometimes it is movement, sometimes it is reliance, for it generates.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a theological-philosophical argument about the nature of the muwallad (generated cause). The author begins with an example: a hand not moving fails to move a key and a stone, then the movement of the key or stone generates stillness in the intention (a kind of effect). Abu Hashim then argues that the real generator (muwallid) for both is reliance (i'timād), giving an example of a column that falls when a support is removed even though the person leaning did not move. The author criticizes this as weak, saying there is no proof of exclusivity. Therefore, the generator may be movement in some cases and reliance in others. A further objection is raised: the archer's movement is temporally posterior to the stone's movement, but the author refutes this by noting that interpenetration does not force temporal priority—they could be simultaneous like the parts of a rotating ring. If essential posteriority is meant, the hand must move before the stone, so the generator is movement. The conclusion is that the generator can be either movement or reliance, whichever produces the effect.

مولد (muwallid)
اعتماد (i'timād)
حركة (haraka)
سكون (sukūn)

Misconception: Some might think muwallad always requires direct physical movement.
Correction: This passage shows that reliance (i'timād) can also generate effects, as in the column example.

Key Takeaway
The muwallad (generated cause) can be either movement or reliance, as both can generate effects, and there is no exclusive proof for either.

Philosophers' Threefold Classification of Mayl
Introduces mayl and its threefold classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' classification of mayl (inclination) into natural, compulsory, and psychic types, and the criteria used to distinguish them.

Core Idea
Mayl is classified by its source: external, internal with consciousness, or internal without.

Philosophers systematically classify the concept of mayl (inclination) based on its originating principle. This classification is foundational for understanding causation and motion in their thought. The passage below lays out the three types and the criteria that define them, along with some terminological refinements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation to grasp the full meaning.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the three types and their criteria
Find the three types: natural, compulsory, psychic. Note the condition: if principle is external → compulsory; if internal with consciousness → psychic; if internal without consciousness → natural.
The passage also mentions voluntary (irādī) as a subset of psychic.
3
Examine the examples given
The passage provides examples: arrow for compulsory, human reliance for psychic, stone for natural, plant growth for a variant. Understand why each fits its category.
Some examples may be clarified in the commentary.
4
Consider the terminological nuances
Note that some philosophers use 'voluntary' (irādī) instead of 'psychic' or make 'psychic' more inclusive. Also note the plant's inclination is sometimes considered composite.
This shows that classifications can have variations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والفلاسفة يسمونه الميل، ويجعلونه طبيعياً وقسرياً ونفسانياً، لأن مبدأه إن كان من خارج فقسري، وإلا فإن كان مع شعور فنفساني وإلا فطبيعي، وبعضهم يخص الشعور بالإرادي ويجعل النفساني أعم منه لتناوله ميل النبات التبرز والتزيد. أي الاعتماد ميلا ويقسمونه إلى الطبيعي والقسري والنفساني، لأن مبدأه وما ينبعث هو عنه، إن كان أمراً خارجاً عن محله فقسري كميل السهم المرمي إلى فوق، وإلا فإن كان مع قصد وشعور فنفساني كاعتماد الإنساني على غيره، وإلا فطبيعي سواء اقتضته القوة على وتيرة واحدة أبداً، كميل الحجر المسكن في الجو، أو اقتضته على وتائر مختلفة كميل النبات إلى التبرز والتزيد، ومنهم من سمى المقرون بالقصد والشعور إرادياً وجعل النفساني أعم منه ومن وتر أحد قسمي الطبيعي، أعني ما لا يكون على وتيرة واحدة لاختصاصه بذوات الأنفس فربما يختلف على حسب اقتضاء النفس، فبهذا الاعتبار يسمى ميل النبات نفسانياً، ومنهم من جعله خارجاً على الأقسام لكونه مركباً على ما سيأتي في بحث الحركة مع زيادة كلام في هذا الباب.
He said: The philosophers call it mayl (inclination), and make it natural, compulsory, and psychic, because if its principle is from outside, then compulsory; otherwise, if with consciousness, then psychic; otherwise natural. Some of them specify consciousness as voluntary (irādī) and make psychic more general, including the plant's inclination to void and growth. That is, it is reliance as inclination, and they divide it into natural, compulsory, and psychic, because its principle and that from which it emanates: if it is something external to its locus, then compulsory, like the inclination of an arrow shot upward; otherwise, if with intention and consciousness, then psychic, like a human's reliance on another; otherwise natural, whether the force requires it on a single uniform pattern always, like the inclination of a stone at rest in the air, or requires it on varying patterns, like the plant's inclination to void and growth. Some of them call what is coupled with intention and consciousness 'voluntary' and make psychic more general than it and than one of the two divisions of natural – namely, what is not on a uniform pattern because it is specific to ensouled beings, so it may vary according to the soul's exigency; by this consideration, the plant's inclination is called psychic. Some of them make it outside the divisions because it is composite, as will come in the discussion of movement with additional discussion in this chapter.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the concept of mayl (inclination) as used by philosophers. It opens by stating that philosophers call the generated cause 'mayl' and divide it into three types: natural (ṭabīʿī), compulsory (qasrī), and psychic (nafsānī). The classification is based on the source of the mayl: if the principle is external, it is compulsory (like an arrow's flight); if internal but accompanied by consciousness, it is psychic (like human reliance); if internal without consciousness, it is natural (like a stone's tendency). Some terminological nuance is added: some restrict consciousness to 'voluntary' (irādī) and make 'psychic' more general to include plant movements. A more detailed classification follows, repeating the three types with examples. Finally, there is a note that some scholars consider plant inclination as composite and postpone fuller discussion.

Correction: The passage shows that mayl includes psychic (conscious) and natural types, and even plants have a kind of psychic mayl according to some.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Two Properties that Reveal the Nature of Mayl
Lists properties of mayl showing hesitation about its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key properties of mayl that show whether it is the sensed impetus itself or its proximate principle.

Core Idea
Mayl's properties reveal its ambiguous nature: is it impetus or its cause?

The scholars of kalam present properties of mayl (inclination) that highlight their hesitation about its true nature. Is mayl the actual sensed impetus (mudāfaʿa) or its proximate principle (mabdaʾ qarīb)? This passage discusses two such properties: the absence of natural mayl in a body's natural place and the coexistence of natural and compulsory mayl. By examining these, we see the theoretical ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
The passage lists properties that indicate hesitation about mayl's nature. Read it once to get the overall flow.
The end is cut off; mark the potential missing part.
2
Understand the first property: no natural mayl in natural place
The passage argues that natural mayl does not exist in a body when it is in its natural place. If it did, it would lead to absurdities. This supports the view that mayl is the principle rather than the impetus.
The principle can have delayed effects due to missing conditions or obstacles.
3
Understand the second property: coexistence of mayl types
Natural and compulsory mayl cannot coexist toward opposite directions, but can coexist toward the same direction. A stone thrown upward has potential downward mayl.
This shows that mayl can be potential, not always actual.
4
Reflect on the hesitation
These properties show why scholars hesitated: both interpretations have strengths and weaknesses.
The passage does not resolve the issue but presents evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم قد ذكروا أحكاماً تدل على ترددهم في أن الميل نفس المدافعة المحسوسة، أو مبدؤها القريب الذي يوجد عند كون الحجر صاعداً الهواء، أو ساكناً على الأرض فيها أن الميل الطبيعي لا يوجد في الجسم عند كونه في حيزه الطبيعي، وإلا فإما أن يميل إليه فيلزم طلب حصول الحاصل، أو عنه فيلزم أن يكون المطلوب بالطبع متروكاً بالطبع، ولا يتأتى هذا في مبدأ الميل إذ ربما يتخلف الأثر عنه لفقد شرط، أو وجود مانع، ومنها أن الميل الطبيعي لا يجامع الميل القسري إلى جهتين، لأن امتناع المدافعة إلى جهة مع المدافعة عنها ضروري فالحجر المرمي إلى فوق لا يكون فيه مدافعة هابطة بالفعل، بل بالقوة، بمعنى أن من شأنه أن يوجد فيه ذلك عند زوال غلبة القوة القسرية وإما إلى جهة واحدة فقد يجتمعان كما في في.
Then they mentioned properties that indicate their hesitation concerning whether mayl is the sensed impetus itself or its proximate principle that exists when the stone is ascending the air or resting on the ground. Among them: natural mayl does not exist in a body when it is in its natural place; otherwise, either it would incline toward it, necessitating seeking what is already attained, or away from it, necessitating that what is naturally sought is naturally abandoned. This does not apply to the principle of mayl, for the effect may be delayed from it due to loss of a condition or presence of an obstacle. And among them: natural mayl does not coexist with compulsory mayl toward two directions, because the impossibility of impetus toward a direction with impetus away from it is necessary. Thus a stone thrown upward does not have a downward impetus in actuality, but in potentiality, meaning that it is its nature to have that when the dominance of the compulsory force ceases. But toward one direction they may coexist, as in [؟].
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents a series of 'ahkām' (properties) that reveal the scholars' hesitation about whether mayl (inclination) is the actual sensed impetus (mudāfaʿa) or its proximate cause (mabdaʾ qarīb). The first property: natural mayl does not exist in a body when it is in its natural place; if it did, it would either incline toward it (seeking what is already attained) or away (contradicting natural desire). This is not a problem if mayl is the principle, since the effect can be delayed by missing conditions or obstacles. The second property: natural and compulsory mayl cannot coexist toward opposite directions; a stone thrown upward has only potential downward mayl, which becomes actual when the compulsory force weakens. However, they can coexist toward the same direction (as in an example cut off). The properties illustrate the theoretical ambiguity.

Key Terms
ميل (mayl)
مدافعة (mudāfaʿa)
مبدأ قريب (mabdaʾ qarīb)
حيز طبيعي (ḥayyiz ṭabīʿī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mayl (inclination) is always an actual impetus (mudāfaʿa) in the body.
Correction: The passage shows that mayl can be potential (bil-quwwa) and that natural mayl is absent in the natural place, indicating it is not always an actual impetus.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if natural mayl existed in a body when it is in its natural place?
A) The body would move away from its natural place
B) It would lead to seeking what is already attained or abandoning what is naturally sought
C) The body would experience no change
D) The mayl would become compulsory

Key Takeaway
The properties of mayl—its absence in natural place and the difference in coexistence with compulsory mayl—show that mayl might be a principle rather than the actual impetus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The scholars hesitated about the nature of mayl despite clear properties. In what area of your faith do you feel a similar hesitation, and are you willing to sit with that uncertainty rather than forcing a simplistic answer?
?
When you face a paradox like natural mayl not existing in its natural place, do you tend to dismiss it or dig deeper? What does that reveal about your intellectual humility before Allah's creation?
?
If the proximate principle (mabdaʾ qarīb) can be delayed, what does that tell you about how you wait for answers to your prayers? Are you patient with the condition and obstacles, or do you demand immediate results?

Rulings on the Nature of Inclination and Its Coexistence
Enumerates two rulings on mayl and its relation to resistance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key rulings on natural and forced inclinations (mayl) and how they inform the debate on whether mayl is identical to resistance or its underlying principle.

Core Idea
Inclination is not mere resistance; its essence is revealed through rulings on natural and forced motions.

The scholars of kalām debated whether mayl (inclination) is the same as the sensible resistance we feel when pushing an object, or whether it is the intrinsic principle that causes that resistance. To answer this, they examined two specific cases: the state of a body in its natural place, and the interaction of natural and forced inclinations in the same direction. These rulings shed light on the subtle nature of mayl and its effects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the central question
Read the opening sentence: are the scholars hesitant about mayl being resistance or its principle? Notice the stakes of this debate.
This question underpins the entire passage.
2
Examine the first ruling
Natural inclination does not exist in a body when it is in its natural place. Why? Because otherwise it would seek what is already attained or abandon it.
This argument only works if mayl is the principle, not the resistance itself.
3
Reflect on the implication
The author notes that this reasoning applies to the principle (mabda') because effects can be delayed or blocked.
This supports the idea that mayl is a distinct principle.
4
Examine the second ruling
Natural and forced inclinations cannot coexist toward opposite directions, but they can coexist toward the same direction, as in a stone pushed downward.
Consider the example of a stone thrown upward vs. pushed downward.
5
Connect to the central question
These rulings confirm that mayl is a real principle distinct from its effects, and explain observable phenomena like faster motion when pushed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم قد ذكروا أحكاماً تدل على ترددهم في أن الميل نفس المدافعة المحسوسة، أو مبدؤها القريب الذي يوجد عند كون الحجر صاعداً (۳) الهواء، أو ساكناً على الأرض فيها أن الميل الطبيعي لا يوجد في الجسم عند كونه في حيزه الطبيعي، وإلا فإما أن يميل إليه فيلزم طلب حصول الحاصل، أو عنه فيلزم أن يكون المطلوب بالطبع متروكاً بالطبع، ولا يتأتى هذا في مبدأ الميل إذ ربما يتخلف الأثر عنه لفقد شرط، أو وجود مانع، ومنها أن الميل الطبيعي لا يجامع الميل القسري إلى جهتين، لأن امتناع المدافعة إلى جهة مع المدافعة عنها ضروري فالحجر المرمي إلى فوق لا يكون فيه مدافعة هابطة بالفعل، بل بالقوة، بمعنى أن من شأنه أن يوجد فيه ذلك عند زوال غلبة القوة القسرية وإما إلى جهة واحدة فقد يجتمعان كما في الحجر المدفوع إلى أسفل، فإن فيه مدافعة (1) هابطة يقتضيها الحجر إذا خلي وطبعه وأخرى أحدثها فيه القاسر على حسب قوته وقصده، ولهذا يكون حركته حينئذ أسرع مما إذا سقط بنفسه فهبط، وتتفاوت تلك السرعة بتفاوت قوة القاسر.
Then they have mentioned rulings that indicate their hesitation as to whether mayl is the sensed resistance itself, or its proximate principle which exists when the stone is ascending through the air, or stationary on the ground. Among them: that natural inclination does not exist in a body when it is in its natural place; otherwise it would either incline toward it, necessitating the request for the attainment of what is already attained, or away from it, necessitating that what is sought by nature is abandoned by nature. And this is not possible in the principle of inclination, because the effect may be delayed due to the lack of a condition or the presence of an obstacle. And among them: that natural inclination does not coexist with forced inclination toward two [opposite] directions, because the impossibility of resistance toward a direction together with resistance away from it is necessary. So the stone thrown upward does not have an actual downward resistance, but only potential, meaning that it is its nature to have that when the dominance of the forced force ceases. But toward one direction they may coexist, as in the stone pushed downward, for it has a downward resistance necessitated by the stone when left to its nature, and another caused by the pusher according to his force and intent; hence its motion then is faster than if it fell by itself, and that speed varies with the variation of the pusher's force.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings (aḥkām) that reveal the scholars' uncertainty about whether mayl is identical to sensible resistance (mudāfaʿa) or is its immediate cause. The first ruling: natural inclination does not exist in a body when it is in its natural place (e.g., a stone at rest on the ground). If it did, it would either incline toward its place (impossible, as it is already there) or away from it (impossible, as nature seeks its place). The author notes that this argument applies to the principle of mayl, not necessarily to its effect, which may be blocked. The second ruling: natural and forced inclinations cannot coexist toward two opposite directions simultaneously; a stone thrown upward has only potential downward resistance, actualized when the forced force weakens. However, they can coexist toward one direction, as in a stone pushed downward: both its natural downward tendency and the forced downward push combine, making it fall faster than if dropped naturally. The speed increases with the pusher's force.

Key Terms
مدافعة (mudāfaʿa)
مبدأ (mabda')
حيز طبيعي (ḥayz ṭabīʿī)
ميل قسري (mayl qasrī)
ميل طبيعي (mayl ṭabīʿī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that natural inclination is completely absent in a body in its natural place.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is not absent; rather, its effect is not realized because the goal is already attained, but the principle (mabda') may still exist.

Checkpoint
What happens when a stone is pushed downward?
A) Its natural inclination is cancelled.
B) Natural and forced inclinations combine, making it fall faster.
C) It moves slower than a dropped stone.
D) Only forced inclination exists.

Key Takeaway
The two rulings confirm that mayl is a real principle distinct from its effects, and they explain why a pushed object moves faster than a naturally falling one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your natural spiritual inclination is not actualized when you have already attained a good state (e.g., contentment), what specific practice will you implement today to prevent complacency and actively strive for growth?
?
The stone pushed downward moves faster because two forces combine. Identify one external pressure in your life that has accelerated your deen. Are you relying on that pressure, or are you cultivating your own natural inclination so it continues even when the pressure is gone?
?
Potential inclination is real but unseen—like the stone that will fall when the forced force ceases. What hidden potential good in your heart is waiting for the removal of a specific obstacle? What one step can you take this week to remove that obstacle?

The Stone Motion Argument for Natural Inclination
The author presents the stone motion argument for natural inclination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the stone's forced and natural motion demonstrates the existence of a natural inclination, challenging the philosophers' concept of nature.

Core Idea
The stone's motion reveals a natural resistance that grows as forced motion fades.

This passage presents the first of two arguments used to prove the existence of natural inclination. By analysing the stone's upward and downward motion, the philosophers attempt to show that a natural principle must be at work. We will follow their reasoning and later see how the Imam responds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage describing the stone's motion. Notice the description of how forced motion weakens and natural motion strengthens.
Pay attention to the phrase 'mabda' al-mudāfaʿa' (principle of resistance).
2
Identify the two types of motion
The passage distinguishes between forced (qasrī) and natural (ṭabīʿī) motion. Note how their intensities change over time.
3
Understand the role of air resistance
The forced inclination weakens due to collisions with air. This allows the natural inclination to increase until it overcomes.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does this prove natural inclination exists? Because the stone's return cannot be due to forced motion alone; something internal must take over.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما ذكروا في بيان سبب أن الحجر الذي يتحرك صاعداً. بالقسر، ثم ) يرجع هابطاً بالطبع، أن حركته القسرية تشتد ابتداء وتضعف عند القرب من النهاية، والطبيعية بالعكس، لأن ميله القسري يزداد ضعفاً بمصاكات تتصل عليه من مقاومة الهواء المخروق، فيزداد الميل الطبيعي أعني مبدأ المدافعة قوة إلى أن يتعادلا ثم يأخذ القسري في الانتقاص، والطبيعي في الغلبة فيأخذ حركته في الاشتداد ومنها استدلالهم على وجود الميل الطبيعي بأن الحجرين المرميين بقوة واحدة إذا اختلفا في الصغر والكبر اختلفت حركتاهما في السرعة والبطء وليس ذاك إلا لكون المقاوم الذي هو الميل الطبيعي أعني مبدأ المدافعة في الكبير أكثر منه في الصغير، لأن التقدير عدم التفاوت في الفاعل والقابل إلا بذلك . وأجاب الإمام : بأن الطبيعة قوة سارية في الجسم منقسمة بانقسامه فيكون في الكبير أكثر، وبزيادة المقاومة أجدر والفلاسفة يزعمون أنه أمر ثابت ليس مما يشتد ويضعف أو يقل ويكثر في الجسم الواحد حتى إن (۳) طبيعة كل الماء وبعضه واحد، ولا يتبين الحق من ذلك إلا بمعرفة حقيقة ما هو المراد بالطبيعة ها هنا، وهم لم يزيدوا على أن الطبيعة قد يقال لما يصدر عنه الحركة والسكون أولاً، وبالذات دون شعور وإرادة، وقد يقال لما تصدر عنه أمر لا تتخلف عنه ، ولا يفتقر الصدور إلى علة خارجة عنه كنزول الحجر إلى السفل، وقد يخص بما يصدر عنه الحركات على نهج واحد دون شعور، وقد تسمى كل قوة جسمانية طبيعة ، وشيء من ذلك لا يفيد معرفة حقيقية، وأما إطلاقها على المزاج، أو على الكيفية الغالبة ( ) من الكيفيات المتضادة، أو على الحرارة الغريزية، أو على النفس النباتية، أو نحو ذلك (ه) على ما يذكره الأطباء فمختص بالمركبات .
And among them is what they mentioned in explaining the reason that the stone which moves upward by force then returns downward by nature: that its forced motion intensifies at the beginning and weakens near the end, and the natural motion is opposite, because its forced inclination increasingly weakens due to collisions connected to it from the resistance of the air that is cut, so the natural inclination—that is, the principle of resistance—increases in strength until they become equal, then the forced begins to decrease and the natural to prevail, so its motion begins to intensify. And among them is their proof for the existence of natural inclination: that two stones thrown with equal force, if they differ in smallness and bigness, their motions differ in speed and slowness, and that is not except because the resistant—which is natural inclination, i.e., the principle of resistance—is greater in the big than in the small, because it is assumed there is no difference in the agent and the recipient except that. And the Imam (al-Razi) answered: that nature is a force flowing in the body, divisible by its division, so it is greater in the big, and more capable of increasing resistance. And the philosophers claim that it is a constant thing, not something that intensifies and weakens or decreases and increases in a single body, such that the nature of all water and some of it is one. And the truth of that is not apparent except by knowing the reality of what is meant by 'nature' here. And they (the philosophers) have not added beyond that nature may be said of that from which motion and rest proceed primarily and essentially without awareness and will, and may be said of that from which something proceeds that does not lag and does not depend on an external cause, like the descent of a stone downward, and may be specific to that from which motions proceed on a single course without awareness, and every bodily force may be called nature. None of that yields knowledge of its reality. As for its application to temperament, or to the dominant quality among opposing qualities, or to innate heat, or to the vegetative soul, or the like, as the physicians mention, it is specific to composites.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents two related arguments and the Imam's response. The first argument explains why a stone thrown upward slows near the apex and then falls faster: forced inclination weakens due to air resistance, while natural inclination strengthens until it overcomes. The second argument proves the existence of natural inclination by contrasting two stones of different sizes thrown with equal force; the larger stone moves slower because its natural inclination (which acts as resistance to forced motion) is greater due to its larger mass. The Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) responds that nature is a divisible force pervading the body, so indeed the larger stone has more nature and thus more resistance. The philosophers, however, claim nature is indivisible and uniform, e.g., all water has the same nature. The author (Taftazani) dismisses this, noting that the term 'nature' has multiple definitions (e.g., source of motion without awareness, uniform motion, etc.) but none provide a true essence. He distinguishes the philosophers' usage from that of physicians, who apply nature to composite things like temperament. The conclusion is that the meaning of nature must be clarified to resolve the debate.

Key Terms
طبيعة (ṭabīʿa)
مبدأ المدافعة (mabda' al-mudāfaʿa)
قسر (qasr)
طبع (ṭabʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the stone's return is purely due to external forces like gravity without an internal principle.
Correction: The passage argues that the stone has an internal natural inclination that resists forced motion and eventually prevails.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the stone's forced motion weaken?
A) Because natural inclination cancels it out
B) Because of collisions with air
C) Because the stone's nature changes
D) Because the stone loses energy

Key Takeaway
The stone's motion pattern proves an internal natural inclination that opposes forced motion and ultimately prevails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel forced to act against your true nature—whether by peer pressure, societal expectations, or your own desires—where do you find that inner principle of resistance? Have you experienced it growing stronger as the forced pressure weakens? What would it take for you to let your true nature prevail, like the stone finally falling?
?
The stone's forced motion weakens due to collisions. What are the 'collisions' in your life—hardships, reminders, consequences—that gradually erode your resistance to returning to what is natural and right? Are you allowing them to do their work, or are you resisting the resistance?
?
The stone's natural inclination is always present, even when forced upward. In your daily life, what is the one natural inclination of your soul (toward truth, justice, worship) that you have been suppressing? What would it look like to let it prevail today?

The Two-Stone Argument and al-Razi's Response
The second argument for natural inclination and the Imam's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the differing speeds of stones of different sizes prove that nature is a divisible force, and how Imam al-Razi counters the philosophers' objection.

Core Idea
Size affects motion because nature divides with the body.

The philosophers present a second proof: two stones thrown with equal force but different sizes move at different speeds. They argue that the difference is due to natural inclination being greater in the larger stone. Imam al-Razi confirms this but adds that nature is a divisible force, contrary to the philosophers who claim nature is indivisible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about two stones
Read the Arabic for the second argument and the Imam's response. Note how the argument uses the assumption of equal force and differing sizes.
2
Understand the reasoning
Why does the larger stone move slower? Because its natural inclination (which resists forced motion) is greater. The passage says that except for the difference in size, all conditions are equal.
3
Examine the Imam's answer
Al-Razi says nature is a force that flows through the body and divides as the body divides. Therefore, a larger body has more nature and more resistance, which explains the slower speed.
4
Contrast with the philosophers' view
The philosophers think nature is indivisible and constant, so size should not affect motion. This sets up the debate.

Source Text

And among them is what they mentioned in explaining the reason that the stone which moves upward by force then returns downward by nature: that its forced motion intensifies at the beginning and weakens near the end, and the natural motion is opposite, because its forced inclination increasingly weakens due to collisions connected to it from the resistance of the air that is cut, so the natural inclination—that is, the principle of resistance—increases in strength until they become equal, then the forced begins to decrease and the natural to prevail, so its motion begins to intensify. Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents two related arguments and the Imam's response. The first argument explains why a stone thrown upward slows near the apex and then falls faster: forced inclination weakens due to air resistance, while natural inclination strengthens until it overcomes. The second argument proves the existence of natural inclination by contrasting two stones of different sizes thrown with equal force; the larger stone moves slower because its natural inclination (which acts as resistance to forced motion) is greater due to its larger mass. The Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) responds that nature is a divisible force pervading the body, so indeed the larger stone has more nature and thus more resistance. The philosophers, however, claim nature is indivisible and uniform, e.g., all water has the same nature. The author (Taftazani) dismisses this, noting that the term 'nature' has multiple definitions (e.g., source of motion without awareness, uniform motion, etc.) but none provide a true essence. He distinguishes the philosophers' usage from that of physicians, who apply nature to composite things like temperament. The conclusion is that the meaning of nature must be clarified to resolve the debate.

Key Terms
مبدأ المدافعة (mabda' al-mudāfaʿa)
قوة سارية (quwwa sāriya)
مقاومة (muqāwama)
انقسام (inqisām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the larger stone should move faster because it's heavier, but here it's about resistance to forced motion.
Correction: The larger stone has more natural inclination, which resists forced motion, making it slower.

Checkpoint
What is the key point of the Imam's response regarding nature?
A) Nature is indivisible
B) Nature is a force that divides with the body
C) Nature is constant in all bodies
D) Nature is not affected by size

Key Takeaway
The two-stone argument shows that natural inclination varies with size, supporting the view that nature is a divisible force.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The larger stone resists motion more because it has more natural inclination. What is the 'natural inclination' in your heart that resists turning to Allah? Is it greater when your ego is large? When have you felt that your own 'bigness' (pride, knowledge, status) made you slower to submit to truth?
?
Al-Razi says nature divides with the body. Do you treat your faith as a fixed, indivisible essence, or do you allow it to be distributed among all your actions? How can you actively 'divide' your devotion to cover every part of your life starting today?
?
The philosophers argued that greater resistance proves natural inclination. How often do you resist God's call because of your own 'natural inclination'? What is the size of your resistance right now, and are you willing to ask Allah to shrink it?

Critique of the Philosophers' Concept of Nature
The author critiques the philosophers' definitions of nature and distinguishes their usage from physicians'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author dismisses the philosophers' definitions of nature as insufficient and why clarifying the term is essential for the debate.

Core Idea
Without a clear definition, the debate on nature is meaningless.

Here the passage moves from proofs to a conceptual critique. The philosophers claim nature is indivisible and constant, but the author (Taftazani) points out that the term 'nature' is ambiguous with many definitions, none of which reveal its true essence. He also distinguishes the philosophers' usage from the physicians'.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
They claim nature is a constant thing that does not intensify or weaken in a single body, using water as an example.
2
Examine the author's critique
The author says that to judge the truth, we need to know the reality of 'nature'. The philosophers offer multiple definitions, but none give a true essence.
3
List the definitions given
The passage enumerates several meanings: source of motion without awareness, something that produces invariable effects, etc. Note each one.
4
Understand the final distinction
The author says that when physicians use 'nature', it applies specifically to composites (e.g., temperament, heat), not to simple bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما ذكروا في بيان سبب أن الحجر الذي يتحرك صاعداً. بالقسر، ثم ) يرجع هابطاً بالطبع، أن حركته القسرية تشتد ابتداء وتضعف عند القرب من النهاية، والطبيعية بالعكس، لأن ميله القسري يزداد ضعفاً بمصاكات تتصل عليه من مقاومة الهواء المخروق، فيزداد الميل الطبيعي أعني مبدأ المدافعة قوة إلى أن يتعادلا ثم يأخذ القسري في الانتقاص، والطبيعي في الغلبة فيأخذ حركته في الاشتداد ومنها استدلالهم على وجود الميل الطبيعي بأن الحجرين المرميين بقوة واحدة إذا اختلفا في الصغر والكبر اختلفت حركتاهما في السرعة والبطء وليس ذاك إلا لكون المقاوم الذي هو الميل الطبيعي أعني مبدأ المدافعة في الكبير أكثر منه في الصغير، لأن التقدير عدم التفاوت في الفاعل والقابل إلا بذلك . وأجاب الإمام : بأن الطبيعة قوة سارية في الجسم منقسمة بانقسامه فيكون في الكبير أكثر، وبزيادة المقاومة أجدر والفلاسفة يزعمون أنه أمر ثابت ليس مما يشتد ويضعف أو يقل ويكثر في الجسم الواحد حتى إن (۳) طبيعة كل الماء وبعضه واحد، ولا يتبين الحق من ذلك إلا بمعرفة حقيقة ما هو المراد بالطبيعة ها هنا، وهم لم يزيدوا على أن الطبيعة قد يقال لما يصدر عنه الحركة والسكون أولاً، وبالذات دون شعور وإرادة، وقد يقال لما تصدر عنه أمر لا تتخلف عنه ، ولا يفتقر الصدور إلى علة خارجة عنه كنزول الحجر إلى السفل، وقد يخص بما يصدر عنه الحركات على نهج واحد دون شعور، وقد تسمى كل قوة جسمانية طبيعة ، وشيء من ذلك لا يفيد معرفة حقيقية، وأما إطلاقها على المزاج، أو على الكيفية الغالبة ( ) من الكيفيات المتضادة، أو على الحرارة الغريزية، أو على النفس النباتية، أو نحو ذلك (ه) على ما يذكره الأطباء فمختص بالمركبات .
And among them is what they mentioned in explaining the reason that the stone which moves upward by force then returns downward by nature: that its forced motion intensifies at the beginning and weakens near the end, and the natural motion is opposite, because its forced inclination increasingly weakens due to collisions connected to it from the resistance of the air that is cut, so the natural inclination—that is, the principle of resistance—increases in strength until they become equal, then the forced begins to decrease and the natural to prevail, so its motion begins to intensify. And among them is their proof for the existence of natural inclination: that two stones thrown with equal force, if they differ in smallness and bigness, their motions differ in speed and slowness, and that is not except because the resistant—which is natural inclination, i.e., the principle of resistance—is greater in the big than in the small, because it is assumed there is no difference in the agent and the recipient except that. And the Imam (al-Razi) answered: that nature is a force flowing in the body, divisible by its division, so it is greater in the big, and more capable of increasing resistance. And the philosophers claim that it is a constant thing, not something that intensifies and weakens or decreases and increases in a single body, such that the nature of all water and some of it is one. And the truth of that is not apparent except by knowing the reality of what is meant by 'nature' here. And they (the philosophers) have not added beyond that nature may be said of that from which motion and rest proceed primarily and essentially without awareness and will, and may be said of that from which something proceeds that does not lag and does not depend on an external cause, like the descent of a stone downward, and may be specific to that from which motions proceed on a single course without awareness, and every bodily force may be called nature. None of that yields knowledge of its reality. As for its application to temperament, or to the dominant quality among opposing qualities, or to innate heat, or to the vegetative soul, or the like, as the physicians mention, it is specific to composites.
