Philosophers' Argument That Sound Is Carried by Air
Al-Taftazani presents the philosophers' empirical evidence for sound transmission via air.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the empirical evidence used by philosophers to support their claim that sound is physically carried by air to the ear.

Core Idea
Sound is not heard unless the air carrying it physically reaches the ear.

The philosophers of natural science argued that hearing depends on the movement of air from the source to the eardrum. In this passage, Al-Taftazani presents their key empirical observations — from wind direction to echoes — that together build an intuitive certainty. This lesson examines each piece of evidence and the logic behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the list of empirical indicators.
2
Identify the core claim
The philosophers assert that sound reaches the ear by means of air carrying it. Look for the statement that shows this.
The passage opens with the claim.
3
Examine the first evidence
The first piece of evidence is that sound inclines with the wind. Ask yourself: if sound travelled independently, why would wind affect it?
This is the most direct observation.
4
Consider the propagation model
The philosophers explain that sound conditions the air, and this condition propagates in all directions. Only ears within reach hear it.
This explains how sound travels even without wind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رأي الفلاسفة في إدراك وصول الصوت بالهواء أنه قال : ويدل على كون إدراكه بوصول الهواء الحامل له إلى الصماخ يميل مع الرياح، وأن من تكلم في طرف أنبوبة طرفها الآخر على أذن واحد من القوم ينفرد هو بسماعه وأنه متأخر بحسب الزمان عن مشاهدة سببه، كضرب الفأس من بعيد وأمثال هذه إمارات لا يستبعد إفادتها اليقين الحدسي، وإن لم تقم حجة على الغير إلا أنه لو استبعد قيام أمثال تلك الكيفية بجميع أجزاء الهواء وبقاؤها على هيئاتها مع هبوب الرياح والنفوذ في المنافذ صلب الأجسام لم يكن بعيداً، وكذا رجوعه عن مصادمة الجسم الأملس على ما زعموا في الصدى سواء جعل الواصل نفس الهواء الراجع، أو آخر متكيفاً بكيفية على ما هو الظاهر. رأي الفلاسفة أنه إذا وجد سبب الصوت في موضع تكيف هواء ذلك الموضع بذلك الصوت، ثم المجاور فالمجار في جميع الجهات إلى حد ما شدة الصوت وضعفه ولا يسمعه إلا المسامع التي تقع في تلك المسافة، ويصل إليها ذلك الهواء، وتمسكوا بوجوه : الأول : أن الصوت يميل مع هبوب الريح ولا يسمعه من كان الهبوب من جهته لعدم وصول الهواء إلى صماخه، فلو لم يكن الهواء حاملا له ولم يتوقف السماع على وصول ذلك الهواء لما كان كذلك
The philosophers' view on the perception of sound reaching via air: He said: It indicates that its perception is by the arrival of the air carrying it to the eardrum, because it inclines with the winds, and because one who speaks at one end of a tube whose other end is on the ear of one person among a group, that person alone hears it, and that it is delayed in time relative to the observation of its cause, like the striking of an axe from a distance. And such signs are not improbable to provide intuitive certainty, even if they do not constitute a proof for others. However, if one deemed improbable the occurrence of such qualities in all parts of the air and their persistence in their states despite the blowing of winds and penetration through passages of solid bodies, that would not be far-fetched. Likewise, its return from collision with a smooth body as they claim in the case of echo, whether one considers the arriving air to be the same returning air or another conditioned by a quality, as is apparent. The philosophers' view: that when the cause of sound occurs in a place, the air of that place becomes conditioned by that sound, then the adjacent, then the adjacent in all directions according to the intensity and weakness of the sound, and only those ears that are in that distance hear it, and that air reaches them. They adhered to [several] aspects: First: that sound inclines with the blowing of the wind, and one from whose direction the wind blows does not hear it due to the lack of air reaching his eardrum. If the air were not carrying it and hearing did not depend on that air's arrival, it would not be so.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument that sound is perceived by means of air carrying it to the eardrum. The author begins by listing empirical indicators: sound inclines with the wind; if a person speaks into one end of a tube placed at another person's ear, only that person hears it; sound is temporally delayed after its cause, like the sound of an axe hitting from afar. These provide intuitive certainty (yaqīn ḥadsī). The author then notes that even if one doubts that air can retain such qualities across all its parts despite wind and obstacles, this is not improbable. He also mentions the phenomenon of echo, where sound returns from a smooth surface. The philosophers further explain that when a sound occurs, the air at that place becomes conditioned by the sound, and this condition propagates through adjacent air in all directions, and only ears in that path hear it. They present specific evidence: the first is that sound is carried by wind, so one positioned upwind does not hear it because the air does not reach his ear, proving that the arrival of air is necessary for hearing.

Key Terms
هواء حامل
صماخ
صدى
يقين حدسي
أنبوبة
هبوب الرياح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think sound travels directly through air without the air itself moving; but the philosophers argue that the air carrying the sound must physically reach the ear.
Correction: The passage shows that if air does not reach the eardrum (e.g., due to wind direction), sound is not heard, proving that air must physically convey the sound.

Checkpoint
What is the first evidence the philosophers use to support their view that sound is carried by air?
A) Echo phenomena.
B) Sound being delayed after its cause.
C) Sound inclining with the wind and not being heard from the wind's direction.
D) The use of tubes to direct sound.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers used observable phenomena like wind carrying sound to argue that air physically conveys sound to the ear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers argue that the air carrying the sound must physically reach your ear for you to hear. When you hear the call to prayer or the recitation of the Qur'an, do you ever pause to consider the physical journey of that sound from the source to your ears? How does this awareness affect your sense of gratitude for the precise means by which you receive guidance?
?
You have experienced the delay of sound after seeing a distant cause, like an axe strike or a clap. Does that split-second gap ever make you reflect on the chain of causes that must align for you to hear? How does this ordered sequence point to the necessity of a single Cause who set all things in motion?
?
If the wind blows away the sound of the Qur'an from your direction, would you still feel accountable for having heard it? How does the physical reality of sound transmission challenge you to actively place yourself in positions where the truth reaches your ears, rather than passively waiting for it?

The Philosophers' Three Evidences for Sound via Air
Al-Taftazani presents the philosophers' three empirical evidences for sound perception via air.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three empirical evidences the philosophers used to argue that sound is perceived through the arrival of air, and the epistemic status of such indications.

Core Idea
Three everyday observations point to air as sound's carrier, but they yield intuitive certainty, not proof.

Al-Taftazani here summarizes the philosophers' case for sound being carried by air. He presents three simple, observable phenomena—wind, tubes, and echo delays—as indications that hearing depends on air reaching the ear. But he also notes a crucial limitation: these signs produce intuitive certainty for the observer, not a demonstrative proof that can compel others. Let's examine each evidence and its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' core claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Taftazani first states the general position before listing the three evidences.
The passage begins with 'رأي الفلاسفة...' and then 'تمسكوا بوجوه'.
2
Identify the three evidences
Look for the three numbered aspects. For each, understand how it shows that hearing depends on air reaching the ear.
Wind evidence: sound inclines with wind, upwind person doesn't hear. Tube evidence: only the person with the tube end in his ear hears. Delay evidence: sound is heard after a delay proportional to distance.
3
Grasp the epistemic limitation
Al-Taftazani explicitly states that these are 'إمارات' (indications) that yield intuitive certainty but not a proof for others. Understand why this distinction matters in theological argumentation.
The phrase 'وإن لم تقم حجة على الغير' is key.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why these particular observations are chosen and how they collectively support the air-carrier theory.
If air were not the carrier, why would wind affect audibility? Why would a tube isolate the sound? Why would distance cause delay?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رأي الفلاسفة في إدراك وصول الصوت بالهواء أنه قال : ويدل على كون إدراكه بوصول الهواء الحامل له إلى الصماخ يميل مع الرياح، وأن من تكلم في طرف أنبوبة طرفها الآخر على أذن واحد من القوم ينفرد هو بسماعه وأنه متأخر بحسب الزمان عن مشاهدة سببه، كضرب الفأس من بعيد وأمثال هذه إمارات لا يستبعد إفادتها اليقين الحدسي، وإن لم تقم حجة على الغير إلا أنه لو استبعد قيام أمثال تلك الكيفية بجميع أجزاء الهواء وبقاؤها على هيئاتها مع هبوب الرياح والنفوذ في المنافذ صلب الأجسام لم يكن بعيدا ، وكذا رجوعه عن مصادمة الجسم الأملس على ما زعموا في الصدى سواء جعل الواصل نفس الهواء الراجع، أو آخر متكيفا بكيفية على ما هو الظاهر. من رأي الفلاسفة أنه إذا وجد سبب الصوت في موضع تكيف هواء ذلك الموضع بذلك الصوت، ثم المجاور فالمجار في جميع الجهات إلى حد ما شدة الصوت وضعفه ولا يسمعه إلا المسامع التي تقع في تلك المسافة، ويصل إليها ذلك الهواء، وتمسكوا بوجوه : الأول : أن الصوت يميل مع هبوب الريح ولا يسمعه من كان الهبوب من جهته لعدم وصول الهواء إلى صماخه، فلو لم يكن الهواء حاملا له ولم يتوقف السماع على وصول ذلك الهواء لما كان كذلك . الثاني : أن من وضع طرف أنبوبة في فمه وطرفها الآخر في صماخ إنسان، وتكلم منها بصوت عال سمعه ذلك الإنسان دون غيره من الحاضرين وما ذلك إلا بمنع الأنبوبة وصول الهواء الحامل للصوت إلى أصمختهم . الثالث: أنا نرى سبب الصوت كضرب الفأس على الخشبة مثلا، ويتأخر سماع الصوت عنه زمانا يتفاوت بحسب تفاوت المسافة قربا وبعدا، فلو لا أن السماع يتوقف على وصول الهواء لما كان كذلك .
The philosophers' view on perceiving the arrival of sound through air: that he said: And it indicates that its perception is by the arrival of the air carrying it to the ear canal; it inclines with the winds, and that whoever speaks at the end of a tube whose other end is at the ear of one of the people, he alone hears it, and that it is delayed in time compared to observing its cause, like striking an axe from afar. And the likes of these are indications whose yielding of intuitive certainty is not improbable, even if they do not constitute a proof for others. However, if one were to consider improbable the subsistence of such qualities in all parts of air and their remaining in their states with the blowing of winds and penetration into the passages of hard bodies, that would not be far-fetched. Likewise, its returning from impact with a smooth body as they claim regarding echo, whether one makes the arriving air itself the returning one, or another air qualified by a quality as is apparent. From the philosophers' view: that when a cause of sound exists in a place, the air of that place becomes qualified with that sound, then the adjacent air [becomes qualified] in all directions up to a limit according to the intensity and weakness of the sound, and only the ears that fall in that distance and to which that air reaches hear it. And they adhered to [the following] aspects: First: that sound inclines with the blowing of wind and is not heard by one from whose direction the wind blows due to the non-arrival of air to his ear canal. So if the air were not carrying it and hearing did not depend on the arrival of that air, it would not be so. Second: that one who places the end of a tube in his mouth and its other end in the ear of a person and speaks into it with a loud voice – that person hears it without the others present, and that is only due to the tube preventing the arrival of the air carrying the sound to their ears. Third: that we see the cause of sound, like striking an axe on wood, and the hearing of sound is delayed in time varying according to the distance, near or far. So if hearing did not depend on the arrival of air, it would not be so.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' view on sound perception and the three empirical evidences they use to support it. Al-Taftazani first summarizes the core claim: sound is perceived because the air carrying it reaches the ear drum (الصماخ). He then lists three observations that serve as indications (إمارات) for this, noting that while these may yield intuitive certainty (اليقين الحدسي) for the observer, they do not constitute a conclusive proof (حجة) for others. The first evidence is that sound inclines with the wind, and a person upwind does not hear it because the air does not reach his ear. The second is the tube experiment: a person speaking into a tube heard only by the one whose ear is at the other end, because the tube blocks the air from reaching others. The third is the temporal delay between observing the cause (e.g., striking an axe) and hearing the sound, which varies with distance; this delay would not occur if hearing did not depend on the air reaching the ear. The philosophers conclude that these signs strongly suggest that sound is carried by air.

Key Terms
الصوت (sound)
الهواء (air)
الصماخ (ear canal)
اليقين الحدسي (intuitive certainty)
الإمارات (indications)
الحجة (proof)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophers' three evidences are considered definitive proofs that compel acceptance.
Correction: Al-Taftazani explicitly states they are indications (إمارات) that yield intuitive certainty for the observer but do not constitute a proof (حجة) for others.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three evidences used by the philosophers to support the view that sound is perceived via air?
A) Sound inclines with the wind and is not heard by someone upwind.
B) A tube allows only one person to hear a voice spoken into it.
C) Sound travels faster than light.
D) Hearing is delayed after seeing the cause of sound.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' three empirical observations—wind, tube, and delay—strongly suggest that sound is carried by air, but they yield only intuitive certainty, not definitive proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers' evidences give you intuitive certainty about sound, but not proof. What beliefs in your own life do you hold with intuitive certainty that might not survive scrutiny? Are you willing to examine them honestly before Allah?

?

In your daily life, where have you become so accustomed to the regularities of nature that you have forgotten they are sustained moment by moment by Allah? What would change if you woke up tomorrow and found air no longer carried sound? Would your faith stand?

Correlation Does Not Prove Causation
Al-Taftazani refutes the philosophers' three evidences, arguing they only show correlation not causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' evidences for sound being carried by air are insufficient, and the difference between correlation and causation in theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Correlation is not causation; intuitive certainty is not dialectical proof.

Al-Taftazani now delivers the refutation of the philosophers' three evidences. He argues that all they show is correlation — sound occurs together with air — but that does not prove that air causes sound. This is a crucial lesson in distinguishing between empirical observation and logical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the key term 'correlation' (al-dawarān) and how it is used to undermine the proofs.
2
Identify the three examples
The passage mentions three phenomena: the inclination of sound with wind, the tube experiment, and the delay of hearing after striking an axe. Identify each one.
These are the philosophers' original evidences.
3
Understand the core objection
Al-Taftazani argues that all these evidences rely on correlation (الدوران). Correlation does not yield certainty of causality (السببية) because an unknown cause could be responsible.
This is the central logical point.
4
Appreciate the nuance
Notice that the passage grants these indications may produce intuitive certainty (اليقين الحدسي) for the observer, but not a logical proof (حجة) against an opponent.
This refines the refutation without denying all value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب عن الكل بأن غايتها الدوران وهو لا يفيد القطع بالسببية، فيجوز أن يكون ميل الصوت مع الرياح واختصاص صاحب الأنبوبة بالسماع وتأخر السماع عن ضرب الفأس بسبب آخر فلا يدل توقف السماع على وصل هواء حامل للصوت. والحق أن هذه أمارات ربما تفيد اليقين الحدسي للناظر وإن لم تقم حجة على المناظر
And it was answered about all of them that their limit is correlation (al-dawarān) and it does not yield certainty of causality; so it is possible that the inclination of sound with the winds, the exclusivity of the tube owner's hearing, and the delay of hearing after striking the axe are due to another cause, so it does not prove that hearing depends on the arrival of air carrying the sound. And the truth is that these are indications that might yield intuitive certainty for the observer, even though they do not constitute a proof against the opponent.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage gives the answer (الجواب) to the philosophers' three evidences. Al-Taftazani states that the core problem is that these evidences only establish correlation (الدوران) — the phenomenon co-occurs with the arrival of air — but correlation does not prove causation (السببية). It is possible that the sound's inclination with the wind, the tube experiment, and the delay are due to some other unknown cause, and not necessarily because hearing depends on the air carrying the sound. However, he acknowledges that these indications (أمارات) may provide intuitive certainty (اليقين الحدسي) for the one who observes them directly, but they do not constitute a logical proof (حجة) that can compel an opponent in a dialectical debate.

Key Terms
الدوران (correlation)
السببية (causality)
اليقين الحدسي (intuitive certainty)
الحجة (proof)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the answer completely denies any role of air in sound perception.
Correction: It only denies that the evidences prove causation definitively; air may still be involved, but not proven by these correlations.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are based on faulty experiments.
B) Because they only show correlation, not causation.
C) Because sound is not real.
D) Because air cannot carry sound.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' evidences only demonstrate correlation, not causation, so they fail as proofs, though they may provide intuitive certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How can you avoid mistaking your intuitive certainty for an irrefutable proof, following the example of Al-Taftazani's honesty about the limits of the evidence?

Two Arguments Against the Necessity of Air for Hearing
Al-Taftazani presents two arguments against the necessity of air for hearing, with an answer to the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments used by al-Taftazani against the view that hearing depends on air reaching the ear, and the response to the first argument.

Core Idea
Sound perception is analogous to vision: we perceive the distant source, not just the arriving air.

In this passage, al-Taftazani challenges a philosophical claim about how sound is perceived. He presents two logical difficulties that arise if we insist that sound requires air to travel to the ear. The first argument is accompanied by an answer that draws an analogy to vision, while the second stands unanswered in this excerpt. This lesson will walk through each step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English translation to get an overview of the arguments and the answer.
Pay attention to the structure: arguments labelled 'first' and 'second'.
2
Examine the first argument
Notice the reasoning: if hearing required air to arrive, we could not perceive direction or distance, because only air in the ear canal arrives.
Consider why direction and distance perception would be problematic.
3
Study the answer to the first argument
Understand the analogy to vision: the perceived object is not limited to the arriving air but includes the distant source, just as in vision we see distant objects even though light is local.
Compare with the sense of touch mentioned in the answer.
4
Consider the second argument
Observe the empirical observation: when wind blows, the sound of the mu'adhdhin seems to incline away from us, suggesting sound does not travel straight via air.
Note that no answer is given for this argument in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل على بطلان توقف السماع على وصول الهواء الحامل بوجوه : الأول : أنه لو كان كذلك لما أدركنا جهة الصوت وحده من القرب والبعد لأن الواصل لا يكون إلا ما في الصماخ . والجواب ما سبق من أن المدرك الموقوف إدراكه على وصول الهواء ليس هو القائم بالهواء الواصل فقط كما في اللمس بل البعيد أيضاً كما في الإبصار. الثاني : أنا ندرك أن صوت المؤذن عند هبوب الرياح يميل عن جهتنا إلى خلافها .
And [the author] argued for the invalidity of the dependence of hearing on the arrival of the carrying air with aspects: First: that if it were so, we would not perceive the direction of the sound alone, [nor] nearness and distance, because what arrives is only what is in the ear canal. And the answer is what preceded: that the perceived whose perception depends on the arrival of air is not only what subsists in the arriving air, as in touch, but also the distant, as in vision. Second: that we perceive that the sound of the mu'adhdhin, when the wind blows, inclines from our direction to its opposite.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage presents two counter-arguments (استدلال) against the philosophers' view that hearing requires the air carrying the sound to reach the ear. The first argument: if hearing depended solely on air arriving at the ear, we would not be able to perceive the direction of the sound or its distance (near or far), because what arrives is only the air in the ear canal itself, not the direction from which it came. The answer given is that the perceived object is not limited to the air that arrives, but includes the distant source, analogous to vision where we perceive distant objects even though the light or air reaching the eye is local. The second argument: we observe that when wind blows, the sound of a mu'adhdhin seems to incline away from our direction, which would not happen if sound were carried directly from the source to our ear along a straight path. This second argument is presented without an answer within this passage.

Key Terms
جهة الصوت
القرب والبعد
اللمس
الإبصار
المؤذن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer to the first argument rejects any role for air in hearing.
Correction: The answer refines the role: air is a medium, but perception includes the distant source, not only the air that reaches the ear.

Checkpoint

B) Perception of direction is not relevant to hearing.
C) What is perceived is not just the arriving air but also the distant source, as in vision.
D) The argument was already refuted earlier by the philosophers.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani uses analogy and empirical observation to argue that hearing does not require air to physically reach the ear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Taftazani uses an analogy between hearing and vision to answer the first argument. In your own life, when has an analogy helped you understand a difficult theological concept? Are you willing to let go of that analogy if it is shown to be imperfect, as the first argument's analogy is corrected?
?
The second argument relies on ordinary observation (wind affecting sound) to challenge a philosophical theory. Are there everyday experiences in your life that contradict assumptions you hold about Allah's attributes or actions? How can you train yourself to pay attention to such experiences instead of dismissing them?

Correlation Not Causation: Observations on Sound
Two observations on hearing and a reply on causation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why correlation (دوران) is insufficient to prove causation in kalam arguments about sound and hearing.

Core Idea
Correlation is not causation in kalam.

Al-Taftazani presents two empirical observations that seem to show hearing depends on air reaching the ear. But he then offers a crucial methodological response: these observations only show correlation, not causation. This lesson explores why such observations are insufficient for certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two observations
The passage presents two observations: the tube experiment and the sound delay. Find each one and notice how Al-Taftazani describes them.
3
Understand the response
The reply states that these observations only show correlation (دوران), not causation. Ask yourself why correlation is not enough.
4
Reflect on the methodological point
Consider why this point is important in kalam: it prevents drawing certain conclusions from regular patterns in nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن من وضع طرف أنبوبة في فمه وطرفها الآخر في صماخ إنسان، وتكلم منها بصوت عال سمعه ذلك الإنسان دون غيره من الحاضرين وما ذلك إلا بمنع الأنبوبة وصول الهواء الحامل للصوت إلى أصمختهم . الثالث: أنا نرى سبب الصوت كضرب الفأس على الخشبة مثلاً، ويتأخر سماع الصوت عنه زماناً يتفاوت بحسب تفاوت المسافة قرباً وبعداً، فلو لا أن السماع يتوقف على وصول الهواء لما كان كذلك . وأجيب عن الكل بأن غايتها الدوران وهو لا يفيد القطع بالسببية، فيجوز أن يكون ميل الصوت مع الرياح واختصاص صاحب الأنبوبة بالسماع وتأخر السماع عن ضرب الفأس بسبب آخر فلا يدل توقف السماع على وصل هواء حامل للصوت.
The second: that a person who places one end of a tube in his mouth and its other end in the ear canal of a person, and speaks from it with a loud voice, that person hears it without others present, and that is only because the tube prevents the air carrying the sound from reaching their ear canals. The third: that we see the cause of sound like the striking of an axe on wood, for example, and the hearing of the sound is delayed from it by a time that varies according to the difference in distance, near and far. So if hearing did not depend on the arrival of air, it would not be so. And it was answered concerning all of them that their outcome is correlation, and it does not yield certainty of causation, so it is possible that the inclination of sound with the winds, the exclusivity of the tube owner's hearing, and the delay of hearing from the axe strike are due to another cause. So the dependence of hearing on the arrival of carrying air is not indicated.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage presents two empirical observations that seem to show hearing depends on air reaching the ear, and then gives a general response. The first observation (labeled 'second' because a first was given earlier) is a tube experiment: a person speaks into a tube placed in someone's ear, and only that person hears, because the tube confines the air to that ear. The second observation (labeled 'third') is the delay of sound with distance: we see an axe strike and hear the sound later, with the delay proportional to distance, suggesting the sound must travel through air. The response to all such observations is that they show correlation (دوران), not causation. It is possible that the sound's inclination with wind, the exclusivity of the tube's hearing, and the delay are due to other causes. Therefore, these observations do not prove that hearing depends on the arrival of air carrying the sound. The author emphasizes that correlation does not give certainty of causation, a crucial methodological point in kalam.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Observing that two events regularly occur together (like sound and air) is enough to prove a causal relationship.
Correction: The passage argues that correlation (دوران) does not yield certainty of causation; other factors may be responsible.

Checkpoint

D) That the tube observation proves hearing requires air, but the delay does not.

Key Takeaway
Correlation between sound and air does not prove that hearing depends on air; other causes are possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage teaches that sound delay could be due to another cause besides air travel. Consider an area where you have been impatient or angry because a desired outcome did not follow a usual cause. First Argument Against Air Dependence: Direction Perception
Al-Taftazani presents the first counter-argument: we perceive direction and distance of sound, which would not happen if only the air reaching the ear carried sound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against the theory that hearing requires air to reach the ear, and how the response defends the theory by analogy with vision.

Core Idea
We perceive distance and direction of sound — a fact that challenges purely local air transmission.

Al-Taftazani introduces a strong objection to the air-dependence theory: we can tell where a sound comes from and how far away it is. If only the air that actually touches the ear carried sound, this would be impossible. This lesson walks through the argument and its rebuttal, showing how the theory can be salvaged by comparing hearing to vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first a remark about signs, then the argument, then the answer.
2
Identify the core objection
The objection states that if hearing depended only on air reaching the ear, we would not perceive direction or distance. Why? Because only the air in the ear canal arrives. Find this in the passage.
The phrase 'جهة الصوت' (direction of sound) and 'قرب وبعد' (nearness and distance) are key.
3
Understand the response
The response says that the perceived object when hearing is not just the air that arrives, but also the distant source. This is similar to vision: we see distant objects via the medium. Reflect on this analogy.
Think about how we see a tree: the light from the tree reaches our eyes, but we perceive the tree itself, not just the light.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحق أن هذه أمارات ربما تفيد اليقين الحدسي للناظر وإن لم يقم حجة على المناظر واستدل على بطلان توقف السماع على وصول الهواء الحامل بوجوه : الأول : أنه لو كان كذلك لما أدركنا جهة الصوت وحده من القرب والبعد لأن الواصل لا يكون إلا ما في الصماخ . والجواب ما سبق من أن المدرك الموقوف إدراكه على وصول الهواء ليس هو القائم بالهواء الواصل فقط كما في اللمس بل البعيد أيضاً كما في الإبصار.
And the truth is that these are signs that may yield intuitive certainty for the viewer, even if they do not constitute a proof against the disputant. And he argued for the invalidity of the dependence of hearing on the arrival of the carrying air with arguments: The first: that if it were so, we would not perceive the direction of sound alone, nor nearness or distance, because what arrives is only what is in the ear canal. And the answer is what preceded: that the perceived, whose perception depends on the arrival of air, is not only that which subsists in the arriving air as in touch, but also the distant as in vision.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage begins with a remark that the earlier observations, though not decisive proofs, may give intuitive certainty to the observer. Then it presents the first of three arguments against the dependence of hearing on air reaching the ear. The argument is: if hearing depended solely on air arriving at the ear, then we would not be able to perceive the direction, distance, or location of a sound, because the only air that reaches the ear is that which enters the ear canal. Yet we do perceive these aspects. The answer, referencing an earlier point, is that the object perceived when hearing depends on air is not only the air that arrives (as in touch), but also the distant source (as in vision). So the perception of direction and distance is not incompatible with air transmission, because we perceive the distant source via the medium.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument disproves the air transmission theory for hearing.
Correction: The author shows that perception of distance and direction is compatible with air transmission, because we perceive the distant source via the medium, similar to vision. The argument is answered, not conclusive.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against hearing depending on air reaching the ear?
A) That sound cannot travel through air.
B) That we perceive direction and distance of sound, which would be impossible if only air in the ear canal carried sound.
C) That the ear canal is too narrow for sound to enter.
D) That air does not carry sound at all.

Key Takeaway
The first argument against air dependence fails because perception of distance and direction is compatible with air as a medium, similar to vision; the perceived object includes the distant source via the medium.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been absolutely certain about the direction of a sound, only to realize you were mistaken? What does this tell you about the reliability of your senses, and how does it urge you to seek knowledge beyond mere perception?
?
The passage compares hearing to vision: we perceive distant objects via a medium. In your daily life, do you often forget that your knowledge of the world is mediated? How can this awareness change the way you seek certainty—especially in matters of faith?
?
If the ability to hear direction and distance is taken as proof against a theory, yet you know from experience that hearing can be fooled, does that make you doubt the argument? Reflect on how you balance empirical observation with logical reasoning in your own quest for truth. Will you commit to examining your assumptions more carefully after this lesson?

The Second Counter-Argument: Sound and Wind
Al-Taftazani presents the second argument and its rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the objection that wind causes sound to lean away, thereby defending the necessity of air transmission for hearing.

Core Idea
Sound deflection by wind confirms air dependence through disturbed hearing.

This passage continues the debate on whether hearing requires air reaching the ear. The opponent presents a second observation: when the wind blows, the sound of the mu'adhdhin seems to lean away from us. Al-Taftazani responds by explaining that such deviation only occurs when air can still reach us, albeit imperfectly, resulting in unclear hearing. This reinforces the causal link between air and sound transmission.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise style of the objection and answer.
The passage is only two sentences long but contains a complete argument.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
The objection states that the sound of the mu'adhdhin leans away from us when the wind blows, suggesting sound is not carried by air. The answer explains that this occurs when air can still reach us, though not in the normal manner.
Focus on how the answer does not deny the phenomenon but reinterprets it.
3
Reflect on the causal connection
Consider why the answer preserves the necessity of air. The presence of disturbed hearing (تشويش السماع) actually confirms that air transmission is occurring, just imperfectly.
This reinforces the principle that sound depends on air reaching the ear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنا ندرك أن صوت المؤذن عند هبوب الرياح يميل عن جهتنا إلى خلافها . والجواب : أن ذلك إنما يكون عند إمكان الوصول في الجملة وإن لم يكن على وجهه ولذا لا يخلو عن تشويش السماع .
The second: that we perceive that the sound of the mu'adhdhin, when the wind blows, inclines away from our direction to its opposite. And the answer: that this only occurs when arrival is possible in general, even if not in its proper manner, and therefore it is not free from disturbance of hearing.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the dependence of hearing on air reaching the ear: when the wind blows, we hear the sound of the mu'adhdhin (the caller to prayer) as if it is leaning away from us, i.e., the direction seems different. This suggests that the sound is not simply traveling in a straight line with the air. The answer is that this phenomenon occurs when the air can still reach us in some way, even if not in the normal direct manner. Consequently, the hearing is disturbed or unclear, which aligns with the idea that air transmission is still involved but imperfectly. So the objection does not disprove the dependence; it only shows that wind affects the sound's path.

The deflection of sound by wind does not negate air dependence; it illustrates disturbed transmission of air.

Sound Through a Solid Wall: Refuting the Third Argument
Al-Taftazani refutes the third argument against air dependence with a detailed logical and physical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why hearing through a solid wall does not disprove air transmission of sound, and how al-Taftazani explains sound persistence and echo.

Core Idea
Sound through a wall still involves air; persistence of sound quality is improbable but not impossible.

Al-Taftazani now addresses a seemingly powerful objection: we hear someone speaking through a solid wall even though air cannot penetrate. If sound depends on air reaching the ear, how can this happen? His response is a masterclass in nuanced argumentation, distinguishing between physical shape and quality, and introducing the even more puzzling phenomenon of echo.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and initial answer
Read the Arabic passage from 'الثالث' to 'لا نسلم السماع'. Notice the objection and the immediate counter: if the wall has no pores, hearing does not occur.
The author uses the example of a mark on water to show the fragility of air shape.
2
Examine the explanation of shape persistence
Read from 'ألا ترى' to 'في بقائه مع النفوذ والمضايق'. Al-Taftazani distinguishes between the 'shape' (configuration) of air and the sound quality itself. Understand why each case leads to a different conclusion.
3
Grasp the truth statement
Read from 'والحق أن قيام' to 'مستبعد جداً'. The author states that sound quality subsists in all parts of air, but its persistence during penetration is extremely improbable.
This is not an outright denial but a nuanced stance.
4
Understand the echo discussion
Read from 'وأبعد منه' to 'الأشبه'. Echo is presented as an even more improbable case. Two theories are mentioned; the author favors the second.
Compare with the ball analogy to visualize air returning.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Read the final sentence. Al-Taftazani concludes that the whole process is highly improbable, almost impossible. This shows his careful reasoning without dismissing empirical evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنا نسمع صوت من يحول بيننا وبينه جدار صلب مع القطع بامتناع نفوذ الهواء في المنافذ من غير أن يزول عنه ذلك الشكل الذي هو أضعف وأسرع زوالاً من الرقم على الماء، وقد صار مثلاً في عدم البقاء. وأجيب بأنه إذا لم يكن للحائل منافذ أصلاً، ولا يكون هناك طريق آخر للهواء فلا نسلم السماع ألا ترى أنه كلما كانت المنافذ أقل كان السماع أضعف، وأما بقاء الشكل فإن أريد به حقيقة التشكل الذي يعرض للهواء فيصير سبباً لحدوث الكيفية المخصوصة، فلا حاجة لنا إلى بقائه لأنه من المعدات، وإن أريد به تلك الكيفية المسببة عنه المسماة بالصوت والحرف فلا إستحالة بل لا استعداد في بقائه مع النفوذ والمضايق، والحق أن قيام تلك الكيفية المخصوصة الغير قارة لكل جزء من أجزاء الهواء، بدليل أن الكل من في تلك المسافة يسمعها، وبقاء أجزاء الهواء مع فرط لطافتها على تلك الهيئة والكيفية مع هبوب الرياح ومع النفوذ في منافذ الأجسام الصلبة مستبعد جداً، وأبعد منه حديث الصدى وهو أن الهواء إذا تموج وقاومه أملس كجبل أو جدار بحيث يرد ذلك المتموج إلى خلف على هيئته كما في الكرة المرمية إلى الحائط المقاوم لها حدث من ذلك صوت هو الصدى، وترددوا في أن حدوثه من تموج الهواء الأول الراجع على هيئته، أو من تموج هواء آخر بيننا وبين المقاوم متكيف بكيفية الهواء الراجع، وهذا هو الأشبه . وكيف ما كان فبقاء الهواء على كيفيته التي لا استقرار لها مع مصادمة الجسم الصلب، ثم رجوعه على هيئة وإحداثه وكيفيته فيما يجاوره، وزواله بمجرد الوصول إلى الصماخ من المستبعدات التي تكاد تلحق بالمحالات.
The third: that we hear the sound of someone who is separated from us by a solid wall, while being certain of the impossibility of air penetrating the pores, without that shape being removed from it, which is weaker and more quickly vanishing than a mark on water, and it has become a proverb for non-endurance. And it was answered that if the barrier has no pores at all, and there is no other path for the air, then we do not concede hearing. Do you not see that whenever the pores are fewer, the hearing is weaker? As for the persistence of the shape: if what is meant by it is the reality of the configuration that occurs in the air, making it a cause for the occurrence of the specific quality, then we have no need for its persistence because it is among the preparatory causes. And if what is meant by it is that specific quality caused by it, called sound and letter, then there is no impossibility, but rather there is no readiness for its persistence during penetration and narrow passages. And the truth is that the subsistence of that specific non-permanent quality in every part of the air parts—as evidence that everyone in that distance hears it—and the persistence of the air parts, despite their extreme subtlety, in that state and quality, during wind blowing, and during penetration into the pores of solid bodies, is extremely improbable. And more improbable than it is the report of echo: that when air undulates and strikes a smooth surface like a mountain or wall, such that that undulating [air] returns backward in its form, as in a ball thrown against an opposing wall, a sound occurs from that, which is echo. And they disputed whether its occurrence is from the undulation of the first air returning in its form, or from the undulation of another air between us and the opposing surface, configured with the configuration of the returning air, and this is more likely. However it may be, the persistence of air in its quality, which has no stability, when colliding with a solid body, then its return in a form, its production of a quality in what is adjacent to it, and its disappearance as soon as it reaches the ear canal are among the improbable things that almost approach impossibilities.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the dependence of hearing on air reaching the ear: we hear sound through a solid wall, even though air cannot penetrate it, and the sound's shape (i.e., its form) persists, which is supposedly impossible if air is the carrier. The answer is detailed. First, if the wall has no pores at all and no other path for air, then hearing does not occur; in fact, the fewer the pores, the weaker the sound. So sound through a wall does involve some air passage. Second, regarding the persistence of the shape: if by 'shape' is meant the configuration of air that causes the sound, then its persistence is not necessary because it is only a preparatory cause. If by 'shape' is meant the sound quality itself, then there is no impossibility in its persistence during penetration, though it is not ready to persist in narrow passages. The author (al-Taftazani) then states the truth: that the specific non-permanent quality (sound) subsists in every part of the air, as shown by the fact that all in that distance hear it. But the persistence of air parts in that quality during wind and penetration into solid bodies is extremely improbable. Even more improbable is the phenomenon of echo: air undulates, strikes a smooth surface, and returns, producing a second sound. Scholars differed on whether echo is from the returning air or from new air configured by the returning air. The author finds the latter more likely. In any case, the persistence of air's quality, its return, and its disappearance upon reaching the ear are all highly improbable, almost impossible. This discussion highlights the complexity of sound transmission and the difficulties in explaining it purely by air reaching the ear.

شكل
كيفية مخصوصة
صوت
حرف
صدى
تموج

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hearing through a solid wall disproves that sound travels via air.
Correction: The sound still relies on air passing through pores or another path; if no pores exist, hearing does not occur.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, what is his primary response to the argument that we hear sound through a solid wall?
A) That sound does not require air at all.
B) That if the wall has no pores, hearing does not occur, and pores are necessary.
C) That the sound shape persists indefinitely.
D) That echo proves sound travels without air.

Key Takeaway
Sound through a solid wall does not refute air transmission; it only shows that air can pass through pores, and the persistence of sound quality is highly improbable but not impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear sound through a wall, do you instinctively assume it reached you without any medium? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to trust your immediate perception over reasoned analysis, and what does that reveal about your intellectual humility?
?
Al-Taftazani distinguishes between something that is a preparatory cause (a means) and the actual quality itself. In your daily life, have you ever demanded that a means persist indefinitely when it was only meant to facilitate? What would it take for you to stop treating means as ends?
?
The survival of sound quality through wind and solid bodies is called 'extremely improbable, almost impossible.' Yet it occurs by Allah's constant power. When is the last time you reflected on the sheer improbability of a simple act like hearing? If it has been a while, what does that tell you about your awareness of Allah's continuous creation?

Life as a Distinct Principle: Proof, Objection, and Answer
al-Sanusi presents proof, objection, and answer on the distinction of life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that life is distinct from the powers of sensation and nutrition, and how al-Razi's objection is answered using the concept of actual efficacy.

Core Idea
Life is a distinct principle from the powers it activates, not identical to them.

This passage presents a classic theological argument for the distinction between life and its associated faculties. The author (al-Sanusi) provides a proof, followed by a sharp objection from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi and a precise answer. Engaging with this argument will sharpen your understanding of causality and the nature of life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the overall flow: proof, objection, answer.
Pay attention to the key terms: مغايرة, قوة الحس والحركة, قوة التغذية.
2
Identify the main claim and proof
The author claims life is distinct from sensation and nutrition. Find the evidence: life exists in paralyzed limbs (no sensation) and withered limbs (no nutrition).
The proof relies on the presence of life without the effects.
3
Understand al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi argues that absence of effect does not prove absence of power; there could be an obstacle (مانع) on the part of the recipient.
A common misunderstanding: the objection seems to refute the proof if unanswered.
4
Analyze the answer
The answer redefines 'power' as that which actually produces its effect (بالفعل). The power that produces life's effects remains, while the powers of sensation/nutrition are absent, so they are distinct.
This shows the importance of precise definitions in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا حصل في المركب اعتدال يليق بنوع من أنواع الحيوان فاض عليه قوة الحياة فانبعثت عنها بإذن الله تعالى الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة والقوى المحركة نحو جلب المنافع ودفع المضار فتكون الحياة مشروطة باعتدال المزاج، ومبدأ القوة الحس والحركة فتغاير هما بالضرورة وكذا تغاير القوة الغاذية لوجودها في النبات بخلاف الحياة لكن هذا إنما يتم لو ثبت أن الحياة مبدأ لقوة الحس والحركة لا نفسها وأن الغاذية في النبات والحيوان حقيقة واحدة ليلزم من مغايرة تلك الحياة مغايرة هذه لها فاستدلوا على مغايرة الحياة لقوة الحس والحركة ولقوة التغذية الحيوانية بأن الحياة موجودة في العضو المفلوج للحيوان من غير حس وحركة ، وفي العضو الذابل من غير اغتذاء . واعترض الإمام بأن عدم الإحساس والحركة وعدم الاغتذاء لا يدلان على عدم قوة الحس والحركة وعدم قوة التغذية لجواز أن توجد القوة ولا يصدر عنها الأثر لمانع من جهة القابل . وأجيب بأن القوة ما يصدر عنه الأثر بالفعل بمعنى أنا نريد أن القوة التي تصدر عنها بالفعل آثار الحياة كحفظ العضو عن التعفن مثلاً ،باقية، والقوة التي يصدر عنها بالفعل الحس والحركة والتغذية غير باقية فلا تكون هي هي بهذا يشعر كلام تلخيص المحصل وليس معناه أن القوة اسم لما يصدر عنه الأثر
When a temperament suitable for a species of animals occurs in the compound, the power of life emanates upon it, and from it, by the permission of God Almighty, the external and internal senses and the motive powers for attracting benefits and repelling harms are activated. Thus life is conditioned by balanced temperament and is a principle of the power of sensation and movement. So it necessarily differs from both, and likewise it differs from the nutritive power because it exists in plants unlike life. But this is only complete if it is established that life is a principle of the power of sensation and movement, not identical to it, and that the nutritive power in plants and animals is one and the same reality, so that from the difference of that life there follows the difference of this (nutritive power) from it. So they adduced as evidence for the difference of life from the power of sensation and movement and from the animal nutritive power that life is present in the paralyzed limb of an animal without sensation and movement, and in the withered limb without nutrition. Imam (al-Razi) objected that the absence of sensation and movement and the absence of nutrition do not prove the absence of the power of sensation and movement and the nutritive power, because it is possible that the power exists but its effect does not occur due to an obstacle on the part of the recipient. It was answered that power is that from which the effect actually proceeds, meaning that we intend that the power that actually produces the effects of life, such as preserving the limb from decay, remains, while the power that actually produces sensation, movement, and nutrition is not remaining. So they are not identical. This is indicated by the statement of Talkhis al-Muhassal, and it does not mean that power is a name for that from which the effect proceeds.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (istidlal) that life is distinct from the powers of sensation and nutrition, followed by an objection (i'tirad) and an answer (jawab). It begins by describing the process: when balanced temperament occurs in a compound, the power of life emanates, and by God's permission, the senses and motive powers are activated. Thus life is conditioned by balanced temperament and is a principle of the power of sensation and movement, so it is necessarily distinct from them. It is also distinct from the nutritive power because nutrition exists in plants without life. However, the proof is only valid if it is established that life is a principle, not the same as the power it produces, and that the nutritive power in plants and animals is the same reality. The evidence adduced is that life is present in a paralyzed limb (عضو مفلوج) without sensation or movement, and in a withered limb (عضو ذابل) without nutrition. Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi objects that the absence of effect does not prove the absence of the power, because the power may exist but its effect is prevented by an obstacle (مانع) on the part of the recipient. The answer clarifies that by 'power' here we mean that which actually produces its effect (بالفعل). The power that actually produces life's effects (like preserving the limb from decay) remains, while the power that actually produces sensation, movement, and nutrition is absent. Therefore, they are not identical. The answer is supported by the text of Talkhis al-Muhassal, and it emphasizes that 'power' is not merely a name for that which produces an effect (i.e., it is not a tautology).

قوة الحس والحركة
قوة التغذية
العضو المفلوج
العضو الذابل
مانع
بالفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection by al-Razi successfully disproves the proof that life is distinct from the powers of sensation and nutrition.
Correction: The answer clarifies that by 'power' we mean actual efficacy; thus the absence of sensation and nutrition in living limbs shows that the powers are distinct, because the power producing life's effects remains while the others are absent.

Checkpoint

A) The proof assumes the existence of life

C) The witness of the paralyzed limb is invalid

Key Takeaway
Life is a distinct principle from the powers of sensation and nutrition; their absence in living limbs proves they are not identical, even if a power can be impeded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you measure your worth by your abilities—your ability to move, to feel, to produce? This lesson distinguishes life itself from the powers it activates. In a paralyzed limb, life remains even when sensation and movement are gone. If you were to lose your most treasured ability tomorrow, would you still know that your life—given directly by God—has not diminished one bit? Or are you living as if your abilities define your existence?
?
When you face a failure or loss of function—whether physical, mental, or spiritual—do you conclude that your 'life' (your very being) is somehow less? The proof in this lesson shows that life remains as a distinct principle even when its powers are absent. Where in your life are you confusing the loss of a power with the loss of life itself? What concrete step can you take today to honor the life God has given you, independent of what you can accomplish?
?
The objection of al-Razi challenges the proof by saying that a power might exist even if its effect is blocked. But the answer clarifies that the power in question is the actual source of the effect. This means that in God's sight, what matters is the reality of what is actually present, not just potential. Are you harboring any spiritual 'potential' that never produces fruit—claiming faith but without actual obedience? What effect of faith is absent from your life that you have been excusing with 'obstacles'? This lesson calls you to examine whether the power of iman is truly present in its effects, not just in name.

Correlation vs. Necessity: The Ash‘ari Response to Empirical Objections
Al-Taftazani presents the philosophers’ and Mu‘tazila’s empirical objection and the Ash‘ari response of correlation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash‘aris reject the claim that life is necessarily dependent on bodily structure, temperament, and spirit, based on the principle that correlation does not imply metaphysical necessity.

Core Idea
Observation shows correlation, not metaphysical necessity.

The philosophers and many Mu‘tazila argue from empirical observation that life is conditional on the body’s structure, temperament, and spirit. Al-Taftazani presents their objection and then provides the Ash‘ari response: what we observe is mere constant conjunction, not a necessary condition. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage focusing on the philosophers’ empirical evidence. Note the specific bodily factors they list.
They mention structure, parts, temperament, and spirit circulation.
2
Identify the Ash‘ari response
Read the response: the observation only shows correlation (dawaran), which does not prove conditionality.
The key term is dawaran.
3
Understand the principle
Reflect on why this distinction matters. Correlation alone cannot establish metaphysical necessity because God can sustain life without these factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب الفلاسفة وكثير من المعتزلة إلى أن هذا الاشتراط بناء على ما يشاهد من زوال الحياة بانتقاض البنية وتفرق الأجزاء، وبانحراف المزاج عن الاعتدال النوعي وبعدم سريان الروح في العضو لسدة أو شدة ربط يمنع نفوذه، ورد بأن غايته الدوران وهو لا يقتضي الاشتراط بحيث يمتنع بدون تلك الأمور.
And the philosophers and many of the Mu'tazila held that this conditionality is based on what is observed of the cessation of life with the disruption of the structure, the dispersion of parts, the deviation of temperament from its proper balance, and the lack of circulation of the spirit in a limb due to an obstruction or a severe binding that prevents its penetration. And the response is that its ultimate basis is correlation (dawaran), and correlation does not imply conditionality such that [life] is impossible without those things.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of the philosophers (falasifa) and many Mu'tazila, who argue that life is conditional on balanced temperament, bodily structure, and animal spirit. Their evidence is empirical observation (mushāhada): life ceases when the bodily structure is disrupted, parts disperse, temperament deviates from its proper balance, or the spirit cannot circulate in a limb due to a blockage or tight binding. The author then gives the Ash'ari response: the basis of their argument is mere correlation (dawaran), i.e., a constant conjunction between life and these bodily factors. But correlation does not prove that these factors are necessary conditions (shart) such that life is impossible without them. This is a key principle in kalam: observable regularity does not imply metaphysical necessity; God may sustain life without these conditions.

Key Terms
دوران
الفلاسفة
المعتزلة
البنية
المزاج
الروح الحيواني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That empirical correlation constitutes proof of causal dependence.
Correction: The Ash'aris distinguish between correlation and metaphysical necessity; correlation alone does not mean life cannot exist without those bodily factors.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the philosophers' and Mu'tazila's claim that life is conditional on bodily factors?
A) Rational proof that life cannot exist without a body.
B) Observed correlation between life and bodily factors.
C) Scriptural texts indicating the necessity of the body.
D) Consensus of the early Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Observed correlation between life and bodily factors does not prove that those factors are necessary conditions for life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life have you assumed that 'this always leads to that' without considering that God could bring about a different outcome? Will you consciously surrender that assumption to His unbound power today?
?
The philosophers based their argument on what they saw—but their conclusion went beyond the evidence. When have you made a similar leap from 'this is how it usually is' to 'this must always be' in your own beliefs or actions? What would it cost you to admit that God's will is not tied to your patterns?
?
You rely on regularities every day: food nourishes, sleep restores, medicine heals. How would your trust in God deepen if you truly believed that these are not necessities but habits of His mercy? What one practical step can you take this week to depend more directly on Him rather than the means?

The Correlation Argument: Life and Bodily Structure
Taftazani presents and refutes the empirical argument for life's dependence on body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why empirical correlation does not prove necessary conditioning of life by bodily structure in kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
Correlation is not causation—especially not in kalam.

This lesson introduces the initial claim that life is conditioned by the body based on observation, and the critical response that this only shows correlation, not necessity. You'll see how kalam thinkers distinguish between what is always observed together and what is necessarily dependent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and its translation to see the claim and the reply. Note the three empirical observations cited by the philosophers.
The key term 'dawrān' (correlation) is central.
2
Identify the empirical evidence
List the three observations: dissolution of structure, deviation of temperament, and blocked spirit flow. Ask: Does this prove causation?
3
Understand the reply
The reply states that these observations only show correlation (dawrān), not that life is impossible without them. Reflect on why correlation is insufficient in kalam.
This connects to the broader principle that necessity requires rational proof, not just observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب الفلاسفة وكثير من المعتزلة إلى أن هذا الاشتراط بناء على ما يشاهد من زوال الحياة بانتقاض البنية وتفرق الأجزاء، وبانحراف المزاج عن الاعتدال النوعي وبعدم سريان الروح في العضو لسدة أو شدة ربط يمنع نفوذه، ورد بأن غايته الدوران وهو لا يقتضي الاشتراط بحيث يمتنع بدون تلك الأمور.
واستدل بعض المتكلمين على امتناع كون الحياة مشروطة بالبنية بأنها لو اشترطت فإما أن تقوم بالجزئين من البنية حياة واحدة فيلزم قيام العرض بأكثر من محل واحد وقد مر بطلانه، وإما أن يقوم بكل جزء حياة، وحينئذ إما أن يكون القيام بكل جزء مشروطاً بالقيام بالآخر فيلزم الدور أو لا فيلزم الرجحان بلا مرجح لتماثل الأجزاء واتحاد حقيقة الحياة. لا يقال لا يجوز أن يقوم بالبعض فقط لأسباب مرجحة من الخارج. لأنا نقول : فيكون الحي ذلك البعض لا البنية المؤلفة .
وأجيب بأنها تقوم بالمجموع الذي هو البنية المؤلفة، وليس هذا من قيام العرض بمحلين على ما سبق، أو يقوم بكل جزء حياة، ويكون اشتراط كل بالآخر بطريق المعية دون التقدم فلا يلزم الدور المحال، أو يكون قيامها ببعض الأجزاء مشروطاً بقيام حياة بالآخر من غير عكس المرجح يوجد في الخارج، وإن لم يطلع عليه .
لا يقال : فحينئذ تكون الحياة غير مشروطة بالبنية حيث تحققت في الجزء الآخر من غير شرط لأنا نقول : عدم اشتراط قيام الحياة به بقيام حياة بالجزء الأول لا يستلزم عدم اشتراطه بوجود الجزء الأول الذي به تتحقق البنية.
And the philosophers and many of the Mu'tazila held that this condition [is] based on what is observed of the cessation of life with the dissolution of the bodily structure and the separation of parts, and with the deviation of temperament from specific equilibrium, and with the absence of the spirit's flow in the limb due to an obstruction or a strong binding that prevents its penetration. And it was replied that its utmost is correlation, and correlation does not necessitate condition such that it is impossible without those things. And some of the mutakallimun argued for the impossibility of life being conditioned by the bodily structure: that if it were conditioned, then either one life would inhere in two parts of the structure, necessitating the inherence of an accident in more than one locus, which has already been shown false; or a life would inhere in each part, and then either the inherence in each part is conditioned by the inherence in the other, necessitating circularity, or not, necessitating preponderance without a preponderant due to the similarity of parts and the unity of the reality of life. It may not be said: it is not permissible that it inhere in some parts only due to preponderant causes from outside. For we say: then that part would be the living thing, not the composite structure. And it was answered that it inheres in the aggregate which is the composite structure, and this is not of the type of inherence of an accident in two loci as mentioned; or a life inheres in each part, and the condition of each by the other is by way of concomitance (al-mu‘iyya) without priority, so that impossible circularity does not follow; or its inherence in some parts is conditioned by the inherence of a life in the other without the inverse, [and] the preponderant exists externally, even if not known. It may not be said: then life is unconditioned by the bodily structure since it is realized in the other part without condition. For we say: the absence of condition of the inherence of life in it by the inherence of life in the first part does not entail the absence of its condition by the existence of the first part through which the bodily structure is realized.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about whether life (al-hayat) is necessarily conditioned by bodily structure (al-buniya). The author presents the view of philosophers and many Mu'tazila who assert that life is conditional on the body based on empirical observation: life ends when the body dissolves, temperament deviates, or spirit flow is blocked. The author replies that this only shows correlation (dawrān), not necessary condition. Then some mutakallimun give a proof that life cannot be conditioned by structure: if it were, then either one life would inhere in two parts (impossible because an accident cannot inhere in more than one locus), or each part would have a life. If each part has a life, then either the life in each part is conditioned by the life in the other (leading to circularity) or not (leading to preponderance without a preponderant, since parts are similar). An objection is raised: could not life inhere only in some parts due to external preponderant factors? The reply: then that part would be the living thing, not the whole structure. Then the author presents possible answers: life inheres in the composite whole (not as an accident in two loci), or each part has a life with mutual conditioning by way of concomitance (mu‘iyya) without temporal priority, avoiding circularity, or life in some parts is conditioned by life in others but not vice versa, with an external preponderant. A final objection: if life is realized in the other part without condition, then life is not conditioned by structure. The reply: the absence of condition in one part does not negate condition by the existence of the other part that constitutes the structure. The argument carefully defends the contingency of life on the body while rejecting necessary conditioning.

Key Terms
الحياة
البنية
الدوران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Constant conjunction (correlation) necessarily implies causal conditioning.
Correction: In kalam, correlation (dawrān) is not sufficient to prove that one thing cannot exist without another. A rational proof is required to establish necessary condition.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author rejects the philosophers' argument?
A) The observations are not reliable.
B) Correlation does not necessitate necessary condition.
C) The body is not the subject of life.
D) Life is a substance, not an accident.

Key Takeaway
Empirical correlation alone cannot establish necessary dependence; kalam requires a rational argument for such conditioning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something always happens in a certain way, it must be caused by that factor? What in your life have you taken as necessary that may only be correlated? How would your trust in Allah be affected if you truly internalized that nothing in creation necessitates anything except by His will?
?
If the mutakallimun reject empirical correlation as proof of necessary dependence, then what does that mean for how you view your health and safety? Are you tempted to believe that your body's health is the direct cause of your life, rather than a sign of Allah's direct preservation?
?
Consider the philosophers' argument: life ceases when the body is disrupted. The mutakallimun agree on the correlation but deny necessity. What does that say about your own assumptions regarding the 'causal' relationship between your actions and their outcomes? Do you live as if your efforts necessitate results, or as if Allah is the only necessary cause?

The Mutakallimun's Proof: Life Cannot Be Conditioned by Structure
The mutakallimun present a reduction ad absurdum of life being conditioned by bodily structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that the mutakallimun use to prove life cannot be conditioned by bodily structure.

Core Idea
If life were conditioned by structure, it would either be impossible (accident in two loci) or lead to circularity.

This lesson presents the core proof of the mutakallimun. They argue that if life were conditioned by bodily structure, it would have to inhere either as one life in two parts (impossible for an accident) or as multiple lives, each in a part, leading to unacceptable consequences. This argument shows why theological reasoning must be precise about inherence and conditionality.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the Arabic and translation of the mutakallimun's argument. Notice the two options they present.
The proof is structured as a dilemma.
2
Analyze the first option
One life in two parts would mean an accident in more than one locus, which is impossible in kalam. Understand the principle that accidents inhere in a single subject.
3
Analyze the second option
Multiple lives, each in a part, leads to either circularity (if each conditions the other) or preponderance without a preponderant (if not). Consider why these are problematic.
The objection about some parts only is also addressed: then only that part is living, not the whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A) Life would be a substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just reasoned that life is not conditioned by your body's structure. If your life is not a product of your physical organs, then what is keeping you alive at this very moment? Does this truth make you feel more dependent on the One who directly grants life, or do you still live as if your existence is self-sufficient? What will you change today to acknowledge that He is the sole Giver of life?
?
The mutakallimun argued that if life were tied to structure, it would lead to absurdities. Now look at your own fears: do you fear the decay of your body because you think your life will end with it? How does this argument challenge that fear? Can you genuinely say you believe your soul survives death? If not, what prevents you from living with that certainty?
?
The objection that life might inhere in some parts due to external factors was refuted: only those parts would be alive, not the whole. Consider your own self-worth: do you sometimes base it on only a few 'parts' of who you are—your appearance, skills, or achievements—rather than the whole person that God created? This lesson teaches that life is not a product of any part; so ask yourself: are you living as a whole person before God, or are you fragmented, relying on parts to define your worth? What step will you take to integrate your identity in the One who gives life to all parts?

Responses to the Mutakallimun's Proof: Possible Answers
Taftazani presents and discusses three responses that defend life's conditioning by bodily structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible answers that rescue life's conditioning by structure without logical absurdity.

Core Idea
Life can be conditioned by structure if it inheres in the composite whole, or by concomitance, or by external preponderance.

After the mutakallimun's proof, the author presents several answers that defend the possibility of life being conditioned by bodily structure. These answers explore different metaphysical models: inherence in the composite, conditioning by concomitance (mu'iyya), and conditioning by external preponderant. This lesson unpacks each answer and the final objection that ties them together.

Guided Path
1
Read the three answers
Read the Arabic and translation of the answers. Notice each one begins with 'أو' (or).
These are alternative solutions, not a single answer.
2
Understand the first answer
Life inheres in the composite whole as a single accident. This avoids the problem of two loci because the whole is one subject.
The author notes this is not like inherence in two loci.
3
Understand the second answer
Each part has a life, but each life is conditioned by the other via concomitance (mu'iyya), not priority, so no circularity.
Concomitance means simultaneous existence without temporal precedence.
4
Understand the third answer and final objection
Life inheres in some parts conditioned by life in others, with an external preponderant. The objection says this makes life unconditioned in one part. The reply clarifies that conditioning is by the existence of the other part, not by its life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimun's argument sought to sever the link between life and bodily structure, which could undermine belief in bodily resurrection. But Taftazani offered three responses to preserve that link. Right now, is your certainty in the resurrection based on rational arguments like these, or on something else? If the arguments were refuted, would your faith waver? What is the true foundation of your hope that God will recreate your body after death?
?
The third response admits that we do not fully know the preponderant (al-murajjih) that conditions life. Your own life is being sustained every moment by factors beyond your perception. Do you live with the humility that you do not fully understand the mechanism of your own existence? Or do you subconsciously believe that you are self-sufficient? Will you today acknowledge the Preponderant who alone sustains you, and thank Him for a life you cannot fully explain?
?
If life can be conditioned by structure in a way that is not circular (as the second response shows), then your body and soul are intimately linked without contradiction. Does this truth change how you treat your body? Do you see your physical health as part of your spiritual wellbeing, or do you dismiss it as merely material? Since your life depends on this structure, will you honor it as a trust from the One who created it?
